% ---------- 简介（无页码） ----------
\vspace*{4cm}

\begin{center}
    \rule[0.5ex]{5cm}{0.8pt}\\[6pt]
    {\Large\scshape Introduction}\\[6pt]
    \rule[0.5ex]{5cm}{0.8pt}
\end{center}

\vspace{2cm}

{\centering
\color{gray!80!black}
\begin{minipage}{0.8\textwidth}
    \setstretch{1.6}
    \itshape
    \raggedright
    \setlength{\parindent}{1em}   
Science Clubs × Everyday Mysteries: Volume 2
Come, step into the fields of youth.

Izuta and the other three members of the Biology Club have stumbled upon a bizarre investigation report dating back fifty years.
In the document written by a former club member, who looked into the strange phenomena of the *tengu*, the critical concluding section has vanished without a trace—
as if left behind for the underclassmen to pick up the research themselves.

To uncover the lost truth, the four head out on a field investigation during the long holiday.
They explore the deep woods, wander the shrine grounds at night, and examine the mummy in the treasure hall,
embarking on a three-day, two-night camp to unravel the legend.

“I’ve always wanted to do an investigation like this—with people my age who share the same interests.”

This is unquestionably the youth of science students.
But what they find in their pursuit of the truth is an unsettling secret that could tear their ordinary days apart—?
\end{minipage}
\par}

% ---------- 正文开始，恢复页码 ----------
\pagenumbering{arabic}          % 恢复阿拉伯数字页码

% ==============================
%        Prologue
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Prologue\\
    The Mysterious Document
\end{center}
\label{sec:prologueV2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Prologue}
\vspace{1cm}

Countless things in this world defied explanation, leaving me with no choice but to label them ``hard to make sense of.'' I harbored no belief in gods, ghosts, or yokai, yet if the devil challenged my certainty of their nonexistence, I would ultimately have to concede with a simple ``I don't really know.''

Even when I felt a semblance of understanding, I often hesitated to claim I truly grasped the truth.

I would likely take that same stance if asked to describe the kind of person Kannabi Rei was. It wasn't that I was grouping her with deities, apparitions, or monsters, of course. Despite spending a year and a half in the same club, I still felt unable to articulate her character. We had weathered our share of minor storms together in our own way, yet I remained unable to say with any confidence what true feelings lay hidden behind that thick veil of darkness.

If I could say one thing for certain, it was this: she was far more interesting than anyone would ever expect.

The summer of my second year was already entering its final stretch.

On a Monday morning, with the student council president election a mere two days away, I was walking toward class when a silent Kannabi intercepted me and dragged me to the corner of the third-floor hallway. I took in the situation at a single glance.

A single notice had been posted. It was a pale sheet of paper featuring glossy, oversized black Gothic lettering. A brazen piece of anonymous slander was pinned to the bulletin board with a lone thumbtack. It consisted of only three lines, but they were enough to make a cold sweat break out.

{\bfseries
\begin{enumerate}
\item The culprit behind last spring's ``Tengu Festival Incident'' was Hijiri Mitsuyuki.\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note:} Tengu (天狗) are famous beings from Japanese folklore, often depicted with a long nose (or sometimes a beak) and associated with mountains and shrines. Depending on the story they can be protective or dangerous---mischievous ``youkai'' who punish arrogance, test travelers/monks, and are linked to ascetic mountain practice (so it's common to see tengu statues, masks, and imagery at shrine sites connected to mountain worship).}
\item In this school year's first-term final exams, Hijiri Mitsuyuki cheated.
\item Hijiri Mitsuyuki is dating Kannabi Rei.
\end{enumerate}
}

I tore the paper down in a hurry. Hijiri Mitsuyuki was a friend of ours. A brilliant student ranked second in our year, he excelled in the Physics Club even while aiming for law school. He was also the most likely candidate to win this year's student council presidency. I was rooting for him as well.

If anything, I had been doing everything in my power until today to ensure Hijiri became president.

``It's probably pointless to take it down. It looks like there are plenty more posted,'' Kannabi noted flatly.

I couldn't read her face. I didn't know what I was supposed to say.

For the moment, I flipped open my phone, and as expected, multiple Signal messages had already arrived from Hijiri and Mikage. I glanced at the contents so Kannabi wouldn't see, then quickly closed the screen.

``I don't know what to say\dots\ are you all right?''

Her dark eyes seemed to waver slightly as they met mine, but she maintained her resolute composure nonetheless.

``About that third point---Delta, do you think it's true?''

``No\dots\ who knows. I have zero interest in other people's love lives.''

Since she was the one bringing it up, she was likely putting on a brave face. Kannabi let out a fearless little smile.

``I wonder who on earth would post something like this; even a prestigious school has its share of lowlifes.''

``Well, high schoolers are like that regardless of where you go,'' I replied, my mind racing desperately.

The goal of this anonymous slander was almost certainly to smear our friend and sabotage his election. All three points were rumors that had already been whispered about in the shadows.

We had to issue a flat denial of these claims in a way that everyone could understand before the vote took place.

The second point should be manageable. The day after tomorrow, right before voting, there'd be another all-school assembly. Hijiri had messaged me that he intended to prove his innocence regarding the cheating there.

The problem lay with the first point. We couldn't allow the truth about the ``Tengu Festival Incident'' to go public.

``Hey, you don't have to answer if you don't want to, but\dots''

There were students passing by. There wasn't much time until the morning short homeroom, but I led Kannabi toward the science building anyway. When we went down to the second floor, we found a lounge area in front of the gymnasium. At this hour, no one else was there.

It was the perfect spot for a quiet conversation.

``Honestly\dots\ what's the truth of the matter?''

In response to my question, Kannabi met my question with expressionless silence. \textit{I probably shouldn't have asked.}

A faint, medicinal scent reminiscent of adhesive bandages drifted through the awkward air. I knew that smell---moreover, I had just read about it. Apparently, while preparing for the school festival in this area yesterday evening, the Chemistry Club had an accident that sprayed phenolphthalein solution everywhere.

While I couldn't fathom why they would need such a vast amount of indicator, it was a stroke of luck that Mikage, who had arrived early to clean the mess, was the first to discover the slanderous notice posted outside the chemistry lab.

Though a fair number of people had already seen the anonymous smear and containment seemed difficult, Mikage had already located and collected copies from several other spots. 

That was the gist of the report.

Our initial response hadn't been bad. We had circumstantial evidence on our side. We could still turn the tide.

``Personally, I couldn't care less whether you two are dating, but if we're going to deny this rumor, we need to drag the credibility of these claims---and more importantly, the person who posted them---straight through the mud.''

``In other words, our actually dating would be an inconvenience.''

Her tone was unnecessarily sarcastic, yet as always, she had hit the nail right on the head. I let out a sigh.

``If you are, fine; but if we issue a denial and then a photo of you two kissing behind the school surfaces, it's our credibility that will take the hit next time, right?''

``Is Delta really the type to sneak behind the school for that sort of thing?''

``I'm exactly the type who doesn't do that sort of thing.''

``Don't worry. If I were going to kiss someone, I'd choose a place like this---somewhere it's hard for anyone to spy on us.''

Kannabi suddenly seized my tie and gave it a sharp yank, hauling me back toward the wall. I stepped forward to meet the pull so my tie clip wouldn't pop off, resulting in me pinning the shorter Kannabi against the wall at point-blank range. To put it crudely, it was a textbook kabedon.

Gazing down at Kannabi from this proximity, I saw that her eyes were slightly---yet unmistakably---more bloodshot than usual. The slender fingers clutching my tie were trembling, despite the lack of a chill in the air.

Regardless of whether they were actually dating, Hijiri had been Kannabi's friend since middle school.

In a desperate voice, she whispered at a volume so low it barely reached my ear.

``False rumors are like vibrant, heavy paint. The moment they're splashed, they make everyone uncomfortable, and scrubbing them away takes significant time and effort. Even then, all that remains in the public's mind is the impression of just how garish that color was.''

I gave a silent nod. The school chime began to ring. I had to get back to class.

``\dots Do we have a chance?''

``Of course.''

Kannabi's hand let go of my tie. I straightened my collar, and we faced each other again, back at a normal distance.

``Hijiri-kun isn't the kind of person who should be left scarred by something like this\dots\ especially not because of someone like me.''

It was phrased in a way that made it difficult to either affirm or deny. I gave a noncommittal tilt of my head before speaking with conviction.

``It's all right. I am absolutely going to make sure he wins.''

I had intended it as encouragement, but perhaps she took it for mere bravado, as Kannabi continued her plea regardless.

``Please. I'm begging you---please help Hijiri-kun.''

Watching Kannabi's slight back as I followed her toward the classroom, I typed out a reply to Hijiri on my phone.

The strategy he'd proposed to me was straightforward. Identify the source of the anonymous slander and force them to apologize for `telling a blatant lie.' If we could manage that, then his credibility could certainly be restored. But could we truly track down the culprit?

\textit{Who, specifically, was it?}

As I sifted through my thoughts, my mind drifted back to memories of last spring.

\textit{`---Leave identifying the culprit to me.'}

The moment I hit send, it was marked as read, and within three seconds, a reply flashed back: `I'm counting on you.' Then, as a follow-up, he sent a single character---the handshake emoji.

Hijiri had a habit of using that emoji; every time he did, it brought back the memory of our first meeting, leaving me with a faint, wry smile. It had taken place shortly after we enrolled, during the training camp held over Golden Week last year. Maybe those three days had marked the true beginning of this current incident.

My mind wandered back to April of my freshman year---a time so painfully earnest and so dazzlingly bright it was nearly blinding.

On the night the tengu danced, an irreversible spring had begun.


% ==============================
%        Chapter 1+
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 1+\\
    A Challenge Letter from 50 Years Ago
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch1p}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 1+}
\vspace{1cm}

``Delta-kun, is it true that you have a younger sister?''

During lunch in the classroom, Mikage's casual inquiry caused Mizasaki to fumble and drop his chopsticks.

``Man, the weather is incredible today! Hey, Delta---let's hit somewhere up on the way home after school.''

While he retrieved his chopsticks and wiped them down with a tissue, Mizasaki kept drumming on my shoulder with far more force than necessary.

``I do have a sister.''

``I thought so. I heard from a reliable source that she's a year younger than you?''

``Hey, didn't you hear about that new coffee shop opening? I'm dying to check it out, man.''

``I was born in May, and my sister was born the following March, so while we're nearly two years apart, we're only one school year apart.''

``I heard your sister is quite the looker.''

``Yeah. I mean, she's just normally cute---''

``Hhkk-shoon!''

Mizasaki stifled the rest of my answer with a colossal sneeze.

``What was that?''

``Hhkk---shoooi!''

Glaring at me with a look that clearly said \textit{Drop it}, Mizasaki directed his sneeze straight toward me.

``Gross.''

``Sorry, allergies. How long is cedar pollen supposed to last this year?''

``The peak has passed, but it'll probably keep drifting around through May.''

``Ohh. Spoken like a true plant master.''

``More like I'm just another victim of the pollen.''

Mizasaki's gambit---derailing the conversation even at the cost of spraying spit---worked like a charm. Watching Mikage tilt her head in confusion, I was reminded of an earlier encounter from when she'd first attempted to recruit us into a club. That was when Mikage had hauled Mizasaki and me through Tsunanagai High School's `Science Division'---the STEM clubs---to show us the ropes.

Mikage Aya, a math enthusiast, had an older brother two years her senior who was supposedly just as obsessed with the subject. Mikage Akifumi-senpai. He was a total VIP: President of the Physics Club and the Student Council President to boot. When I finally met him, he struck me as an incredibly personable, stand-up guy.

But there was one problem.

I had a sneaking suspicion that Mikage's feelings for her brother might be a bit too intense.

To put it bluntly, she was a total \textit{brocon}.

She referred to Hongo-senpai---the Chemistry Club president dating her brother---as a `vixen,' while her brother was `the hottest guy on the planet.'

I could objectively admit my own sister was cute, but even then, `best in the prefecture' or `best in Japan' was probably the ceiling. Bringing the entire world into the equation when describing a sibling's appeal was\dots\ on a whole other level.

Discussing my sister in front of Mikage like this was, in all likelihood, a very bad move. I lacked the finesse to read between the lines, but Mizasaki was a social animal. I decided to quietly follow his lead.

``Man, Mikage-san, word sure travels fast with you. Did that story happen to come from Watari?''

``Yeah. My brother---who likes his soba with oni---no, with grated radish---said he heard it from Watari-kun over in the Physics Club.''

Watari Marejou was the local moron from our middle school who'd somehow ended up in the Physics Club. He was constantly badmouthing us, so if you assumed a rumor started with him, you were usually on the money.

A moment ago, Mizasaki had been busy shoveling down a mountain of white rice, but now he'd abandoned his chopsticks entirely to focus on the conversation. He was likely racking his brain for a way to steer the topic elsewhere.

``Oh yeah, I heard something from him too---apparently some humanities kid actually joined the Physics Club?''

``Yeah. Hijiri-kun from Class D. He said he's aiming for law school.''

``That's crazy, right? Law and physics are complete opposites.''

``Technically, they're both fields dedicated to the study of law, aren't they?''

It didn't seem like my joke landed---Mizasaki stared at me blankly.

``\dots I mean, legal law is law, but the laws of nature are also law, right?''

``Wait, isn't that word also pronounced like `low'?''

``That's low.''

``And the thing on the side of a line?''

``That's row.''

``What about liver sashimi?''

``Raw.''

``Spoken like a true Delta---your English pronunciation is spot on.''

Mikage stared at our mindless banter as if she had no idea how to react. Mizasaki immediately picked up on that and brought the conversation back.

``But seriously, is that Hijiri guy going to be okay in the Physics Club? Watari showed me one of those massive reference books they use; they don't mess around with those formulas, do they?''

``My brother says it's nothing to worry about. Even though Hijiri-kun is from this prefecture, he apparently ranked second on the entrance exam.''

``Not third?''

``Yeah. Just like my brother, Hijiri-kun is what you'd call an outlier.''

As I picked at a few cherry tomatoes, I listened to the story with a trace of bitterness.

Tsunanagai High School---which boasted the prefecture's finest record for university admissions---maintained a curious tradition that allegedly stretched back decades.

Every year, two students---one male and one female---were recruited from outside the prefecture based on exceptional entrance exam scores. By long-standing custom, one focused on the sciences while the other pursued the humanities; together, they were known as the ``out-of-prefecture group.''

As a public institution, Tsunanagai High technically only admitted students residing within the prefecture. Nevertheless, the school went to great lengths to scout these outliers from elsewhere. Allegedly, members of the out-of-prefecture group received full tuition waivers, rooms in a lavish dormitory near campus, and a special monthly stipend of 80,000 yen. The compensation package was absurdly generous.

While the rationale behind the system was never officially stated, rumors suggested it was designed to keep the elite students from becoming arrogant---by crushing them from above. Into this gathering of the prefecture's brightest minds, the school dropped even more elite talents to reign over them. To ensure the top tier didn't become ``frogs in a well,'' they were forced to study alongside a truly overwhelming presence.

Occasionally, however, talents emerged from the ``in-prefecture group'' that surpassed even the scouted recruits. For some reason, these rare individuals were referred to as the ``outliers.''

Hijiri---the student who had joined the Physics Club---was exactly that sort of prodigy. There was no need to worry about his academics.

Mikage took a bite of her convenience-store sushi roll before asking,

``Have you two still never met Hijiri-kun?''

``Not really. We don't have any contact with the Physics Club,'' Mizasaki replied with a shrug, to which I added:

``We barely interact with the Chemistry Club, either---this chatting with Mikage-san is basically the extent of it.''

``True, isn't it? Even if you group the Physics, Chemistry, and Biology Clubs under the `Science Division' banner, we're all focused on such different things that we rarely cross paths. It's a bit of a letdown.''

It was now late April, and the initial rush of new club recruitment was finally tapering off. The Biology Club had only recently begun its own activities, so we hadn't yet reached the point of inter-club socialization. I didn't see much cause for regret, but Mizasaki seemed genuinely crestfallen. Hijiri was likely secondary to his real grievance---Mizasaki was simply devastated at the lost opportunity to mingle with girls from the other science clubs.

As Mizasaki's shoulders slumped---he'd only just finished shrugging them; the guy was remarkably flexible---Mikage tilted her head curiously.

``Does the Biology Club have any plans for Golden Week?''

``Golden Week...? No, we haven't given it much thought. We were just planning to discuss the animal care schedule with our senpai.''

``I see. Well, the Chemistry and Physics Clubs are holding a joint training camp.''

``A-a-a-a training camp!?''

If anyone was capable of articulating a quadruple-stuttered ``a'' in a single breath, it was Mizasaki. It wasn't merely a matter of good diction; his soft palate muscles had to be freakishly well-developed.

``Yeah. Apparently, there's a tradition of holding a Golden Week camp at Sentakusou in Oku-Tsunanagai every year. They seem to have revived it last year, though now that I think about it, the Biology Club wasn't involved then, either.''

``Sentakusou... as in the place where the `laundry' gets done?''

``No way.''

While we continued our little routine, Mikage turned her phone screen toward us. It appeared to be a venerable hot-spring inn with a storied history. Oku-Tsunanagai was technically part of the city, but it lived up to its name by being nestled deep within the mountains at the end of a series of winding roads. Hot springs were the area's sole attraction, and Sentakusou leaned heavily on its baths as its primary selling point.

``It's not exactly a joint event, but since both the Physics and Chemistry Clubs stay at the same inn on the same dates, they inevitably end up mingling.''

``Wait, really!? That sounds incredible! When does it start?''

Mizasaki whipped out his phone and began tapping away with feverish intensity. A quick glance confirmed he was already checking for vacancies at Sentakusou. Of course, during the Golden Week rush, there wasn't a single room available.

``Well... that figures. And it's basically this weekend.''

It was already Wednesday; securing a room for a three-day weekend starting this Saturday would be a Herculean task.

``I heard the Physics and Chemistry Clubs have standing reservations as part of their tradition. Apparently, there were so many new members last year that some of the sophomores had to squeeze four people into a room meant for two.''

``Man... maybe the turnout will be unexpectedly low this year.''

``Actually, even more people joined this year, so I heard the room assignments are a complete disaster.''

``Pyo...''

Letting out a bizarre groan, Mizasaki slumped back in his chair.

Before Mizasaki and I joined---eventually followed by Iwama and Kannabi to round us out to four---the Biology Club had been kept on life support by a lone senior, Karato-senpai. It was only to be expected that we couldn't match the scale of the other clubs. I tried to comfort him.

``Even if an overnight stay is out of the question, we could still use one of our days off for some field research.''

``True. But all we've really learned is how to clean hamster cages... If only we could find a research topic we could use to justify an impromptu field survey.''

``Don't say `justify' it.''

At the time, neither of us realized that a perfect opportunity would present itself later that very afternoon.

Once classes let out, the four of us congregated in the biology room and began the only task currently on our agenda: tending to the hamsters. Of our two hamsters, one---named Jounouchi---was currently convalescing at Karato-senpai's home, as her family operated a veterinary clinic. We allowed the other resident, a gray hamster named Rokujougawara, to roam across the long table while we set about cleaning the cage.

``You know, I really feel like the Biology Club should be out doing more fieldwork.''

Mizasaki---who had spent his entire middle school career cooped up in a chemistry lab---was the one to suddenly broach the subject.

``Nice! Fieldwork!''

I didn't miss the way Kannabi's face twitched in a silent grimace right next to Iwama's enthusiastic reaction.

Given that Iwama had considered joining the basketball club, she likely possessed a fair amount of stamina. Through a series of overlapping circumstances, I'd ended up hiking mountain roads with Iwama twice, and she'd handled the inclines at a brisk pace without even breaking her stride.

Kannabi, by contrast, pridefully labeled herself the ultimate indoor type. I'd only hiked a mountain path with Kannabi once, and by the end of it, she had been utterly winded. \textit{(She'd tried to play it cool and wouldn't admit it, of course.)}

``But shouldn't we start with some indoor experiments first? We only just joined, after all.''

As Iwama gently posed the suggestion to Mizasaki, I watched the tension visibly bleed out of Kannabi's face in relief. She was almost comically transparent. I was fond of fieldwork myself, but I knew exactly what Mizasaki was plotting---he was trying to manufacture a pretext for outdoor activities. On this occasion, I found myself siding with the girls.

``We should focus on learning animal care first; while we're at it, we can take our time deciding how to best use the resources at our disposal.''

``Yeah, I mean, sure, that's true, but\dots''

Mizasaki gave me a mournful look, his eyes practically screaming, \textit{You're betraying me.'}

I trailed after him as he trudged toward the trash bin and whispered:

``If you just want to go on an outing, then invite them like a normal person.''

``No, that's not it\dots''

``How is it not?''

``If I don't have the pretext of research, there's no way I can just ask girls to go out and have fun\dots''

Regardless of the extroverted persona he wore, Mizasaki was still my friend at heart.

With his attempt to sway public opinion a failure, we solemnly returned to our hamster-tending duties. Just then, there was a knock at the door. When we answered, Karato-senpai---one of the third-years---entered the biology room.

``Oh, look at you all, hard at work.''

With that, she dropped her bag onto nearby desk with a thud and promptly clicked the door lock shut.

Karato-senpai was the sole remaining upperclassman in the Biology Club. With practiced efficiency, she performed a point-and-call check of the locks before promptly retrieving Rohtgelb---the light-blue budgerigar---from its cage.

``SAMISHIINEE.''

``You're not lonely anymore, Roo-chan. There, susaa.''

Senpai walked over with a sheet of paper, providing a mysterious sound effect while the bird stretched its wings.

``I found this earlier this morning. Let's tackle this one today.''

``KONCHIWA.''

\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note (SAMISHIINEE / KONCHIWA):} Both lines are the budgie (Rohtgelb) mimicking Japanese speech, written in katakana to emphasize they are ``sounds the bird repeats'' rather than clean, normal dialogue. 「コンチワ」 is a casual, clipped ``こんにちは'' (``Hello''), and 「ササミシイネェ」 is a stretched/slurred ``さみしいねえ'' (``Lonely, huh'' / ``I'm lonely, y'know''). I kept them as KONCHIWA and SAMISHIINEE in roman letters to preserve the pet-like, slightly off pronunciation and the ``parrot phrase'' vibe instead of smoothing them into perfectly natural English.}

``Hello!''

Iwama returned the budgie's greeting as she accepted the paper, while Mizasaki leaned in for a peek.

``Tree frogs\dots\ that's all?''

``That's right, Brown-Hair-kun. Things are a bit scattered, so it might take me a while to track down all the manuals. I'll hand over the others as I find them, but for today, could you help me tend to our frog friends?''

``Of course!''

Mizasaki was always eager to go above and beyond whenever a girl asked him for a favor.

The four of us pored over the care manual in Iwama's hands. It was a multi-colored, handwritten document that condensed all the procedures and tips onto a single sheet. Created in 2020. It was a venerable heirloom passed down from Karato-senpai's own predecessors. At this point, we'd only been given two---one for hamsters and one for tree frogs---though the true nature of the situation was likely something only Iwama and I had picked up on.

Tending to the frogs was actually quite enjoyable. Provided you watched out for one specific detail---the tank lid was the same unhelpful shade of yellow-green as the tree frogs, requiring caution not to overlook a frog clinging to its underside (a warning written in massive, red felt-tip letters)---the rest was straightforward work. The process involved transferring the frogs to a smaller enclosure with a net, scrubbing the main tank, and then returning the residents once finished.

Once the task was complete, Iwama---still clutching the manual---casually addressed senpai:

``Has a tree frog ever actually managed to escape?''

``Huh? How did you know that?''

Karato-senpai's eyes widened behind her black-rimmed spectacles.

``The manual emphasized checking the underside of the lid so much that I thought perhaps\dots''

``Good instincts. Apparently, there was an incident involving an escape a long while back. They had opened the lid to clean, only to find one frog missing. They sifted through the gravel and tried every trick in the book to locate it, but the creature was nowhere to be found. In the end, it was as if the frog had simply vanished from the enclosure.''

``A locked-room mystery\dots''

Ignoring Kannabi's ridiculous mutterings, Mizasaki turned to senpai:

``So the punchline was that it had been clinging to the underside of the lid the whole time?''

``Seems like that was the conclusion they reached.''

``Did they ever find the escaped frog?''

When Iwama asked with concern, senpai slowly shook her head.

``Well, they didn't eventually find it\dots\ but not until the major year-end cleaning. It had been mummified behind the ice machine. It's supposedly still enshrined in the back of that cabinet over there.''

``A sokushinbutsu\dots''

Kannabi muttered once more. That sort of muttered, deadpan commentary had likely gone out of style years ago.

``So just make sure history doesn't repeat itself, okay?''

``Yes!''

Even as Iwama gave a cheerful response, her eyes kept darting---almost imperceptibly---toward the built-in cabinet against the wall. It was clear she was dying to see the mummified frog for herself.

Karato-senpai tilted her head slightly at Iwama's behavior, then returned Rohtgelb to its cage.

To avoid getting in senpai's way as she moved briskly on to feed the other creatures with a casual \textit{Leave the rest to me}, we turned our attention to our most pressing task.

The four of us gathered around the long table to hold a strategy session. We needed to decide on our next course of action.

``Golden Week starts this weekend, right? If we're going to launch a project, this is our perfect opportunity.''

Mizasaki clearly wasn't ready to throw in the towel just yet. He lacked the courage to invite them out directly, yet he clung to the idea of an outing with such stubborn tenacity that I had to admire his sheer resolve.

``Exactly! I was thinking the same thing, so I made sure to leave my schedule completely wide open for Golden Week.''

``Wait, for real, Gamma-san? In that case, we have no choice but to meet up every single day!''

``And do what, exactly?''

``That's what we're about to decide! What about you, Navi-san? If you already have plans, you can prioritize those, of course, but---''

Kannabi stared at Mizasaki, her eyes wide with surprise. Being addressed as `Navi-san' by a guy this flashy must have caught her off guard. She seemed to process it quickly enough, however, blinking a few times as she regained her composure.

``I don't have any plans, other than staying in my room to watch anime.''

``Whoa, that's a pretty significant commitment. Just how high on your priority list is it?''

``If there's an official Biology Club activity, I'll prioritize that, naturally.''

``Hell yes! That means we can still build our entire Golden Week schedule around the club.''

``What about me?''

``Oh, do you actually have plans?''

``Not really.''

``Exactly. All four of us are wide open---it would be a crime not to do something!''

Mizasaki was steamrolling the conversation forward when---

``Nwaao!''

A startled, dopey shout rang out, followed by the heavy thud of a massive stack of papers hitting the floor. \textit{Pii-pii!} Rohtgelb let out a sharp, protesting chirp from inside his cage.

When we turned around, we found Karato-senpai standing frozen in front of the cabinet, paralyzed by shock. It resembled a landslide---a collapse so absolute it felt as though there was no hope of undoing the damage. The documents that had spilled from the cabinet carpeted the floor, burying Karato-senpai up to her ankles.

Abruptly ending our meeting, we rushed to senpai's side.

``Are you all right? Are you hurt?''

``I'm okay, Ponytail-chan, thanks. Here---take a look.''

Trapped where she stood, senpai handed Iwama a small plastic container. Inside the sealed, cube-shaped case sat a bed of silica gel---and a perfectly preserved tree-frog-sama.

``Th-thank you\dots''

Iwama likely wanted to inspect the mummy more closely, but she couldn't bring herself to ignore the disaster at Karato-senpai's feet. She carefully placed the case on a nearby desk and began clearing the papers away from around senpai's feet. Kannabi joined in to help.

Even though senpai was wearing slacks, Mizasaki and I felt a vague need to be chivalrous, so we focused on gathering the papers scattered further away from her.

``Sorry... I've always been a bit of a klutz, ever since I can remember.''

``I have a huge weakness for klutzy girls!''

``Really? That makes me so happy.''

While Mizasaki engaged senpai in his usual brand of idiotic banter, the two girls finished clearing the documents from around her feet. Once senpai was free, the five of us began the arduous task of bundling the scattered documents.

They consisted mostly of research papers and survey reports. It was a massive collection of data that seemed to have accumulated over the course of many years. Though some were tattered or creased, the majority were in decent condition, clearly having been preserved for long-term storage. We sorted them into broad chronological categories and spread them out across the long table. Karato-senpai then performed a more detailed organization before returning them to the cabinet.

That was when we stumbled upon a peculiar booklet.

What made it so strange was... well, just about everything about it felt unsettling.

The aged cover was, for some inexplicable reason, a garish, toxic shade of red. Compared to the natural, earthy tones of the other Biology Club materials, this one stood out with aggressive intensity. The cover was virtually devoid of information, save for a single torii-like symbol rendered in black ink.

Kannabi was the first to show any real interest.

``What is this thing?''

``Yeah, this doesn't exactly scream `Biology Club' to me.''

``Hey, Rei---what's inside?''

With Iwama leaning in, her curiosity piqued, Kannabi opened the red booklet for them both to see. The pages were made of discolored, coarse paper---so ancient it likely predated everything else that had spilled from the cabinet.

I peered over their shoulders. The first page was similarly sparse.

\textbf{On the True Identity of Tengu | 1974 | Tsunanagai High School Biology Club (All Members)}

``The true identity of tengu?''

I found myself reading the title aloud. It clearly wasn't something to be taken at face value, yet Iwama pressed a hand to her chin with a look of intense gravity.

``What could it mean...? Senpai, have you ever seen this before?''

``Huh? Um, I have no idea. I don't really know much about things from that far back.''

Karato-senpai seemed entirely disinterested; she didn't so much as glance at the booklet. I offered a suggestion.

``There might be an abstract. Try turning the page.''

If it were a standard paper or report, there should be an abstract at the beginning summarizing its contents.

Kannabi's slender fingers slowly turned the page.

In that instant, time seemed to stand still within the biology room. Sprawled across the entire two-page spread was an eerie passage---writing that felt decidedly out of place for a STEM club.

It bore no resemblance to an abstract; instead, it read more like a poem.

\begin{center}

{\bfseries
Do you believe in tengu\\
Have you ever seen a nue\\
On one night of Golden Week\\
We encountered them\\
The legend of Natsuna Shrine\\
A sealed shintai\\
An illegal art that dances in the dark night\\
When all things are connected\\
The truth will reveal its form

}
\end{center}

``What even is this\dots''

Mizasaki's voice sounded utterly deflated. My reaction was much the same.

``What is this even supposed to be?''

``Text\dots''

Iwama did her best to back up Kannabi's observation, though it was so glaringly obvious as to be almost redundant.

``It's... a rather unique style!''

Kannabi tilted her head and flipped to the next page.

This time, the page was crammed with tiny text from top to bottom. Below a small subheading titled ``Day 1 of the Investigation,'' a chronological account began: ``We set out early in the morning---'' A quick skim revealed that the Biology Club members had traveled to Natsuna Shrine in Oku-Tsunanagai; there, during the ``Tengu Festival'' held in the first half of Golden Week, they had reportedly witnessed several bizarre phenomena.

``Sorry Delta. It looks like there isn't an abstract after all.''

``Aren't you supposed to state your conclusions at the beginning? This isn't a variety show.''

``Or perhaps a mystery novel\dots''

``True! A summary on page one would ruin any good mystery!''

But this was no mystery; judging by the contents, it was a fieldwork report written by the Biology Club.

``Can you flip to the end?''

At my suggestion, Kannabi nodded and began leafing through the pages fast enough to get a general sense of the content.

The chronological descriptions dragged on with maddening persistence. Headlines like ``Encounter! The Nue Mummy'' and ``We Saw It for Sure! The Bizarre Phenomenon in the Dark'' read like snippets from an occult magazine, making me recoil.

Yet, it was the final line that delivered the biggest shock.

\textbf{In the following chapter, we shall interpret our observations from a scientific perspective.}

The surprise didn't stem from the line itself, but rather the fact that it was where the document ended.

Beyond that point, every subsequent page had been cleanly excised by someone. We combed through the stack of documents for matching coarse paper, but nothing else shared that specific level of discoloration.

In short, the report didn't merely lack an abstract---it had been robbed of its conclusion.

``That kind of thing used to be a big deal, didn't it? `Uncovering the Truth Behind the Legend' and all that?''

``The premise of what they were trying to do is actually very interesting.''

``A shame the `uncovering' part is missing.''

Maintaining my stubbornly critical tone, I felt Mizasaki lean into me.

``Come on, though---this is it, isn't it?''

It really was. I knew exactly why Mizasaki's hand was kneading my shoulder with such excitement.

``It's almost as if it's telling us to finish the investigation ourselves.''

Kannabi's words were spot on. The ``research theme'' Mizasaki had been trying to manufacture---a pretext for an impromptu field survey---had quite literally landed in our laps. And the destination was Oku-Tsunanagai. Coincidentally, the very place where the Chemistry and Physics Clubs were holding their training camp.

It didn't take long for Iwama to flash that ``told-you-so'' smile.

``Hey! Let's investigate this during Golden Week... scientifically!''

We would revisit a legend that Biology Club members had supposedly investigated for themselves fifty years ago.

It was an intriguing idea, yet I couldn't help but feel some reservations.

``I have my doubts about whether we'll see any results. A legend is just a legend. If these `bizarre phenomena' can't actually be observed, we won't even know where to start. And as for this `nue mummy'---even if a group of high schoolers asked to borrow it for a CT scan, the shrine wouldn't take us seriously.''

Karato-senpai stubbornly refused to let us help return the documents to the shelves, so while she worked, the four of us huddled around the red booklet at a nearby desk to discuss our options.

``Ehh, but it sounds like fun.''

``I'm not saying it wouldn't be fun. But if we're doing this as an official club activity, that can't be our only reason. Research and investigation---no matter how enjoyable---are not games.''

``\dots Delta. You don't want to do this?''

Kannabi asked quietly. Beside her, I noticed Iwama watching me with cautious eyes.

I'd miscalculated. I hadn't intended for it to sound like that. I scrambled to set the record straight.

``No, naturally I want to join the investigation as well. It's just\dots\ how do I put it\dots''

My mind had drifted back to the cherry blossoms we'd seen on the hill behind the school. We had made a point of hiking that mountain path together---with Iwama pinning her hopes for a fulfilling high school life on the trip---only to find the heart shape shattered when we finally reached the tree\dots\ I couldn't bear the thought of seeing that same look of disappointment on Iwama's face again.

Not having expectations from the start hurt a lot less than having your expectations betrayed.

We had eventually managed to find a heart back then, so things had worked out---but there was no guarantee we would be so fortunate this time. If we traveled all that way only to discover the seniors were just messing around, then the prospect of that being our first endeavor as the Biology Club left a sour taste in my mouth.

``I think we should examine it properly ahead of time. A high school report that doesn't even have an abstract---does it really deserve a re-investigation? I want to vet the contents properly first.''

``So you're saying we should do some legwork first. Yeah, that makes sense. It's obviously sketchy\dots\ Sorry, Gamma-san---when it comes to research, this guy is dead serious, almost to an irritating degree. It's not that he doesn't like your idea, so don't worry.''

``Huh, me? It's fine, totally! Don't worry about it! I just said it on a whim anyway.''

Iwama's face broke into a look of visible relief. Thanks to Mizasaki's intervention, the atmosphere in the room---which had been on the verge of cooling---thawed back to normal.

Perhaps I was overthinking things simply because it was our first project. I mused on the thought.

``If everyone is prepared to accept whatever we find\dots\ then I'm not actually against going to investigate. I phrased it badly.''

``Are you the type who won't confess unless you're guaranteed a positive response?''

Kannabi caught me off guard with that verbal strike, leaving me momentarily speechless.

``No, he's the type who can't confess even if he's one-hundred-percent sure,'' Mizasaki said, running his mouth again.

Iwama stared at me, wide-eyed. I cleared my throat.

``How many things in this world do you think are a strict one hundred percent?''

``See? This is what I mean.''

Mizasaki's ribbing helped lighten the mood. I didn't believe I was in the wrong, but since he had diffused the tension I'd nearly exacerbated with my overly solemn response, I owed him a debt of gratitude.

``Regardless, the first thing we need to do is actually read this report. We've only skimmed it so far\dots''

I pulled the booklet closer and flipped through it. Given that it was fifty years old, the paper had become quite fragile. In certain spots, the paper was already beginning to fray along the lines where it had been awkwardly folded. Maybe we should make copies before we read it.

Hm\dots?

Upon closer inspection, I spotted something unusual. Whether it was dust or simple grime, tiny specks of debris were wedged into every page. If it were only the cover, that would make sense. But this booklet was dirty on the inside too. What kind of treatment would leave it in this state?

``\dots The copies.''

Kannabi suddenly spoke.

``I think we should make copies, but\dots\ could you come with me for a moment?''

The request was aimed at me. I had been thinking along the same lines, so I rose with the booklet in hand. Kannabi didn't even look back---she just strode toward the exit.

``Then\dots\ I'll go.''

``Thanks! If we all went, we'd probably just get in the way, so we'll wait here.''

``Got it.''

As I followed Kannabi, a sense of déjà vu washed over me. Whenever Kannabi acted this suddenly, there was invariably a reason for it.

We headed to the staff room, explained our situation to a teacher who seemed relatively unburdened, and were granted permission to use the annex print room. We hadn't been enrolled for long, so entering the staff room made me a bit nervous---but Kannabi appeared to find the interaction even more daunting than I did, cowering behind me the entire time. Fortunately, the balding gentleman who assisted us was incredibly kind, even taking the time to demonstrate how to operate the multifunction printer.

To activate the machine, one had to scan a student or employee ID equipped with an RFID chip. Since Kannabi made no move, I fished my student ID out of my wallet and scanned it to unlock the controls.

I began with the cover, making five copies of every two-page spread. That gave us four sets for the freshmen and one spare for Karato-senpai. Even with such high-performance equipment, the scanning process was a slow one.

``So, what's the plan?''

Finding ourselves alone in the print room, I questioned Kannabi as she stood beside me.

``I wanted to be alone with you. Is that bad?''

``I'm asking why you made such an effort to isolate us.''

``\dots Love talk.''

``The way you said that makes it pretty easy to tell you're lying.''

Predictably, Kannabi changed the subject at once.

``Didn't you notice something when you looked at that report?''

``Notice\dots? I already mentioned most of what I saw back in the room. But---yeah. The sheer amount of debris wedged into the gutters of the pages caught my attention.''

``And what conclusion do you draw from that fact?''

``Not a clue. Did you spot something, Kannabi?''

``Nothing in particular.''

I lifted the lid of the printer, flipped the page, and hit the copy button once more. A prolonged whir reverberated through the room.

``\dots Delta. Do you have a problem with these kinds of occult stories?''

Being asked that made me reflect---yeah, I really had forced her to walk on eggshells around me.

``I don't hate it, but to be blunt, the whole thing feels incredibly sketchy; are we really okay taking a report like this at face value?''

``You're just so serious.''

The copier ground to a halt; it was out of paper. While Kannabi flailed about in uncertainty, I pulled a fresh ream of B4 from the box beside the printer and tore it open.

Our homeroom teacher had mentioned that Tsunanagai High used a specific brand of recycled paper called ``Wakagawa Paper,'' which was donated in bulk every year by a local firm. It possessed a distinctive, lead-white tint and was apparently the school's big nod toward sustainability goals.

``So, Kannabi, are you actually into stories like that?''

``I adore them.''

\textit{Yeah, that tracked.} It was pure prejudice on my part, but she had always struck me as the type. After all, she had already mentioned her interest in astrology.

``But do you honestly think it's worth investigating as a club project?''

``Of course I do; and honestly\dots\ I think you're secretly hoping for it too, Delta.''

As I tilted my head in silent query, Kannabi elaborated.

``We're going to investigate a local legend by witnessing bizarre phenomena that only occur under the cover of night. Our destination is Oku-Tsunanagai; it's an hour's bus ride from the station, and by the time night falls, the buses will have stopped running.''

``And your point is?''

``Meaning that if we actually investigate, it'll likely turn into an overnight trip.''

It was just Mizasaki and me on the walk home. Kannabi had scurried off with Iwama, so the two of us decided to grab a bite to eat on the way---a little street-food excursion. We veered away from the usual commute route and headed toward the Central Shopping District. Mizasaki's gait was so light it looked as though he might burst into a literal skip at any moment.

To my surprise, the investigation plans began moving at a breakneck pace. By the time we returned from making copies---\textit{(even though I was the one who did all the actual work)}---the first thing I heard was Iwama exclaiming, ``It sounds like we can get rooms!''

Iwama had called Sentakusou on a whim, and by some miracle, a group had just canceled; we managed to snag exactly two rooms for the same dates as the Physics and Chemistry Clubs. Better yet, it was right in the middle of the Tengu Festival---the exact window when those club members from fifty years ago had reportedly witnessed the phenomena. It was no small wonder that Mizasaki looked ready to break into a dance.

Thus, it was decided: we were embarking on an overnight field investigation. On incredibly short notice. This very weekend.

Theoretically, I still believed we should vet the material first, but if I dawdled, the opportunity would vanish entirely. We resolved to continue studying the report while simultaneously drafting a schedule, securing permissions, and prepping for the actual survey. This meant that upon returning home, I'd have to explain this impromptu ``investigation'' to my parents---all while diving deeper into that suspicious report.

Despite the mountain of work ahead, Mizasaki insisted that street-snacking was a vital chapter of the high school experience. We made our way toward the new coffee shop, ducking into a narrow back alley to avoid the crowded main street. It was azalea season; red, white, and pink blossoms were spilling across the roadside in full bloom.

``Back in grade school, everyone used to suck on these, but now everyone claims they're toxic, so nobody touches them anymore.''

For a fleeting second, I thought he was referring to cigarettes---but children don't smoke, and I didn't need to be a genius to realize he was looking at the azalea nectar.

``Toxicity doesn't fluctuate with the era; roadside azaleas have always been, for the most part, perfectly safe.''

``True, but expecting kids to differentiate between species is asking too much, so people just defer to modern, boring common sense and ban them entirely.''

As I spoke, I plucked a pristine-looking blossom that seemed to have just opened and drew the nectar from its base. A tiny bead of nectar emerged, spreading a subtle sweetness across my tongue.

``Are you sure that's safe?''

``This isn't a poisonous rengetsutsuji. You should try it, Mizasaki; it tastes like childhood.''

Mizasaki stared at the blossom with an uncertain gaze for a moment before shrugging.

``Nah, I'll pass. Safety first.''

``If you're that worried, just spit it out; most natural toxins are harmless if you don't actually swallow them.''

``What? Even the poisonous mushrooms?''

``My father used to put anything and everything in his mouth; apparently, there are some fungi so potent they'll turn your entire face numb.''

Mizasaki winced as though he'd already tasted something foul.

``Ugh. This is why fieldworkers are---''

``And yet, chemists are the ones licking unknown reaction products.''

``You're talking about Carl Wilhelm Scheele? That's ancient history, man.''

Ultimately, despite all our back-and-forth, Mizasaki didn't touch the azalea. According to him---

``I've moved past sucking nectar in a way that harms the blossom.''

He likely wanted to sound like some world-weary romantic, but I could state with confidence that he hadn't even brushed against the flower, let alone harmed it. He had been practically attached to my hip the entire time.

The coffee shop we were targeting featured a Western-style exterior with white-washed walls that seemed to glow in the setting sun. A rich, roasted scent reminiscent of brown sugar wafted out onto the street, likely from their own in-house roaster. With their grand opening sale offering half-off cappuccinos at just 300 yen, both Mizasaki and I placed our orders without a second thought.

``So, their specialty is the cappuccino rather than a standard brew.''

Clutching a piping-hot paper cup, Mizasaki tilted his head curiously as we walked.

``They used an espresso machine, didn't they? Unlike the drip coffee at places like Sansetsudo, this shop relies on pressure extraction.''

``Mhm, so it's the actual brewing method that sets it apart.''

As I took a sip, the robust bitterness of the coffee and the richness of the frothed milk complemented each other surprisingly well. Still, at a full price of 600 yen, my wallet would definitely start protesting. This wasn't a place I could afford to frequent often.

Without any particular reason, we took the long way back along the shore. The evening sea had deepened into a dark hue, and the beach---bathed in the low, slanting sun---saw its shadows sharpened into stark relief. I'd seen the view enough times to grow weary of it, yet it remained a perfect spot for a mindless stroll.

Listening to the waves whisper and hiss against the sand, I broached a subject that had been weighing on me with Mizasaki.

``Mizasaki, are you honestly happy with your choice to join the Biology Club?''

``Huh\dots?''

``You've always preferred chemistry over biology, haven't you?''

``And today made it glaringly obvious---next to the Biology Club, which is basically rebuilding on instinct, the Chemistry Club seems far more established.''

``Oh, that? The Biology Club is better, hands down.''

As I looked at him, prompting for an explanation, Mizasaki took an amused sip of his cappuccino.

``Sure, I'll likely end up in chemistry or pharmaceuticals, and if efficiency were my only concern, then the Chemistry Club might have been the logical choice.''

``But if we're going by that logic, you never would've joined the chemistry club back in middle school, either.''

``A foundation in chemistry is essential if you want to understand the biological mechanisms of living organisms.''

``Even so, you really threw yourself into it with me.''

``And look, I'm not saying it's a matter of owing you---I just genuinely enjoyed what we did, and I want to carry that same energy into high school.''

``\dots Then I'm glad.''

My cappuccino was gone. Mizasaki finished his nearly simultaneously and shot me a grin.

``To be honest, I'm actually pretty fired up about this investigation, too.''

``You mean the scientific interpretation of the Tengu legend?''

``Well, that too, but\dots''

Despite being alone, Mizasaki lowered his voice to a conspiratorial whisper.

``No matter how you look at it, this investigation is basically going to be a `test of courage' at a shrine in the dead of night, right?''

``Roasted aroma... a dark roast, likely espresso-based... sand on your shoes... fine, whitish particles\dots''

The second I stepped through the door, my little sister, Asahi, began performing a literal component analysis on me in the entryway. In layman's terms, she sniffed me and scrutinized me from head to toe.

``Hmph. So you hit that new coffee stand in the Central Shopping District, then took a stroll along Maegahama?''

``How could you possibly know that? I'm home.''

``Welcome back. Was it a girl?''

``Mizasaki.''

Asahi narrowed her eyes at me, as if searching for a vulnerability. She was small in every sense of the word, yet her attitude was immense. I hadn't even been granted permission to remove my shoes; I just stood there, enduring my sister's audit.

Ever since she discovered I'd shown Iwama and Kannabi around Tsunanagai, she'd apparently taken it upon herself to `manage' her older brother, and was now plagued by needless worry that I might get swept up in some adolescent romance. Asahi held a rigid ideology: getting bogged down in `who likes whom' was beneath a man, and a true Japanese male should attend an all-boys school to devote himself entirely to academics and athletics. Once she realized that would prevent her from attending the same school as me, however, she grudgingly sanctioned my enrollment at the co-ed Tsunanagai High.

She apparently gleaned some sort of assurance from my expression that I wasn't lying, as she looked satisfied and retreated to the living room.

A quick check of my phone calendar revealed that both of our parents would be home late. Perfect. By the time they returned, Asahi would likely have retired to her room. I could wait until then to broach the subject of the field investigation plan.

\dots Or so I thought, but my parents arrived home together while Asahi and I were still seated at the table. They began drinking wine in the dining room alongside the deli food they'd picked up, and Asahi joined them without a second thought, leisurely peeling an apple.

If I waited any longer, I'd lose the time I needed to scrutinize that report. So I gave up and informed them that an overnight field investigation might have been scheduled for this coming weekend on short notice.

They gave their consent, though Mom naturally raised the obvious question.

``Huh? You're already going on a camp? You only just joined.''

``It's essentially an orientation camp. It's an annual tradition for the science clubs.''

The explanation came from Dad; I had no idea what an `ori-camp' was, but Mom seemed satisfied enough. Dad was a Tsunanagai High alumnus, and he'd actually been a member of the Biology Club himself.

``It's a tradition for Chemistry and Physics, sure, but for Biology, it's just that the dates happen to coincide.''

``Oh, is that right? Now that you mention it, that's how things have been lately. There are all sorts of ominous rumors about Oku-Tsunanagai, so you'd better be careful.''

For some reason, he found that hilarious and burst into laughter---ha ha ha ha. What a drunk.

With overnight permission and a promise of financial backing secured, I retreated to my room. I opened my laptop, pulled up a browser and a document, and laid the copies of the report beside me. Then, I began my research.

For this round of preliminary research, my task was to investigate the vegetation and ecosystem of Oku-Tsunanagai. If there were phenomena unique to that area, there was a high probability that the local environment played a role. I started with a broad search, scouring materials published by the Ministry of the Environment and the prefectural government.

``The rooms are gender-segregated, right?''

Asahi asked the question from directly behind me, as if she'd been standing there the entire time.

``Obviously. We booked two separate rooms.''

She leaned in and sniffed the air around my neck.

``Do I smell a lie?''

``Nope. Just calibration. If you don't establish a baseline regularly, you won't notice any deviations.''

``What are you, a precision instrument?''

By smelling my ``normal'' scent ahead of time, she could apparently detect any odor particles that had clung to me more easily. It was rational, sure, but I wished she'd spare a thought for how it felt to have my little sister sniffing me all the time.

Like a cat, Asahi had disappeared before I knew it.

Just as I was thinking I'd wrap up my research and head to bed, I noticed a sticky note on the back of my chair. Written in pencil, it read: ``No keeping a woman on the side!''

Not that I had any women on the side---or anywhere else, either.

Thursday wasn't originally an activity day, but after school the four of us gathered in the biology room to go over our plan.

It looked like everyone had gotten their parents' permission. The problem was our supervisor.

``I've got something I can't miss, so I can't go. I'll take care of the critters here, so you four go have fun for three days.''

Karato-senpai said that when she dropped by the biology room to check on things, then promptly left.

Tokumura-sensei didn't have a schedule that worked, either. Given how last-minute this was, that wasn't surprising. Apparently, though, sensei was going to talk to the Chemistry Club and Physics Club advisors about it.

``Well, we'll manage. We've already booked the inn, so we've just gotta roll with it.''

``Yeah! Let's share what we found!''

Leaving Mizasaki aside, even Iwama was clearly fired up now, fidgeting like she couldn't sit still.

Each of us got what we'd prepared for the assignment ready.

I pulled the Word document I'd made out of my bag. Three double-sided A4 pages. I'd printed four copies at home and stapled them together. As I handed them out, I looked over what the other three had brought.

Iwama had summarized her notes by hand onto a single A4 sheet, then copied it. The writing looked like a penmanship sample, and the boxes framing the text---and the arrows connecting everything---were so straight they could've been drawn with a ruler. Even the easy-to-follow layout was flawless; it almost felt like a work of art.

Kannabi hadn't prepared anything to hand out; instead, she set several ominous-looking books on the desk.

Mizasaki just had a notes app open on his phone, like he'd typed everything in there.

``I think I should go first!''

Iwama said once we were ready. No one objected.

Based on the roles we'd decided yesterday, Iwama would organize the key points and draft what we should investigate; I would summarize the local environment; Kannabi would compile the ghost stories and legends; and Mizasaki would gather any other relevant anecdotes. The fact that we'd divided things up so smoothly, without a single argument, was almost miraculous. Maybe it helped that our interests and strengths were all slightly different, but they balanced out.

``It was written out at length in that report, but when you boil it down, the structure is pretty simple\dots''

To align our understanding of the report we'd each read, Iwama---efficient as always---condensed its contents and laid out our guiding framework.

Our mission was to recreate the investigation the biology club members had conducted fifty years ago.

Broadly, it consisted of the following four parts:

{\bfseries
\begin{enumerate}[label=\circled{\arabic*}]
\item Scientifically identify the true nature of the tengu phenomenon they claimed to have experienced.
\item Scientifically identify the true nature of the nue mummy at Tsunanagai Hachimangu Shrine.
\item Consider why beliefs like \circled{1} and \circled{2} had developed in the Okutsunanagai region.
\item If possible, find the missing latter half of the report and check our answers.
\end{enumerate}
}

That was the gist of it, explained cleanly and without gaps in Iwama's memo and her spoken overview.

``And I also tried putting together a rough itinerary\dots''

About a third of the handout Iwama passed around was a proposed schedule for the fieldwork. She'd called it tentative, but it wasn't just ``what we'd investigate when''---it even covered meals, bus times, and detailed plans for check-in and check-out. She'd also accounted for that day's sunrise and sunset, plus the inn's dinner and breakfast hours.

``Gamma, you looked all of this up and built it?''

``It wasn't that much work. The only downside was that sunset and dinner were pretty close together\dots''

I skimmed the schedule too, impressed by how smoothly and evenly it allocated our investigation time from morning through night, not just during the day. Since this was biology club activity, our targets would naturally center on nature. And when nature---especially animals---was the subject, conditions changed drastically depending on the time of day, so in the early stages it was best to check broadly: morning, noon, evening, and night.

``Overall, I don't see any issues with Iwama's plan. Is it fine if we decide the specifics---what we check and when---after we weigh the information the rest of us brought?''

``Yeah, that's what I was thinking!''

And with that, next up was Kannabi: she'd explain the crucial legends, incorporating what she'd learned from her books, using slides on her tablet.

Just as Iwama had summarized, the mysteries the club members from fifty years ago said they'd encountered could be divided into two main categories: strange phenomena believed to be tengu-related, and the nue mummy preserved at the shrine.

``The tengu stories weren't actually just an Okutsunanagai thing---they were known nationwide.''

Kannabi opened a thick, ominous book titled \textit{Encyclopedia of Japanese Ghost Stories} to the tengu section and set it beside the copied report. First, she gave us a quick rundown of the stories.

Tengu laughter. In the mountains, laughter echoes from nowhere at all. If you laugh back, it laughs even louder.

Tengu stones. Rocks come flying over people's heads, or down onto roofs. Yet for some reason, the rocks can't be found afterward.

Tengu tree-felling. Deep in the mountains, the sound of someone felling a huge tree echoes out. Yet for some reason, no trace of it can be found afterward.

``These three were the phenomena they said they ran into fifty years ago. Every one of them was in the encyclopedia, and with a quick internet search I found tons of similar legends. Which means the seniors back then experienced well-known tengu ghost stories firsthand---in Okutsunanagai.''

Usually Kannabi only said dumb things, but when it came to ghost stories, she was dead serious.

``On the other hand, it seems the nue mummy only exists in complete condition at Tsunanagai Hachimangu Shrine.''

Kannabi flipped through \textit{Encyclopedia of Japanese Ghost Stories} and opened it to the nue entry.

``I think this is common knowledge, but just in case: the nue is a legendary creature from Japanese folklore, often described as\textit{`a monkey's face, a tanuki's body, a tiger's limbs, a snake's tail, and a voice like a thrush.'} ''

With the book held out toward the three of us, she must have memorized it. She'd called it common knowledge, but I hadn't known. Judging from their expressions, Iwama and Mizasaki hadn't either.

The book even had an illustration of the nue. It was like a Japanese chimera---an eerie monster that made you think, \textit{There's no way something like this could actually exist}.

``At Tsunanagai Hachimangu Shrine, the nue mummy is only put on special display once a year, during the tengu festival season. Photography's prohibited and no official pictures are circulating, so all I could get were impressions from people who'd seen it in person, but there were lots of comments like `it was smaller than I expected' and `it was wrapped in cloth so I couldn't really see it.'''

``They're doing the exhibit this year too, right? Then we might get to see the real thing.''

When Mizasaki added that, Iwama spoke like she couldn't wait to see the mummy.

``Maybe we should go see the mummy first when we arrive! According to the report, the seniors actually experienced the tengu ghost stories from evening into night.''

No one objected. It probably wouldn't take that long to view the nue mummy.

``If there's one problem, it's this `tengu tree-felling,' right?''

I pointed it out, tapping the relevant passage in the copied report and showing the three of them.

``It says tengu tree-felling was something they observed when the club members got lost and ended up entering\textit{`forbidden area: the Northern Forest.'} They claim they witnessed a huge tree fall right in front of them, but if we want to recreate that, we'd have to go into the forbidden area.''

``Yeah\dots\ maybe fifty years ago you could get away with it as an honest mistake, but not anymore\dots''

``What do we do? If we ask the shrine, maybe they'll let us\dots\ but it might be tough. Even for research, I feel like they won't easily allow people into places you can't enter for religious reasons.''

``This time, we don't need to go that far. I don't think a tree is just going to fall on its own right in front of us.''

``Yeah. This is our first investigation. Let's not dig too deep into the messy parts.''

So for tengu tree-felling, we decided to limit ourselves to whatever information we could gather.

After Kannabi, it was my turn to explain Okutsunanagai's environment. Everything was written on the printouts I'd handed out, so I went through it in order while we checked the contents.

``Okutsunanagai is inland from here---about fifteen kilometers in distance, and about three hundred meters higher in elevation. It sits around the boundary between the cool temperate zone and the warm temperate zone, so it's a fun region where evergreen broadleaf forest and deciduous broadleaf forest mix as natural woodland.''

``Um, Delta---when you say fun\dots''

``Just moving a little within the same region, you can see both the kind of laurel forest around here, and beech-and-mizunara forests you usually have to go deeper into the mountains to find. That's fun, isn't it?''

``So it means you can see that much environmental variety, right! Like being able to eat both Italian and French food at the same restaurant.''

``Like carpaccio and terrine being plated on the same dish, maybe?''

Iwama's analogy only got more complicated when Kannabi made it more specific.

``Huh? What was terrine again?''

I brushed off Mizasaki getting hung up on the weirdest detail with a ``Google it,'' and kept going.

``Okutsunanagai's nature has been protected as the shrine grove of Tsunanagai Hachimangu Shrine at the center of the area, so it avoided development until recently. That means there's almost no cedar plantation forest for forestry purposes, but instead there are lots of huge cedar trees near the shrine---trees that were dedicated hundreds of years ago. That's one distinctive feature.''

On the printout, I'd pasted in a rough vegetation map pulled from prefectural government materials. Surrounded by laurel forest, the higher-elevation areas had mixed stands of deciduous broadleaf trees and hinoki; around the shrine, cedar forest was distributed. The settlements were concentrated along the river running through the valley.

``I put the flora and fauna list together on page two. Thanks to the varied environment, you can apparently find most of Japan's typical mammals here. There aren't any particularly rare wild birds, but there are a decent number. For plants, the guide mentioned things like ikarisou and urashimasou for this season---famous wildflowers.''

Rather than just hearing me describe it, it'd be better for them to look at the photo materials. I gave them time.

``There are this many snakes too\dots?''

``Yeah, there are all kinds!''

Iwama reacted to Kannabi's muttered comment. The words were similar, but the meanings were probably the exact opposite.

``It's a mountain area, so of course there are. If we watch out for mamushi and yamagagashi, we should be fine. Neither is aggressive, so if we stay out of the brush, we won't get bitten in the first place.''

``Sounds like we should wear solid shoes and long pants. And if we're walking at night, flashlights.''

``What about the school tracksuit!''

``Yeah. Navi definitely has that too.''

I had no objections. At Tsunanagai High School, apparently thanks to alumni donations, they were issued sportswear from a famous domestic outdoor brand. Long sleeves and long-pants outerwear---the kind field researchers used all the time. You could head into the mountains for a survey in it without it looking out of place.

After that, we checked our belongings, and finally it was Mizasaki's turn to present.

``I mainly combed through unofficial records---personal blogs and map reviews\dots''

As a talk, it was hilariously entertaining, but there was only a little that seemed directly useful to our research.

Still, it was good to know there were quite a few people who claimed they'd experienced strange phenomena like tengu laughter and tengu stones, and rumors of people spotting something like a nue at night.

The fact that there were sightings not just in the record from fifty years ago, but also as recent experiences, suggested it might be possible for us to observe those phenomena ourselves.

Other than that, there were stories like: Okutsunanagai had a ghost tale about stone walls moving; an older blogger claimed a whole period was missing from official maps and records; the surrounding area used to be called something like ``Bandori Mountain,'' but when he looked up ``Bandori'' all that came up was anime, so he had no idea what it meant; and the tengu festival sounded like a blast, with food stalls lining the approach path. Stuff like that.

In the end, we moved on to planning the schedule.

``Of course we'll adjust flexibly on-site, but since most sightings of strange phenomena seem to happen from evening to night, I think it's best to focus our investigation on that time window.''

Everyone agreed with Iwama. I added on.

``At night, our range of movement will be limited. So during the day on day one, we narrow down locations, then do a probing survey that night. During the day on day two, we examine whatever caught our attention, then that night we go all-in and re-check in earnest---that kind of flow.''

``The forecast says it'll be clear the whole time. And we don't even know if we'll run into any ghost-story phenomena, so we should use Delta's two-step approach to raise our odds as much as we can.''

``Yeah! To grasp the situation and then pin down what's happening, we want to do at least two nights of investigation!''

We'd booked the inn for two nights and three days. This itinerary was probably the best we could do.

As she talked through the fieldwork plan, Iwama looked happier than she had all day.


\fullpageimage{1.jpg}



\thispagestyle{plain}
\vspace{1cm}

% ==============================
%        Chapter 1–
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 1–\\
    Yomotsuhegui
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch1m}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 1–}
\vspace{1cm}

As our meeting was wrapping up, Mikage Aya called me over. She said there was somewhere she wanted to show me.

After telling the three others from the Biology Club ``see you tomorrow,'' I left school a little early with Mikage.

``Where are we going?''

``A house where monsters live.''

Mikage answered flatly without even a hint of a smile. Sounds fun.

We passed through the school gate and headed down the ginkgo-lined road toward the sea, then turned right as if slipping away from the flow of other students. The route was quiet and sparsely traveled, and before long a large white mansion came into view.

It had been built on a wide plot of land separated from Tsunanagai High School's grounds by a single small ridgeline. Beyond a theme-park-like garden with a fountain at its center stood a dignified, symmetrical Western-style building. Two stories tall. Calling it the White House would be an exaggeration, but the white-painted exterior walls and the bold façade with its prominent pillars gave exactly that impression.

The property was enclosed by an ornate metal fence. At the entrance were a large gate and a doorbell.

When Mikage rang the chime, a familiar voice came through the intercom---``Okaaay!''---and the lock clicked open. Following Mikage, I stepped inside and crossed the garden.

The voice belonged to Hongo Nagisa-senpai, the Chemistry Club Captain and Student Council Vice President. Truly, a house where monsters lived.

The large front door opened, and senpai---still in uniform---peeked out from inside.

``C'mon in!''

The entry hall had a high ceiling open to the floor above. The interior was just as impressive as the exterior: a dark crimson carpet covered the floor, and---unbelievably---a chandelier glittered overhead. Somewhere, someone must've been baking, because the air carried the warm, delicious smell of butter and toasted flour.

Even senpai's flashy blond hair looked less ``gyaru'' here and more like some young lady from a wealthy family.

``Pretty crazy, right? It was built with alumni donations. The right side is the boys' dorm, the left side is the girls' dorm. And I'm gonna believe Delchi won't do anything weird, so I'll specially invite him into the girls' dorm!''

Hongo-senpai unlocked the door leading to the left wing and beckoned us in. Mikage headed that way, so---feeling a little hesitant---I followed.

``A dorm\dots\ so this is for the out-of-prefecture students?''

When I asked quietly, Mikage gave a small nod. Senpai, sharp-eared, turned back toward us.

``I'm from Tokyo, so if I tried really hard, I could've commuted from home. But the school's close, and on top of the stipend they cover food and utilities too, so even though Mommy and Pappy miss me, I decided to live here. Look---this place is amazing, right?''

The first floor of the girls' dorm was apparently shared space. A lounge area sat beside a large kitchen. The smell of baked sweets seemed to be coming from there. On the south side---the front of the building---big windows looked out onto the garden, lit up beyond sheer lace curtains.

\textit{Could alumni donations really pay for something this lavish?} It didn't feel like a public high school dorm at all---more like a luxury mansion or an upscale hotel.

And there, sprawled on a sofa in a clearly off-guard state, was one girl.

``\dots Huh?''

She froze when she saw me. Of course she did---she was wearing a tank top and dolphin shorts, basically underwear. Her belly button was even showing. As a matter of courtesy, I looked away.

``Senpai, if you're bringing a guy in here, please tell me first.''

``I'll introduce you! This is first-year Izuta Sho-kun. Delchi, this is second-year Hyakuzawa Ringo-chan. One hundred---Hyakuzawa---and a dignified `rin' and `go.' Call her Ringo-chan, okay? She's in the Physics Club, so I think you'll end up interacting too.''

Still looking away, I got introduced and bowed without bringing her into my line of sight.

``You can look now.''

When I lifted my head, Hyakuzawa-senpai had thrown on a thin hoodie. With the dolphin shorts, depending on the angle, it almost looked like she wasn't wearing anything under it---but if she didn't care, it wasn't really my place to care either.

Hyakuzawa-senpai had a sporty very short haircut, and her sharp eyes made her look like she'd just come back from killing someone. She was on the taller side, with a lower voice, and her words carried an accent that faintly suggested somewhere up north.

If she was relaxing in this dorm, then she must be another of those unusual talents scouted from outside the prefecture. She definitely had the same kind of intensity as Hongo-senpai did once she dropped the gyaru mask.

``Here, sit! Ngo-chi's baking an apple pie for us.''

While preparing tea, Hongo-senpai gestured for Mikage and me to take the sofa. At this point I wasn't going to comment on her nicknames. I obediently sat down.
