With nothing to do, I glanced around and noticed a white cardboard box on an empty sofa, filled with apples. On the side, it read: ``Aomori apples --- K\=ogyoku.'' K\=ogyoku was a tart variety that wasn't suited to eating raw; it was meant for cooking. Probably the pie ingredients.

``Those, Ngo-chi had them shipped in from her hometown.''

``Senpai, are you from Aomori?''

I asked Hyakuzawa-senpai, who was glancing toward the kitchen. She gave a small nod.

``Yeah. Hirosaki. The west side.''

``Not that it's the only reason, but Ngo-chi's apple pie is seriously delicious.''

When Hyakuzawa-senpai stood and headed over to the kitchen, Hongo-senpai lowered her voice and whispered to me.

``If I say `Ringo-chan's apple pie,' do you think it might sound kind of\dots\ lewd?''

``The only person who'd even think that is you, senpai.''

``Cold, huh. Even though you were curious about my $\pi$ too.''

I thought, \textit{You're really going to drag that back up?}

Hongo-senpai had a history of skillfully manipulating new students like us into joining the Biology Club. If all she'd done was use a $\pi$ strap to make Mizasaki and me solve a puzzle, I might've let it slide---but I still hadn't forgiven her for the other target she'd turned those poisonous fangs on.

``Hey, stop making that scary face. About that thing of mine---well, I'm sure you can guess, but if it's someone I've already shown my hand to, the success rate drops by about half. Even if it works, a third time is basically impossible. When the function you use for output starts mixing into the input side, it gets complicated, right? Same idea.''

I was thrown off since I couldn't follow the last part, and Mikage, at my side, filled in the gap.

``When you think while assuming this person's ability, your thinking includes a circuit that suspects you're being manipulated. She means it's hard to manipulate you while also accounting for that.''

\textit{This was starting to sound like some kind of supernatural battle\dots}

``So, Delchi---showing my hand is also me declaring that unless something really extreme happens, I won't do that kind of thing to you guys anymore. So, yeah. I want you to get along with me.''

``\dots Sure.''

I did think, \textit{If she lulls us into lowering our guard, it'll be easier to manipulate us again}---but pointing that out would be missing the point.

Then I heard the oven door open. Hyakuzawa-senpai took out a round pie, baked to a perfect golden brown. A moment later, an incredible smell drifted over: butter, flour, apples, and cinnamon.

With practiced hands, she slid the pie onto a cutting board and started slicing it up without hesitation. She made it look so natural that I asked.

``Hyakuzawa-senpai, is baking your hobby?''

``Not really a hobby. More like\dots\ a lifework. Not just baking---cooking in general.''

``Ngo-chi's isn't cooking, it's tuning.''

I didn't really get Hongo-senpai's add-on, but the apple pie brought over to us looked genuinely amazing. Somewhere along the line, vanilla ice cream had even been added on the side.

``Alright, let's eat before it cools! Itada-kimasss!''

``Itadakimasu.''

Mikage and I bowed our heads toward Hyakuzawa-senpai, then dug into the apple pie.

The taste was\dots\
\textit{how should I put it}---perfect.

The balance of sweetness and tartness, the way the crust was baked, even how strongly the cinnamon asserted itself---everything tasted flawlessly calibrated, like it had been calculated down to the last detail. If not for the fact that the vanilla ice cream seemed like the kind you could buy anywhere, it would've been good enough to serve at a top-tier shop. And even that ice cream, considering the light, airy mouthfeel when you ate it with the pie, started to feel like something she'd picked on purpose.

``Wow\dots\ this is amazing.''

``Mm.''

The person who'd made it was chewing her apple pie with an oddly serious expression.

After finishing what should've been a piping-hot pie in no time, Hongo-senpai looked at Mikage and me with a satisfied grin.

``Alright! You two ate something from the demon realm, so now you're residents on this side too.''

``What do you mean, `something from the demon realm'?''

``You don't know? People around town call this place a den of demons. Even though such cute ladies live here and all.''

I barely managed to swallow the point that was about to slip out: \textit{That's mostly your fault, isn't it?}

``\dots So, Hongo-senpai. Could you tell us why you suddenly invited us here?''

The smile on her face---so bright a moment ago---stiffened just slightly.

``Yeah. Delchi probably has dinner coming up too, so I should say it sooner rather than later.''

Like she didn't really want to, Hongo-senpai set down her cup and straightened her posture.

``About your training camp---can you shorten it to one night?''

``Huh?''

Caught off guard, I asked again. It felt like time had skipped for a second.

``I mean, that field survey where the Biology Club is staying at Senzawataku Manor for two nights---can you cut it down to one? That's what I'm asking.''

``\dots Why?''

``Tokumura-sensei can't go, right? Sounds like you're trying to get the Chemistry Club or Physics Club to supervise, but our advisor, Usugi-sensei, is pretty reluctant. Both Chemistry and Physics do some pretty dangerous work in the latter half of their camps, so they don't have the leeway to keep an eye on the Biology Club's survey too.''

I didn't have an answer. I could imagine it would be a burden.

``They're even talking about having you cancel the survey entirely. If it's just the first half---one night---they might be willing to compromise, so could you adjust---no, change it to that?''

I thought for a while, struggled with it, and settled on an answer.

``\dots No.''

Hongo-senpai stared at me in silence.

``This is an investigation into something unidentified. It's not the same as going out to count wild birds. With just one night, we can't secure the opportunities we need, so we can't just shorten it.''

``Yeah, I get that, but we can't provide an advisor. So what are you going to do?''

``It's during Golden Week. For example, we can get permission again from our parents and go not as a Biology Club activity, but as a trip between friends. In that case, there's no problem.''

``Are you serious?''

``Do I look like I'm joking?''

Hongo-senpai looked at me with a frightening expression, her smile completely gone. I really couldn't handle this person, after all.

But I couldn't just refuse to fight here.

``This is a follow-up investigation into what happened during the tengu festival period. If we're doing it, it has to be in the first half of Golden Week. Since it's mainly night surveys in Okutsunanagai, staying overnight is nonnegotiable. I understand we'll be causing you trouble, but can you also understand that we can't just say, `Okay, then let's change the schedule,' that easily?''

``You're putting a lot of heat into this. For a survey that got decided so suddenly.''

``I wasn't that enthusiastic about this survey either. But once it's decided we're doing it, I think we can't end it halfway. It's our first survey.''

``Who cares? If you fail, you can just try harder next time.''

The way senpai tossed that out so lightly made anger well up inside me.

``Hongo-senpai, you're the one saying that? You're the one who pulled us into the Biology Club when it was on the verge of collapsing. Didn't you entrust us with rebuilding it? And now, right from the start, you're telling us to change the plan, and that if we fail we can just try again---are you calling that your sincerity?''

``That's a different matter, and you know it.''

``It's not different. I'm pushing back because it feels like you're suggesting shortening it too carelessly. There are other paths besides shortening it. Why won't you look for them? Why won't you fight for it? If someone needs convincing, I'll go too. At the very least, I can't say yes yet.''

I noticed Mikage looking at me in surprise. Maybe my words had gotten too harsh. I reined myself in a little.

``I see. So Delchi felt like I wasn't doing my best.''

``Convince the teacher, appoint a different supervisor, make it an unofficial activity---there are plenty of other options. If senpai---who went as far as doing what you did to get us into the Biology Club---has judged that every single one of those is absolutely impossible, then\dots\ sorry. I'll apologize.''

I was trying to soften my words, but it only made me sound even more cocky. I wasn't good at fighting.

Hyakuzawa-senpai gave me an exasperated look that said, ``Oh, come on.''

After a stretch of silence that felt like the calm before an eruption, Hongo-senpai stood and stepped in front of me. Then she gave my head a light pat.

``Alright. I like you.''

``\dots Huh?''

``I like you, Delchi. If you're going to rile me up like that, I've got no choice. I'll start by doing what I can on my end so you can stay two nights as planned.''

On the way home, it was just Mikage and me. Right after we passed through the gate, Mikage muttered under her breath.

``Delta-kun, you're the kind of person who really fights.''

``\dots Forget it. That wasn't a good way to negotiate.''

``I think it's amazing you could say that much to that woman, all by yourself. If it were me, I wouldn't have been able to say anything.''

``Maybe it's just that I don't really know how scary Hongo-senpai can be.''

I ignored the ``that woman'' part and corrected her.

\textit{I know I made an unreasonable demand. Usugi-sensei's concerns are valid. We might end up inconveniencing the Chemistry Club\dots\ I'll apologize to you in advance. I'm sorry.}

``Don't worry about it. If complaints come up within the Chemistry Club, I'll steer things so the anger points at that woman, not at the Biology Club.''

``You don't need to go that far.''

``Leave it to me.''

Mikage wore a gentle smile on her cool, composed face. Fine---\textit{then I'd leave it to her}.

``With someone as passionate as you joining, I'm sure Karato-senpai is happy too.''

Maybe she was trying to cover for me after I'd nearly picked a fight with Hongo-senpai, because Mikage said it gently.

``I don't think there are many people who can fight that hard for their club.''

``Well, actually\dots\ it wasn't really for the club.''

At the words I let slip, Mikage looked intrigued. Faced with her questioning expression, I answered while averting my eyes a little.

``Honestly, it looked like the other three were really looking forward to this survey\dots\ and I didn't want anyone throwing a wrench into it. That was the biggest reason.''

\textit{It wasn't like I wanted to be owed anything.} Even if I was the only one who felt bad and I was the one stuck with the stigma, if it benefited everyone around me in the end, I didn't care---that was just how someone who lived in the shadows got by.

``I see.''

After walking a few steps, Mikage added, like she'd reached some kind of understanding.

``You said you'd make Iwama-san happy, too.''

``Please forget that.''

``I'll tuck it away in the back of my mind.''

``It'd help if you didn't put it away---if you threw it out\dots''

There was no reply. Mikage's expression didn't change much, but somehow she looked a little pleased.

``Hey, Delta-kun.''

Mikage began, slowly.

``The substances that make up the human body get replaced by what you eat, little by little\dots\ but how much time do you think has to pass before it becomes something else entirely?''

``That's like the Ship of Theseus.''

``You know it.''

``It's a paradox from Greek mythology, right? You replace a ship's parts little by little, and when the original materials are completely gone---''

``Can you really say that ship is the same as the original ship? That's the question.''

I wasn't sure what she was getting at, but I answered anyway.

``In terms of matter, I've heard even a person's bones are replaced over the course of a few years. Water would get replaced even faster. But the essence of a living thing isn't the matter---it's the system. Even if the water flowing through it isn't the same as yesterday, a river's name doesn't change. So a person doesn't become `something else.'''

``I figured that's what you'd say, Delta-kun.''

After a brief pause, Mikage added a condition.

``Then what if the matter itself---the thing you ate---was under some kind of bad magic?''

``If I think of it like a radioactive isotope, does that work?''

``Yes. Except what it gives off isn't radiation, it's bad magic. And it doesn't just decrease on its own.''

``Hard to say\dots\ I've never really thought about that.''

``Someday, we might have a day where we think about it together.''

The one who'd posed the question seemed satisfied, even with a conclusion that vague.

Mikage's house was on the west side of the city---the opposite direction from mine. Partway along, our paths diverged.

``See you tomorrow.''

``Yeah. See you tomorrow.''

We were just about to part when Mikage looked at me as if she wanted to say something.

``What is it?''

After a moment, Mikage muttered.

``Delta-kun\dots\ you were cool today.''

``\dots Was I?''

``Probably no one else will tell you, so I wanted to say it.''

A lukewarm wind slipped down the dark road.

Like she was delivering something important, Mikage held my gaze for a while.


% ==============================
%        Chapter 2+
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 2+\\
    I Definitely Saw It
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch2p}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 2+}
\vspace{1cm}

At 7:00 a.m. on Saturday, April 27th, the four first-years in the Biology Club met up at the north exit of Tsunanagai Station.

It was a little early, but that was because Mizasaki had suggested we get breakfast at the café in front of the station. Wearing matching sportswear, we squeezed around a cramped four-person table, had coffee and sandwiches, and then hauled two nights' worth of luggage onto the route bus bound for Tsunanagai.

In the end, the Biology Club's field investigation would run for three days and two nights.

According to Hongo-senpai, an unbelievable coincidence had come to light: the vice-principal just happened to be scheduled to stay at the same inn on the same dates. And on top of that, the wife who was supposed to come with him couldn't make it due to a mild cold, and the vice-principal himself had been wavering right up until the last second about canceling---so they'd been able to appoint him as our chaperone at the last minute, which was an absurd stroke of luck. We should be grateful for Hongo-senpai's quick thinking, too.

I felt bad for the vice-principal, but thanks to his sacrificed vacation, we were able to go on our investigation without any friction. And it looked like I wouldn't have to tell the other three there'd been talk of shortening it to one night.

Once the route bus escaped Tsunanagai's central area to the northwest, it followed a winding riverside road, smoothly passing bus stops with names that sounded like old folktales---``Tanuki Stone,'' ``Teppo Weir,'' ``Tengu's Taro-Washing''---and headed up into the mountains.

Getting breakfast together had been the right call. If I ate at home, I tended to half-ass it, and on the way there I usually spent the ride still dragging my sleepiness along, but after eating something proper while chatting with everyone, I was wide awake.

With Iwama and Kannabi in the front pair of seats and Mizasaki and me in the back, we talked over today's plans and the roughly hour-long ride flew by.

By the way, the Chemistry Club and the Physics Club were apparently each chartering their own bus. Since we had to work around a route bus with limited runs, we were supposed to be the first to arrive.

``Hell yeah, we're here!''

The moment he got off the bus, Mizasaki stretched his arms and legs, putting his joy and sense of liberation on full display.

It was unmistakably a rural hot spring town. Green-covered mountains surrounded it, and along the road running through the center, a clear river rushed by with a loud roar. The bus stop we got off at was the last stop, and it doubled as a bus turnaround with a parking lot. Since it was morning, nothing was open yet, but it looked like there were diners and souvenir shops around. Whether they'd actually open when it mattered was another question.

We wanted to drop off our luggage, so now we headed to our inn, Senzawaso. The road's pavement was peeling and rough. Since Kannabi didn't have a good bag, Mizasaki took her suitcase and carried it swinging from one hand. Iwama, as you'd expect, moved along briskly with a small carry-on backpack in front and a large overnight backpack on her back. I did the same. It was gear that let you walk for a long time over any terrain without tying up your hands. Mizasaki was the same as always---he'd packed his overnight stuff into an enamel sports bag.

Senzawaso was up the river a ways. It was probably the biggest inn in this area. A three-story reinforced-concrete building that felt a bit old. It looked like it had plenty of rooms. If it weren't during a long vacation, it would probably have a lot of vacancies.

Next to a sign that said they were accepting day-trip bathing in the natural hot springs, I saw the word ``Welcome,'' and I couldn't believe it still existed in this day and age. It was the kind where they write down the names of the groups with reservations and line them up. ``Tsunanagai High School Science Club'' was written at the top, but I didn't see the Biology Club. Ours should've been a separate reservation, but maybe the Biology Club was included under the Science Club booking.

When we stepped into the wide entrance, that unmistakable ``inn smell'' washed over us in a thick wave. Whether it came from detergent, deodorizer, or insect repellent, it was a scent you didn't normally encounter, and it made me think, \textit{this really is a trip}.

``I'll take care of the check-in stuff!''

Iwama headed straight for the front desk. We set our luggage down and waited behind her.

We were only supposed to drop off our bags, but Iwama's back was turned as she received a long explanation, leaning forward as if she were hunching in to listen. I had a bad feeling, so I stepped up beside her.

``Did something happen?''

When Iwama turned to me, her face was plainly shaken. While I braced myself for bad news, I decided to stay composed.

``So, um\dots\ it looks like there was a mix-up, and the room\dots\ they only have one left for us.''

A young male staffer at the front desk bowed deeply toward me.

``I'm very sorry, but we have no vacancies today, and the only room we can provide is a four-person tatami room\dots\ we sincerely apologize.''

``We were told there were two rooms available, so we should've reserved two.''

``We're very sorry. It seems there was another request at the same time, and since your party is four people, we mistakenly prepared only one room for you. If you choose to cancel, we of course won't charge you\dots\ we're terribly sorry.''

``Sorry, it's my fault for not confirming properly.''

``Iwama, you didn't do anything wrong. Let's talk it over, the four of us.''

After giving the staffer a small bow, we went back to Mizasaki and Kannabi and explained what had happened.

``Yeah, it'll be fine.''

Mizasaki was the first to speak.

``If it's their screw-up, they should be able to find a way to work it out for us. The two guys can crash in the Physics Club's room or something.''

``But Mizasaki-kun, I heard the Physics Club is right at the limit for rooms too\dots''

``Delta and I can sleep on the floor. We've even done an overnight bus trip together. A cramped space won't bother us.''

That was true, but making that argument in front of Iwama was a mistake.

``There's no way I'm going to make you two do that. If Rei is okay with it, I can share the room too\dots''

Kannabi froze. Seeing that expression out of the corner of his eye, Mizasaki reacted instantly.

``There's no way that'll work. My snoring's insane. Everyone'll end up sleep-deprived.''

That was a lie. I held back the obvious question of what that made me and Watari.

``Wait here. I'll go negotiate with the inn staff one more time.''

With the three of them waiting behind, I went back to the front desk. The staffer looked at me with a deeply apologetic expression. It stung to make an adult look like that when I was just a high schooler. But this was something I couldn't back down on.

``Other students from Tsunanagai High School are supposed to be staying here too. Can't you work something out with their room assignments? Two of us could stay in the room we reserved, and two could move to another room.''

``I'm terribly sorry, but from the standpoint of fire safety regulations, we cannot have them increase their number of guests any further\dots''

``Even swapping would be fine. I'll negotiate to have two girls from their side move into our room.''

``That would be possible, however\dots\ if you're concerned about mixing boys and girls, the other students are also assigned to rooms right up to capacity, with floors separated by gender, so it may be difficult.''

That made me realize something. If the Chemistry Club and Physics Club had split rooms by gender while pushing the capacity limit, then even if we moved rooms, whoever moved in our place would still be a guy. That solved nothing. If there were a six-girl room and a four-boy room, we could swap those, but that would ignore the floor separation.

The option of having the vice-principal---who'd been considering canceling---give up his room also crossed my mind. But if the chaperone no longer had a room, that defeated the whole purpose. Either we shared with the vice-principal, or we pretended the four of us were sharing and then only Mizasaki and I quietly moved rooms.

``Please forgive my presumption, but we can also install a partition in your room. It does not lock, but if you would consider it\dots''

``A partition\dots\ like a fusuma?''

\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note:} Fusuma (襖) are traditional Japanese sliding panels (often paper-covered on a wooden frame) used as room dividers and doors inside a house/inn. Here, the inn staff brought in a removable fusuma to split a single tatami room into two sections for a bit of privacy---though it doesn't lock and can be slid open easily.}

``Yes, sir.''

With the staffer guiding us, we went to take a look at the room.

The room set aside for the Biology Club was in the annex. Unlike the reinforced-concrete main building, the annex looked even older---probably made of wood. It was a small, single-story Japanese-style building connected to the first floor of the main building by a covered corridor, and it only had two rooms: ``Kaede'' and ``Sakura.'' We were led to ``Sakura.'' The hallway was dark and narrow, but the room itself was very clean---maybe it had been renovated.

The staffer took out a fusuma from a wall storage space and slid it into place across the center of the room. We helped. In the end, it would open easily if you put a hand to it, but it did at least divide the space cleanly into two rooms. That said, there was only one entrance. The two in the back would have to pass through the front room if they wanted to leave.

``How does that look\dots?''

The staffer---poor guy---was practically doubled over. I nodded at him with a look that meant \textit{This isn't your fault, so please don't worry too much}, then turned to the other three.

``What do you think? We should be able to secure the bare minimum of privacy, and if we take the side by the entrance, we won't end up going into the girls' side.''

The biggest concern was Kannabi, who'd been hesitant, but her face was blank, unreadable. Maybe because she didn't want to decide the flow herself, Iwama still didn't speak.

``\dots If it's like this, isn't it fine?''

Kannabi said it.

``As long as it's okay with you two if I have to pass by your feet at night, though.''

``Totally fine! If we're in the way, you can step on us!''

Getting stepped on would be a problem, but that was how we managed to settle the room situation.

Since the tatami room was already available for check-in, we left our big luggage in the room, then headed out of the inn with only the gear for the investigation.

We'd left early, so it was only nine in the morning. We had plans to meet the vice-principal at noon for lunch as a first check-in, but until then, we were free. The sky was perfectly clear, and according to the forecast, it didn't look like it would break today. Following the original plan, we decided to go see the nue mummy first.

The first torii gate of Nagatsuna Shrine faced the riverside road. It was an oddly imposing torii, painted vermilion. On the pillar, it read: ``\textbf{Dedicated: CHOSEI. Heisei 24. An auspicious day in April.}'' It still looked new.

\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note:} The pillar inscription is a standard dedication plaque for a donated shrine gate. ``Dedicated'' (奉納) means the torii was offered as a donation, and ``Heisei 24'' is the Japanese era year 2012. ``An auspicious day in April'' (四月吉日) is a conventional phrasing meaning the exact date is intentionally left vague---``on a good day in April.'' ``CHOSEI'' is written in Roman letters on the gate (likely the donor's name, company, or organization).}

Beyond it, a wide approach stretched into the forest. Along both sides of the approach, preparations for food stalls had already begun---putting up tents, hauling in gas canisters. It seemed the Tengu Festival would run for three days starting today.

The shrine's grounds were simple. The approach that began at the first torii turned into a long stone staircase partway up and continued to the second torii in the mountains. Passing through the second torii brought you to a broad, gravel-covered central area with the worship hall, main hall, and treasure hall. A third torii stood quietly to the side of the main hall, and beyond it was a mountain path. If you climbed it, the inner sanctuary at the very back awaited. That much we'd looked up in advance.

Maybe because they held the Tengu Festival every year, there were long-nosed tengu statues scattered around the grounds. Every last one of them wore a fierce expression, glaring down at worshippers with a dagger or a fan in hand.

The four of us lined up at the worship hall and prayed for this investigation to go well. I was the first to lift my head, and Iwama kept praying intently until the very end. Being devout was all well and good. When she finished the final bow, Iwama looked over at me with a refreshed, radiant smile.

``Ah, sorry. Kept you waiting!''

``No\dots\ yeah.''

I answered like an idiot. Mizasaki's ``did you see that smile'' still rang true.\textit{How many people on Earth could put on a smile that pure?} 

A blue wind, carrying faint humidity and the scent of fresh green leaves, brushed my cheek.

Suddenly, it felt like I'd been thrown into something wildly non-routine. I'd done more outdoor fieldwork than I could stand, dragged along by my dad, and yet this time it felt like it was somehow special.

In an instant, the itinerary ahead started to scare me. Not ``scary'' in the literal sense---more like the feeling of standing in line for a roller coaster famous for being a scream machine. A sense that something so intense it would be unbearable was waiting for me in the near future---an omen like that rose up in the pit of my chest.

Luckily, it seemed no one noticed how shaken I was. We moved to the treasure hall. There were hardly any people on the shrine grounds this early, but a small line had formed in front of the treasure hall.

The sign by the entrance read:

{\bfseries
\begin{center}
【The only place in the world】\\
【Special public showing of a real Nue mummy】\\
【27 (Sat)--29 (Mon)】
\end{center}
}

The slogan was ridiculously tacky, brushed in ink onto strips of imitation calligraphy paper, and hung beside the entrance.

``Holy crap! Not just Japan---this is the only mummy in the world!''

``If a Japanese yokai were overseas, that'd be weird.''

``Yeah, but c'mon---maybe the British Museum has one or something.''

``Hold up. The weird part is we're even having this argument on the assumption a real nue exists.''

``It's too early to decide it's fake. You've gotta see the real thing before you judge.''

``Fine. I'll see the real thing first, and then I'll call it fake.''

At our stupid back-and-forth, Iwama laughed like she was having fun.

``Del-chan and Mizasaki-kun's talks are like manzai. They're so funny.''

We weren't trying to do manzai, but if she enjoyed it, I was glad.

``It's nonsense, but weirdly logical.''

With Kannabi, I couldn't tell if that was praise or an insult. It probably was.

The line moved quickly, and our turn came. Admission was 200 yen. Since you could apparently submit expenses like this and have them covered by club funds, Iwama paid for all of us at once.

We headed into the treasure hall. Unlike the outdoors under a blue sky, it was pretty dark inside. Along the walls were Buddhist statues, and seated statues of monk-like figures. The nearest statue was probably Fud\=o My\=o\=o. Carved wooden flames blazed behind it, and it held a long sword while glaring with its eyes wide open.

\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note:} Fud\=o My\=o\=o (不動明王, ``Immovable Wisdom King'') is a fierce-looking Buddhist deity widely revered in Japanese esoteric Buddhism. He's typically depicted with an intense stare, flames behind him (symbolizing purification), and a sword in hand to ``cut through'' ignorance and evil---so the scary, glaring statue with a blade and fire imagery matches the standard iconography.}

``Whoa.''

Mizasaki let out a sound he couldn't help. When I went over to look, there was a tengu mask hanging on the wall, about as tall as we were. Apparently it was from the Edo period.

``This might be life-size.''

``If it is, then it's, like, twelve meters tall.''

``Oh---Delta, you just did that assuming a tengu has a seven-head-tall body ratio, didn't you?''

``Don't go out of your way to do a conversation that's logical but nonsense.''

Assuming the tengu mask was around our height at 1.7 meters, multiplying by seven gave 11.9---about twelve meters. But pointing out the process meant nothing. It was a bad habit Mizasaki and I had---banter like a little skit we weren't even performing for anybody.

While we traded that pointless exchange in low voices, Iwama had a completely captivated look as she stared at various illustrated depictions of tengu, occasionally taking notes. For some reason, Kannabi was staring at Fud\=o My\=o\=o with a seriousness she rarely showed. Mizasaki's feet drifted that way.


``Huh? Come to think of it, they've got Buddhist statues too, even though this is a shrine.''

``Shinbutsu-shugo.''

Kannabi said it, half-blended into the darkness.

``Huh?''

``Nagatsuna Shrine follows the line of Shugendo---where indigenous mountain worship linked up with esoteric Buddhism. It sounds like during the Meiji era, with the separation of Shinto and Buddhism and the anti-Buddhist movement, anything related to Buddhism got removed from the grounds, but traces like this still remain in places like this.''

``Shinbutsu\dots\ what was it again?''

``Up through the Edo period, there were a lot of places where they didn't separate kami and buddhas at all---they were worshipped together. But once the Meiji era began, as the new government aimed to make Shinto the state religion, the Shinbutsu Bunri Edict demanded that the two be clearly distinguished. And the movement that happened along with that was haibutsu kishaku---the abolishing and destroying of Buddhist-related elements.''

``Whoa\dots\ so back then, these guys were probably enshrined in some kind of temple hall?''

It seemed Kannabi hadn't just compiled ghost stories because she liked the occult---she'd researched the history of the shrine that served as the setting, too. I had zero interest in that side of things, so it was a huge help. Of course, how much history would end up tying into a Biology Club investigation was still an unknown.

The nue mummy was apparently beyond a small door from the room we were in. A sliding door---probably usually kept locked---had been thrown open, and beside it was a sign that read: ``Special Public Showing: Nue Mummy. Photography Strictly Prohibited.'' Beyond the doorway was pitch-black---even darker than this already dim room.

After finishing the first room, we went through the door with Iwama in front. It was like a haunted house. The air in the back room felt dusty, earthy, like mold could be growing.

The mummy was straight ahead. Inside a large wooden box, yellowed cloth was packed in tightly, and in the center something dark lay there. There were candles set on the left and right---ones that hadn't even been set out for Fudo Myoo---and their small flames were the only light sources.

The four of us stood in a line facing it. No one said a word.

The mummy, about the length of a human forearm, was wrapped in dark brown cloth. It was almost stick-like, like a thick rush roll, but its head was swollen and round, and what looked like hands and feet jutted out in one direction. From the cloth, I could see claws---huge and sharp, far too big for the body. It really might be tigerlike. A tail extended in the opposite direction from the limbs, as long as the torso, thin as a snake.

It was probably made from some kind of animal, at the very least. Excluding the tail, it looked close to fifty centimeters, about the size of a marten. But just from looking, there was no way to tell if it had started as a single animal, if it was stitched together from multiple animals, or if clay or something had been used to shape it.

``Yeah, it really is pretty thin.''

Mizasaki murmured. Just like the info we'd gotten in advance.

As if she'd been expecting more, Kannabi let out a disappointed sound.

``You can barely see anything. It's almost entirely cloth.''

``You can see the claws, though\dots\ wow. They're huge and sharp---like a bear's. Maybe it's a carnivore?''

``Maybe it really is made with Asiatic black bear claws. The body looks like it's from the marten family, but the claws are way too big for that.''

``So it's a chimera, then. A marten's torso with bear claws.''

``Yeah. Maybe they put it together before it dried.''

``It's real.''

A raspy voice came from right nearby, and all four of us almost jumped out of our skin.

``A real nue that the gyounin-sama has subdued on our behalf.''

The owner of the voice was an old man sitting on a folding chair beside the mummy. He was probably a guard, but he'd been in the shadow of a pillar that was a complete blind spot from the candlelight, so we hadn't noticed him at all.

Embarrassed that we'd been making biological observations in front of someone who apparently believed in the nue, we bowed our heads and hurriedly took our leave.

After that, we walked around the grounds to get a feel for the layout while thinking about where we should investigate at night.

From the first torii facing the road to the second torii leading into the central area, there would probably be food stalls and plenty of foot traffic, so we decided to avoid that for now. We figured it would be better either beyond the second torii, or along the ``Chinju no Mori Hiking Course'' that stretched toward the mountain from beside the first torii.

A little after ten in the morning, it was still too early for lunch. We decided to save the hiking course for the afternoon, and for now, we tried the path that led from the third torii beside the main hall toward the inner sanctuary deep in the mountains.

It was a gentle uphill slope surrounded by massive cedar trees that had probably been planted hundreds of years ago. It was a mountain path, but it was maintained, and maybe because people actually walked it, the dirt underfoot was packed down, firm and easy to walk on. The trail was wide, so it would probably feel safe even at night. Of course, if this weren't the kind of sacrilegious investigation where we were trying to witness the paranormal firsthand, I wouldn't want to be walking somewhere like this at night.

``Doesn't look like there's any lighting. We'll have to make sure we bring flashlights.''

Iwama looked like she fully intended to comb this place over.

``Looks like the tengu phenomenon is mainly seen near the shrine buildings and around big trees. This fits the conditions.''

Kannabi added that. So we really did have no choice but to walk this path.

``It looks like it's a pretty good distance to the inner sanctuary.''

``It should be about a kilometer. Since it's a mountain trail, the climb will probably take around thirty minutes.''

Walking while we talked, my breathing started to pick up. Sure enough, Kannabi---who had the least stamina---started wheezing and panting. She was saying something like, ``This is a special breathing technique,'' but we weren't on a schedule where we had to rush, so we took breaks little by little as we headed for the inner sanctuary.

In the end, we reached it just before eleven. The last stretch of the climb was pretty brutal, so it worked out to more than forty minutes total.

``This is the inner sanctuary\dots?''

At the end of the trail, Mizasaki tilted his head.

``Inner sanctuary\dots\ more like inner, I guess.''

He wasn't wrong. Two old-looking stone torii stood in a row at the end of the path, but unbelievably, there was nothing beyond them. The view opened up cleanly, and all there was was a deep mountain forest. If I had to point to anything, I could see that the forest across the way had been cleared and covered with a huge array of solar panels. There was no way a mega-solar farm was what people were worshipping.

When I walked through the torii, I found that just beyond it was a sheer cliff, several meters high. The stone torii were apparently set atop a massive rock formation. That explained the view. I didn't feel like falling, so I quickly backed away. It was clear nothing had ever been built on the other side of the torii.

Iwama marched right up to the cliff's edge and peered down below, curiosity written all over her face.

``What's going on with this, I wonder. What were they enshrining here?''

``About the inner sanctuary\dots\ there isn't\dots\ much information\dots\ online, so\dots\ we'll have to\dots\ properly look into\dots\ the local historical materials\dots''

``Don't push yourself to talk.''

When I said that to Kannabi, who was barely catching her breath, she straightened her back for some reason.

``I'm not\dots\ pushing myself. Who do you\dots\ think I am?''

\textit{Who are you, exactly?}

By the time we got to the soba shop we'd agreed on, it was 11:55. We'd made it in time for the ``meet five minutes early,'' but the vice-principal was already inside, planted heavily at a six-person table as he waited for us.

Vice-principal Nihitsu was a balding, slightly overweight older man. Yesterday, I'd gone with Hongo-senpai to greet him and ask for his cooperation, and that's when I realized I'd actually met him once before. When Kannabi and I went to the staff room to get copies of a report, he'd been the one who kindly helped us.

At school he'd been in a dress shirt, but today was a day off---even if he was supervising the Biology Club---so he was in casual clothes. Over a beige long-sleeve shirt, he was wearing an orange fishing vest, like he was about to go fishing. The kind with way too many pockets.

``G-go ahead and sit. No need to be stiff about it.''

According to Hongo-senpai, the vice-principal was a fairly sharp Tsunanagai High School alumnus. I wasn't well-versed in Kansai dialect, but the way he spoke sounded a bit Kyoto-ish. His catchphrase was ``kamahen, kamahen,'' and apparently he was a kind person who let most things slide.

By the way, ``akan, akan'' was supposedly his sign for ``you can make it work if you push,'' and ``I'll hold on to my end'' was his sign for ``absolutely not.'' Just that part was reversed, so watch out, senpai whispered in my ear.

Once we sat down, the vice-principal immediately called over a server.

``Everyone okay with shrimp? If soba doesn't work for you, we can do rice bowls instead. If you're fine, let's go ahead and order. Excuse me---five tempura soba sets. Any large portions? Normal is fine? I might go large. How about you kids? Ah, you're normal. Then one large.''

He placed the order with a brisk, rhythmic patter, then slurped his soba tea.

``Let me properly introduce myself. I'm Nihitsu, the vice-principal. I'm the guy who always sits next to the principal. I don't really teach, so I don't have much involvement with students, but I do things like stamp paperwork and wipe asses. Here, I'll give you my business card---if anything happens, call me anytime.''

He pulled a thick business card holder from his fishing vest pocket. After handing each of us a Tsunanagai High School business card, he leaned back, looking relieved.

``Alright. My job's done. You all do whatever you like for three days. If you're in trouble, just tell me and I'll help. I'm the supervisor on paper, so I'll be nearby.''

Iwama's eyes went wide.

``Are you sure\dots?''

``Kamihen, kamihen. Our school motto is jigyo jiritsu jigyo jitoku. When it comes to the jigyo jitoku part, my job is to give you as soft a landing as possible. If you die, I can't undo it, so I do want you to be careful about safety. Hahahaha.''

\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note:} The vice-principal quotes the school motto as「自業自律・自業自得」(jigy\=o jiritsu, jigyo jitoku). It's a punny pairing: 自業自得 is a common idiom meaning ``you reap what you sow,'' while 自業自律 swaps in ``self-discipline/self-governance'' (not a fixed idiom in the same way) to emphasize independence and personal responsibility. He then riffs on the motto jokingly, framing his role as helping students ``land softly'' so their self-directed freedom doesn't turn into the bad kind of ``you reap what you sow.''}

I'd seen hints of it at school, but he was a pretty rough-and-ready type.

``I'm sorry to bother you on your day off. How is your wife doing?''

He just laughed and fluttered a hand at my concern.

``It's just a cold. I'm a Showa-era man, you see---I've neglected the home front so much my missus tells me not to stick around and nurse her, and to just stay out of the house.''

After that, we didn't talk about anything particularly official. The five of us ate ten-seiro soba (large portions for everyone except Kannabi), argued about the mysterious wild vegetable in the tempura, and the vice-principal agreed with my suggestion---that it was probably shioide---bringing the lunch gathering to a close.

For the record, shioide is a perennial plant in the Smilax family that looks like a long, skinny asparagus spear. The taste is pretty close to asparagus, but once you start thinking about it, you start feeling like actual asparagus tastes better, so if you don't think about asparagus, you can enjoy it just fine.

In the afternoon, we decided to start by walking the hiking course that ran alongside the shrine. Since the main stage of our investigation was inside the shrine grounds, the goal here was mainly to check the surrounding environment.

Sunset today was around 18:30. To make sure our night investigation lined up with that, we'd asked the inn to serve dinner at the earliest slot, 17:00. Which meant our most important mission was somehow dealing with how stuffed we were from ten-seiro before then.

Thankfully---or so you could say---the hiking course was a mountain path with some intense ups and downs. We walked the roughly five-kilometer course, stopping now and then to check the vegetation.

This area was a south-facing slope with plenty of sun. There were plenty of evergreen broadleaf trees like kashi and shii. Even in spring, those trees kept their leaves thick and full, and because the leaves were heavy, the forest floor stayed fairly dark even during the day. Of course, it wasn't only that---where deciduous trees like nara and kaede mixed in, it was comparatively bright, and in dry rocky areas where pines grew sparsely, you could see the sky so clearly it was almost blinding.

Since I'd left the shrine explanations to Kannabi, this time I moved us along while explaining the tree species.

``What is this, a fried shrimp?''

While we were resting on a rocky spot with a good view, Mizasaki picked up something from the ground. On his palm sat a small, weathered object that really did look like a fried shrimp.

``An old fried shrimp.''

``Do fried shrimp even get old?''

The fact that I'd had the same kind of thought as Kannabi, and that Mizasaki tossed out a reasonably accurate retort, hit me for double damage.

``Isn't it a pinecone? I've heard it ends up looking like that when squirrels eat it.''

``Oh, right! So this tail part is the top, and the rest got gnawed off. Sasuga, Gamma-san---sasugamma.''

Mizasaki said something that made no sense, looked around, and came back with a perfectly intact pinecone. Compared side by side, it was obvious. If you stripped off most of the pinecone's scales, it ended up that fried-shrimp shape. Squirrels ate the seed part protected by the scales.

``But we haven't seen any squirrels.''

When I pointed that out, Iwama immediately offered an explanation.

``Pinecones ripen in autumn or winter, right? This looks a little old, too---so maybe they're somewhere else right now.''

That tracked.

``The easiest way to interpret paranormal phenomena scientifically is to blame animals. If we find squirrels, we might be able to say weird noises and stuff are because of them.''

``Sasuga, Delta. Then I'll do my best to find animals.''

Mizasaki might look the way he does, but his sight and hearing were both pretty sharp. I ignored the ``sasuga, Delta'' part and left the animal search to him. Iwama slipped the old fried shrimp into a zip bag she'd brought along, and we started walking again.

I tried listening, too, but all I could really make out was birdsong and the whir of insects' wings---animal footsteps were hard to catch. So I kept focusing on plants.

``Huh, this is\dots?''

Iwama suddenly stopped and picked up a leaf from the ground. Even though it was a fallen leaf, it still looked green. I came closer to take a look.

``That's probably arakashi.''

``Whoa, Delchi---you can tell species all the way down to that?''

``Well, once you get used to it.''

But that wasn't the point. The leaf Iwama had picked up had a big hole right in the middle.

``Bug damage?''

``If it is, that'd have to be a huge bug.''

Kannabi and Mizasaki leaned in from the side, too. Iwama gave a small ``Hmm.''

``This might be the first time I've seen feeding marks like this. It also looks kind of torn, so I think it's probably an animal, but\dots\ how do you tear out just the middle?''

``Maybe it was folded in half.''

I picked up an arakashi leaf that had fallen at my feet and folded it along the midrib down the center, then tore out the middle section. When I unfolded it, it made the same donut shape as the one Iwama was holding.

``Got it! There really might be a fold line!''

``This one too, maybe?''

Mizasaki picked up another leaf. This one was missing its upper half, but it had the same kind of marks---like the center portion had been cut out. The outline looked symmetrical left to right.

After that, the four of us searched the area and gathered more of the same. We found a whole bunch. The most interesting one was a leaf Iwama found---there was a hole right in the dead center shaped like a four-leaf clover. Because it was perfectly symmetrical, it really backed up the idea that something had folded the leaf and then gnawed it.

We also found other signs---twigs that looked like only the new buds had been gnawed off, leaves that had been eaten down to just the midrib, and branches with bark scraped off. We saved representative examples in zip bags.

Before we knew it, the sun had started to dip, so we decided it was about time to head out of the mountains.

Dinner was in the inn's dining hall. Since we'd arrived ten minutes early, they let us in ahead of time, and we were the first ones there. In a large, wood-floored room, black-lacquered tables were scattered here and there, and a spread of dishes had already been set out. It felt exactly like a hot-spring inn. By the time we found our seats, an elderly couple in yukata and others had started coming in, and being there in sportswear---fully geared up to go investigate---made me feel a little guilty.

Maybe because we were early, we didn't see anyone from the Physics Club or Chemistry Club yet. We thanked the heavens that there wasn't any tempura on the menu, then finished dinner first. The portions were generous, but it was a light Japanese meal centered on salt-grilled yamame and a chicken hotpot, and the fact that we'd worked hard walking around that afternoon helped---we managed to finish it all without trouble. (Kannabi got quite a bit of help from Mizasaki.)

Without running into any of the other science club people, we finished getting ready and left the inn at 18:00.

Even for us, it was clearly deep in the mountains. Outside, it had turned cold before we'd realized it. It was still before sunset, but the sun was already hidden behind the western mountains, out of sight. Under a sky still holding onto the last light of dusk, darkness was starting to drape itself over the forest.

As we walked from the inn toward the shrine, the noisy buzz of a crowd gradually reached us. The town itself was dark, but with the festival going on, the area around the torii was bright, and people had gathered there.

``Oh---smells like yakisoba. Now I'm getting hungry.''

Even Iwama couldn't agree with Mizasaki on that one. \textit{What were you doing just a minute ago?}

``Hold it in until tomorrow.''

On the second day, we weren't eating dinner at the inn---we were planning to eat somewhere outside. When we'd made the reservation, they'd asked, ``Every year, Tsunanagai High School usually doesn't have dinner on the second night---what would you like to do?'' and we'd decided to match that. It was easier to investigate if we could move dinner around flexibly.

We went in through the first torii and made our way along the approach, avoiding the clusters around the food stalls. There were so many people gathered---men and women, young and old---that it was hard to believe this many had existed in a place this deep in the mountains. Iwama took the lead, I followed behind her, and then Mizasaki and Kannabi came after that. When I glanced back, I saw Mizasaki whispering something into Kannabi's ear while opening and closing his right fist. Kannabi nodded seriously. \textit{I had a bad feeling about this.}

After climbing the stone steps and passing under the second torii, the shrine's central area was dim and quiet. There were no streetlights or anything like that. A modest light---like a candle flame---glowed inside red-painted metal lanterns. The atmosphere flipped completely from the lively bustle below the steps; it felt like we'd stepped into another world. People were walking here and there, but everyone spoke in hushed voices.

We moved off to the side and decided to do a quick pre-investigation huddle near one of the lanterns.

According to the report, the phenomena from the ghost story had been witnessed around the shrine buildings and near a huge cedar tree. In other words, either the central area where we were now, or beyond the third torii.

``It's going to get darker than I expected. We should probably avoid splitting up, shouldn't we?''

Mizasaki suggested it. Since we were dealing with an unknown phenomenon, we'd been planning to split up in the central area first to raise the odds of observing something. Beyond the third torii was a mountain trail, so from a risk-management perspective, we'd planned to go there with multiple people depending on how things looked.

``True. With this dark, it might get a little dicey.''

Kannabi agreed. \textit{Even at this point, I'd been thinking something felt off.} Iwama said innocently,

``Then let's split into pairs. If all four of us stay together, the chance of missing something gets higher.''

``Yeah. So let's split into two teams and explore around here first---if something seems likely, we keep going as-is. If nothing seems likely, one team goes beyond the third torii and checks it out. Sound good?''

``Mm! Delchi and Rei, is that fine with you?''

Iwama asked before I could pin down what exactly felt wrong, so I nodded too.

``Alright, let's rock-paper-scissors to split.''

At Mizasaki's suggestion, the three of them held out one hand. The instant I saw that, I understood everything---but it was already too late. Even if I wanted to resist, there was no way to get out of this situation without looking suspicious. So I reluctantly held out my right hand.

``Rock-paper-scissors---split up!''

At Mizasaki's prompt, I threw rock. Mizasaki and Kannabi also threw rock. Iwama threw paper.

``Split up!''

I kept throwing rock. The other three threw paper.

``Split up!''

The tempo sped up. In that short span, I couldn't read Mizasaki's strategy in time and threw rock again. Mizasaki and Kannabi threw paper. That was it---I realized too late, and because Iwama threw rock, the teams were decided.

``Alright. Then it's me with Nabi-san, and Delta with Gamma-san.''

``Got it! Nice to work with you!''

Iwama flashed me a ``see that smile?'' look, and all I could do was nod.

Mizasaki's trick was simple. He'd conspired with Kannabi. They'd decided to alternate.

Two of them throwing rock, two of them throwing paper. Unless Iwama or I threw something different and ended it, it would go on forever. And that ending pattern happened with a one-in-four chance. The strategy was to land that one-in-four before we caught on---good odds.

All of it was to pair Iwama with me.

\textit{Ever since the sakura incident, and the way home on the day of the spirited-away case, they hadn't changed at all.}

It was probably all part of the plan---that I'd decide it wasn't worth objecting and making things awkward. And they were right. In the end, I wound up going around the shrine grounds with Iwama, just the two of us.

For now, we set 18:30 as our rendezvous time and split up. I ended up alone with Iwama with no mental preparation at all. \textit{No, this was just an investigation---nothing that should require the kind of preparation I was thinking of}---but ever since Mizasaki had gone and said extra stuff, like how this was a test of courage at a shrine at night, I couldn't quite stay calm. The grounds kept getting darker and darker.

``Hey, Delchi---did you notice? Rei and Mizasaki-kun were throwing the same hand.''

\textit{Iwama was quick, so of course she'd seen through it too.} I pretended I hadn't.

``Were they? I wasn't really thinking that hard about it.''

``Do those two get along that well? Then we can't lose, either---we've gotta keep up!''

\textit{On top of a wrong theory, she'd reached an even wronger conclusion,} and the best I could manage was:

``Yeah. I don't feel like we can lose.''

\textit{What kind of competition is that supposed to be, exactly?}

``\dots This is fun.''

As we walked around, Iwama murmured it under her breath. It might've been something that slipped out before she could stop it, because she added---like she was a little embarrassed---

``I've always wanted to do an investigation like this. With people my age, who share the same hobby.''

``It's probably a little different from going somewhere with parents or teachers supervising.''

“Yeah! This is what youth feels like, doesn’t it?”

Did it? I didn’t really understand what that word meant in the first place, so all I could do was nod vaguely. Still, at least Iwama looked like she was having a good time.

We still hadn’t turned on our flashlights. It would bother other visitors, and if we were shining beams everywhere, no animals—or paranormal phenomena—would come near us anyway. The sky still held a faint light, so we could manage to see where we were going. But a shrine ringed by forest already had pockets of deep darkness. Under the cedars, it felt like midnight. They really weren’t kidding when they called it twilight—if there were an old man standing a short distance away with no face, I probably wouldn’t notice.

It was gradually getting colder. I hunched my shoulders and let myself shiver a little.

“Del-chan, you okay?”

Maybe I’d been acting suspicious, because Iwama looked worried. I averted my eyes.

“I’m totally fine.”

“Don’t tell me you’re bad with the dark?”

“No, I’m totally fine.”

“Mizasaki-kun said, like, you’re kind of a scaredy-cat for someone so surprisingly nice…”

“I’m not, at all. That’s just his nonsense.”

“He told me people who are scared start saying ‘totally’ a lot.”

I replayed what I’d just said and realized I’d been saying “totally” like crazy. That bastard—figures, after being together since elementary school, he knew me way too well.

“I’m not exactly scared. I just get uneasy in the dark because you can’t take in information—it’s animal instinct, a law of nature. Th— I mean, it’s not like I believe in ghosts or anything.”

“Got it.”

Someone might ask what a wallflower has to complain about the dark. But shade and darkness were different, even if they looked the same. Shade was a place the sun didn’t reach, not a place without sun. Sure, you could call night the Earth’s vast shadow, but that wasn’t what I meant.

“Iwama, you’re not scared of the dark?”

When I asked, Iwama nodded with a bright smile.

“Yeah. Totally.”

It was a “totally” from the heart—clearly different from mine.

“Have you always been like that?”


“Hm… I might’ve been scared when I was little, but… once I realized fear doesn’t really mean anything, I stopped being scared.”

I looked at Iwama, not really understanding.

“Doesn’t mean anything?”

“Yeah. The right kind of fear—like for risk management—matters. But if fear won’t improve the outcome, there’s no point in it, right? If anything, it’s better to keep moving than to freeze up out of fear.”

“Logically… I guess you’re not wrong.”

What she was saying was correct, but it amounted to forcing your emotions to submit to reason. Figures—someone who lived by science had a tough, resilient soul.

“You know, I think there are two kinds of people who can look out at the ocean with someone.”

In the middle of the mountains, what was she suddenly on about? Iwama took a breath, then continued.

“The kind who says, ‘Let’s go to the ocean,’ and the kind who goes with them all the way there.”

“I guess that’s true by definition.”

It felt like the most obvious thing in the world, but I didn’t say that out loud.

“I’m probably the first kind. If you’re going to be the one who proposes it, I think it’s better if you can take on things without being scared—stuff like that.”

“I see. That’s a good mindset.”

I couldn’t help being impressed. This was probably what you called leadership. The talent to be the one who says, “Let’s go to the ocean.” “The one who proposes it” and “leadership” even sounded kind of similar. Either way, it was something I, a scaredy-cat, completely lacked.

As we wandered through the dim shrine grounds with no particular destination, I spoke just to fill the silence.

“I’m the second kind for sure. It’s easy as long as you’re just following someone.”

“Is it?”

“...Was there really room to question that?”

“Like, if it was during a typhoon and I said I absolutely had to go see the ocean—would it really be easy to decide to come with me?”

“That’s dangerous. The ocean in a typhoon—you shouldn’t go.”

“But if I insisted and wouldn’t listen, I think you’re the kind of person who’d come with me anyway, reluctantly.”

“Well… I can’t exactly let you go alone.”

“Right? And that’s not easy. If nobody comes with you, it’s no different from going to see the ocean alone. You can only look out at the ocean with someone because they come with you all the way. The person who comes with you to the ocean is actually the more important one.”

Why were we even talking about the ocean? As I wondered that, I remembered when we’d hopped onto the bus together. That day, we really did see the ocean. This was Iwama’s way of making a comparison.

“Then I’ll work on building the strength to go with someone all the way to the ocean.”

When I said that, Iwama smiled happily.

The sun had already disappeared beyond the mountains, and the last light was slowly draining from the sky. The boundary between evening and night slid westward, and overhead was a dark, washed-out purple.

The birds had already gone quiet, and there was no sign of animals. It was a perfectly peaceful twilight.

And then—suddenly, it happened.

Right beside us, something fell with a pararara sound, and I started so hard I nearly jumped. I didn’t jump, but I almost lunged toward Iwama. My heart jumped into my throat. I pressed a hand to my chest as my heart started pounding, and I searched for where the sound had come from.

It seemed like it had come from the cedar.

I signaled to Iwama: Did you hear that just now?

Iwama nodded back. As expected, she didn’t look scared at all.

Something had dropped.

From the cedar—and from high up, too, judging by how big it was.

Even when I looked up, all I could make out of the cedar was its thick, cone-shaped outline. Inside that outline was pure darkness. Still, the sound we’d heard was hard to interpret as anything other than a lot of small things raining down from above.

Iwama quickly pulled out her flashlight and aimed it into the cedar’s interior.

“I’m turning the light on!”

At her warning, I lowered my gaze. I didn’t want to stare straight at the light and ruin the dark adaptation my eyes had finally achieved. A moment later, the ground glowed red. Keeping a hand on my glasses, I slowly raised my eyes—and the beam from Iwama’s flashlight was a vivid red.

It was a desperate workaround for a dilemma you run into when studying animals at night: you can’t see your target without a light, but if you use one, you startle it. Many nocturnal animals evolved to make out things in the dark, but the trade-off is that they’ve sacrificed color vision. Their sensitivity to red light, in particular, is thought to be low. The color red rarely occurs naturally at night, after all. That’s why you use red lights to avoid startling nocturnal animals as much as possible.

Iwama had brought four red lights from home and handed them out to us. I had one in my pocket too, but for now, we’d searched with just the light she was using.

The huge cedar had no branches up to a certain height, and above that, countless branches stretched out like a monster's tentacles. The red beam lit those tentacles, and as Iwama's hand shifted slightly, the black shadows writhed and swayed in an eerie, wiggling motion.

``Do you see anything?''

\textit{My eyesight wasn't that great. Iwama should've been seeing better than me.}

``Mmh\dots\ let's look a little more!''

Shining the light at our feet, Iwama circled to the far side of the trunk. I shifted over with her.

The boundary between the shrine grounds and the forest was vague. Without realizing it, the firm soil under my shoes had changed to the soft feel of piled cedar needles. Just in case, I used the red light I'd borrowed from Iwama to sweep the ground around us. Nothing looked dangerous. No snakes, at least.

We circled the cedar together for a while, but in the end, we came up empty.

``Let's record which tree it was. So we can come back.''

``Yeah. This is near the third torii, so\dots\ is there anything we can use as a landmark?''

``I'm logging the rough coordinates on my GPS. If we take a photo of its shape, we should be able to identify it later.''

``Okay---then I'll take it. From true south.''

``Got it. I'll note it down.''

I copied the coordinates from the GPS I'd borrowed from my dad into my phone's notes, and underneath I wrote: ``Photo taken by Iwama from true south.'' After a moment's thought, I added: ``Sound of multiple small objects falling.''

\textit{It matched perfectly with the legend about tengu throwing stones, but I didn't believe that kind of story, so I hesitated to record it using the word ``tengu.''}

I double-checked the ground, but there weren't any small stones lying around. That left the question of what had actually fallen, but whatever it was, it was too dark for us to find.

I sent a quick update in the group chat with Mizasaki and Kannabi, then we searched the area for a while to see if the same phenomenon would happen again. Iwama looked like she could barely contain her excitement.

``It really happened! The phenomenon from the report!''

Her voice was a whisper, but it bounced with energy.

``Yeah. That's a relief.''

``A relief?''

``Now, at minimum, all we have to do is figure out what this phenomenon actually is, and it'll take shape as research.''

\textit{That sounded like a lab boss giving advice to students.}

Iwama laughed as she said it. \textit{The comparison was pretty spot-on, but where had she even seen a ``lab boss who gives students advice''?}

``What do you think that sound was?''

When I asked, Iwama thought it over with a serious expression.

``I think it's definitely the sound of a lot of small, fairly hard objects---things with some weight---falling. Cedar cones are light and soft, and it's not like they'd fall all at once, so probably not that\dots\ So what, then? Like\dots\ maybe a squirrel dropped acorns it had stuffed in its cheeks?''

``I can't rule it out. Squirrels are mainly diurnal, but it wouldn't be strange for them to be active at dusk. And like when we found that Arakashi earlier, there should be plenty of acorn-bearing trees around here.''

``If we check under that tree during the day and find acorns, that'd be evidence.''

``Yeah.''

\textit{If the truth behind the tengu stones was ``a startled squirrel dropped its acorns,'' it'd feel a little anticlimactic---but it would make sense. More scientific than blaming a tengu, at least.}

By the time we met back up with Mizasaki and Kannabi, it was exactly 18:30, the time we'd set as our target.

``No way---nice catch!''

From the sound of it, Mizasaki and the others hadn't found anything at all. It couldn't be helped. If anything, you could say we were the ones who'd gotten absurdly lucky, running into an unknown phenomenon within the first thirty minutes. We reported what we'd experienced in detail.

Mizasaki and Kannabi had been pouring all their energy into getting me and Iwama paired up, but it looked like my worry about whether those two had managed things properly was needless. You couldn't underestimate Mizasaki's social skills---or the thoughtfulness he showed, especially around girls. Kannabi, on the other hand, must've been really scared of the shrine at night; she was standing there stiff-faced with no room to spare. Still, she seemed to be enjoying the investigation itself well enough.

``Our plan was to keep investigating here if we observed the phenomenon, but the sound came from the cedar tree, not the shrine building. If we're going to focus on the cedars, I think it'd be better to spend half our time on the far side of the third torii. What do you think?''

When I floated the idea, Iwama and Mizasaki agreed---while Kannabi made a sour face. Past the third torii was a mountain path with no lights at all. I didn't mean it as intimidation, but she was probably scared. \textit{Watching someone else be scared was kind of fun.}

``Then let's go---we'll do it. We've got the stamina.''

Iwama said it right away. I'd completely forgotten, but Kannabi got out of breath after walking even a little way up a mountain path.

``Oh---then can we leave it to you, Gamma? We'll keep checking over here.''

We could've suggested reshuffling at this point, but the mood wasn't there anymore. Iwama and I both nodded. After a quick exchange of information, the four of us split into two groups again.

After sunset, it didn't take long at all for the sky to go dark. More like, by the time I noticed, everything had already become irreversibly---inescapably---night.

The night sky peeking through the gaps between the trees was a little bright even without a moon, the branches like black cutouts. Probably city lights diffusing through the air somewhere. The stars glittered beyond that, refusing to be drowned out.

There was no wind. All I heard were the two of us---our footsteps pressing into the mountain path. When we stepped on fallen leaves or small branches, a sharp crack echoed loudly. The air that drifted around us was dense with the smell of earth.

As we entered the dark forest, my senses sharpened. A prickly unease ran across the surface of my skin. It wasn't that cold, but I was probably covered in goosebumps.

\textit{Put plainly, I was scared.}

For safety, I used a red light to illuminate our feet, but I also wanted to keep my eyes adjusted to the dark---and I didn't want to startle any animals---so that was the only light we used. The path was narrow, so we walked side by side, and Iwama kept close, careful not to drift too far from me. Close enough that I almost felt her body heat.

\textit{It had happened naturally because we were doing fieldwork at night, but if I thought about it, it was an abnormal situation. Two high schoolers---a boy and a girl---who'd met only two or three weeks ago, walking alone together through dark hills with no streetlights. If my little sister found out, she'd probably lecture me for hours about what it meant to be a Japanese man.}

Iwama must have trusted me. It should've been something to feel happy about, but even so, it made me tense. \textit{The mountain path was pitch-black---somehow it felt even more like being alone together than any sealed room.}

``Nothing's happening.''

When I muttered that, Iwama leaned in from right beside me, peering at my face. It was an expression I didn't see often---mischievous, with a faint, teasing smile.

``You want something to happen?''

``\dots I mean, yeah. We came out here at night on purpose because we're hoping some weird phenomenon will happen.''

``I hope something weird happens, then.''

\textit{If Mizasaki had said that, I would've snapped back, ``That phrasing is gonna get you in trouble,'' but Iwama probably meant it purely, so I didn't.}

``Can't wait.''

I answered with something that wouldn't quite count as sarcasm.

And then, before I'd even taken more than a few breaths, an unidentified rustling came from somewhere nearby---and I let out a scream, like I'd compressed ``GYOEH!'' into about 0.2 seconds.


``\dots You okay?''

``Totally---no\dots\ I mean, I'm just normally okay.''

``Really?''

``Yeah.''

``\dots What do you think it was? A mouse, maybe, or a little bird? It felt pretty small.''

``I thought it might've been something a bit bigger.''

``Mmm\dots\ maybe. It's so quiet out here that it's hard to tell how big a sound is.''

``Yeah\dots\ if you put it that way, it might've been small after all.''

I was grateful she'd let the scream slide---until Iwama suddenly stopped.

``It's okay, Del-chan. I'm here with you.''

``Yeah\dots\ no, I'm not scared at all, so you don't need to worry.''

I'd said ``totally'' again. \textit{Because of that idiot Mizasaki feeding Iwama extra ideas, it was getting harder and harder to deal with. Later, I was going to stop by a vending machine and bill him for one warm drink in damages.}

While I was mentally cursing Mizasaki, Iwama smiled softly, then quietly held out her hand.

``Want to try holding hands?''

``Hands\dots?''

``Hands.''

The offer was so sudden I ended up doing the world's stupidest back-and-forth, a full loop.

``No, that's kind of\dots\ too much.''

``No one's watching right now.''

Not even the moon was watching us. But was it really okay as long as nobody saw? The way Iwama said it so boldly made me think for a moment.

\fullpageimage{2.jpg}



\thispagestyle{plain}
\vspace{1cm}
``Is there any benefit to holding hands?''

``Why don't you try it and find out?''

Iwama's answer felt pretty unscientific for her, but my hand still moved slowly toward the one she'd offered me. \textit{Of course, this was purely scientific curiosity---to verify what kind of changes skin-to-skin contact with a non-blood relative would cause in the brain's neurotransmitters.}

``Then can I try it for just five seconds?''

``Yeah, that's fine.''

Even before we actually touched, my heart was already pounding harder than it had from being scared of the dark. \textit{If I focused only on heart rate and tried to analyze it, I could argue that something stronger than fear had drowned fear out. Of course, that line of reasoning only held if you assumed the magnitude of emotion correlated with your heart rate.}

Iwama's right hand and my left, overlapping while facing the same direction---\textit{it'd end up like that ``Balse'' thing}, I thought, pointlessly watching us from an outside perspective as I cautiously touched her hand.

The image my brain latched onto in that instant was a jungle somewhere far off in Southeast Asia. A climbing plant, a pitcher plant, with reddish-purple insect traps at the tips of its long, slender leaves. The mouth of the pitcher-shaped trap secreted nectar, luring insects in. The rim was built to be extremely slippery, so any bug that landed there would fall with no way to save itself---down into the bottom of the pitcher, where digestive fluid pooled.

When I somehow managed to drag back the soul that had been sucked out through my palm, I could finally feel the faint warmth of Iwama's hand.

``How is it?''

Her question snapped me back to reality. It had definitely been more than five seconds, so I let go.

``Yeah\dots\ it's a lot better.''

``Really? Thank goodness! Are you done already?''

``Yeah, I'm fine now.''

That was honest truth---the simple, childish feeling of being afraid of the dark had already vanished.

I turned the light on and moved ahead. \textit{If I didn't, she might see the pathetic look on my face while I was still rattled.}

It helped that the light was red. Just like a red sheet makes red letters disappear, red light makes red hard to distinguish. \textit{My ears were probably bright red from the heat, but in this light, you could barely tell.}

``Come to think of it, this light's really handy---and it's light. Where'd you buy it?''

When I abruptly tossed out a completely unrelated topic, Iwama answered in her usual cheerful voice---like she hadn't just held my hand at all.

``Well, I modified a zoom light from a hundred-yen shop. It's a light with a white LED built in, but I swapped it out for a red power LED I bought in Akihabara.''

``You made it?''

I was so shocked I blurted it out normally.

``No, I just modified it. I reused the product's switch, battery box, and resistor, desoldered it, and only replaced the LED part. If you start with a hundred-yen shop light, it's way cheaper and easier than buying parts and assembling it yourself.''

``Huh\dots''

It hadn't even occurred to me to take a product apart.

``Sticking cellophane on is the easiest way, but shorter wavelengths still get mixed in, and if you layer it to make the red stronger, it just gets darker and darker\dots\ And it looks bad, and I figured it might get all torn up when you're out and about.''

``So you really thought it through.''

Impressed by the unusually detailed explanation, I shone my phone's white light on Iwama's modified flashlight. From the outside, you couldn't tell there had been any work done at all. The LED seemed to be fitted into a green circuit board inside the lens that gathered the light. It was a brilliant piece of handiwork.

When I was about to say something like, ``That's amazing,'' and turned back toward Iwama, I realized my mistake.

The white light from my phone was clearly illuminating Iwama's face.

Her ears were dyed a vivid red---redder than I'd ever seen before.

We heard the eerie laughter on the way back, after reaching the double torii at the inner sanctuary and turning around to head back down.

A low, muffled snicker---\textit{gufufufu}---echoed out, and then partway through it shifted into a high, shrill cackle---\textit{kyahahaha}. It came from pretty high up, probably from somewhere in the cedar trees.

Tengu laughter---that phrase flashed through my mind.

``Let's record it.''

``Yeah. Our phones should work, right?''

Iwama and I nodded to each other, took out our phones, and started the recorder. With our phones held up and pointed away from us, the laughter rang out again from somewhere.

Got it!

I was about to send the audio file to Mizasaki and the others right away, but when I checked, we had no signal.

We hung around a bit, and by the time the laughter had stopped, I realized it was just about time for us to meet up. Iwama and I headed down the mountain, unable to contain our excitement.

``It's an animal cry, right?''

``Yeah. It came from higher up, so it's probably a bird or a small mammal.''

``And we have a recording---so we might be able to identify it.''

``Yeah. Let's compare it with the animals that live around here.''

Iwama looked genuinely happy as she held her fist out toward me.

``We did it!''

``Yeah, we did.''

I clenched my own fist and lightly tapped it against hers.

On top of the tengu tsubute, we'd even managed to observe something you could call a strange phenomenon---tengu laughter. For our very first night, it was almost too big a haul.

And that wasn't all.

When we regrouped with Mizasaki and Kannabi, they were excited too, and showed us something on a phone.

``Tengu tsubute! They said it came from over by the shrine building!''

It was a video. The camera was pointed at the shrine roof, but it was so dark I couldn't really tell what was happening. When I leaned in with Iwama to listen to the speaker, I heard a cute little scream---``Kyaa!''---followed by a rattling sound, \textit{katakatakata}.

``They said something like a little pebble probably fell onto the roof tiles.''

``Seriously? It sounded like a pebble?''

Kannabi answered Iwama, who was staring with sparkling eyes, staying calm to the end.
