``It did sound like a pebble to me too, but it's not like we actually confirmed what fell, so---''

Kannabi cut herself off there. In the last part of the video that had been playing nonstop on Mizasaki's phone, a very raw voice had been recorded going, ``Whatwhatwhat, scaryscaryscary.''

``Ah---sorry. I forgot to cut that out.''

Mizasaki apologized to Kannabi in a low voice. Looks like they'd been having plenty of fun on their end, too.

After that, I checked thoroughly to make sure there weren't any weird voices in my recording, then let the two of them listen to my recording.

``That's totally laughter---what is this, it's insane!''

``Doesn't seem like a squirrel\dots''

``When we get back, let's all look into it right away!''

``Just don't stay up too late.''

Anyway, we'd gotten a lot.

We'd successfully observed three anomalies---the cedar-tree tengu tsubute, the ``shrine-building tengu tsubute,'' and ``tengu laughter''---and for the latter two, we'd even obtained data as evidence.

Completely satisfied, we headed back to Senzawa-so.

When I went to the front desk to get the key, I remembered that the four of us were in one room. \textit{Come to think of it, we still had a trial waiting for us tonight.}

They told us the large communal bath was empty right now, so we split into men and women and went in. The hot spring---both the indoor bath and the open-air bath---should've been wonderful, but I couldn't focus, thinking about how I was going to be staying in the same room as the girls after this, even with a divider.

There was only one key, so I was the one responsible for it while we were bathing. The two of us finished early and went back to the room before the girls. Wearing yukata, we turned on the TV and waited---and right at the promised time, ten o'clock, Iwama and Kannabi came back from the bath.

It seemed they'd changed in the bathhouse too, because they were both in yukata as well.

``Oh---''

Mizasaki, who'd apparently been about to say something thoughtful, stopped mid-word for a moment, then said something safe to the girls,

``Welcome back.''

A yukata was basically loungewear, and combined with their damp post-bath hair, it made this feel like some kind of sleepover. \textit{I mean, it was a training camp, so it was technically a sleepover either way, but still---more than ever, it felt like there were fewer ``walls'' between us and them.}

Iwama had let her hair down. Without the ponytail that made her seem so lively, she somehow looked even more demure.

``That bath felt so good! Like, you could really feel the earth\dots\ There wasn't any sulfur, but there was this faint smell like soil or oil clay, and I could smell iron ions too.''

The moment she stepped into the room, Iwama started analyzing the hot spring's composition, completely oblivious to how we felt.

Mizasaki replied in a voice that was just a little higher than usual.

``Yeah, the open-air bath was like that. It was a sulfate spring with a lot of calcium and magnesium, right? There was a lot of sodium too. I licked it, and it was a little salty.''

``You licked it\dots?''

Kannabi looked at Mizasaki with eyes that were genuinely taken aback.

``It was free-flowing from the source, so I just tried a tiny bit from where it was pouring in.''

``He licks it every time we go to a hot spring. He's a pervert who even reads the analysis sheet all the way through.''

An analysis sheet was the paper they often posted in places like the changing room with the hot spring's data on it. Every time Mizasaki went into a hot spring, he'd study it in detail while he was stark naked, so I often pretended not to know him.

``Because it's interesting. They analyze it in that much detail---from ion concentration to pH---and you can verify it with your own tongue. There are hardly any hot springs where licking it would poison you. Looks like this open-air bath had arsenic and mercury, but it was still at a concentration that'd be totally fine to drink.''

He'd laugh at Karl-Will-something-or-other's folly and hesitate to drink azalea nectar, yet here he was licking unknown hot water that bubbled up from underground---what a ridiculous double standard.

``Oh, that sounds fun! Maybe I'll secretly try it too!''

I couldn't bring myself to tell the eager Iwama that she probably shouldn't do that.

\textit{Even though I absolutely thought she shouldn't.}

Even with a divider, we were spending the night in the same room as boys and girls---and the topic that got us fired up first was hot spring composition analysis. It felt a little unnatural, but maybe it was just adaptation: a way to look away from the abnormal situation of sharing a room.

The girls first set their things up on their side of the room, then came over to the guys' side. Mizasaki and I had checked thoroughly, so we hadn't left anything out that we didn't want seen. Mizasaki---being thoughtful---poured some local cider he'd bought at the souvenir shop into yunomi cups, and we drank it (it didn't taste especially unusual), then we sat in a circle on the tatami and had a meeting.

``Can I make a proposal first?''

For once, Kannabi raised her hand first.

``We woke up early today, and we walked around during the day too, so I think I might get sleepy partway through tonight\dots\ If we're going to do any research, can we do it tomorrow?''

``Yeah, I get that. After the bath, I'm kinda sleepy too. I might not be able to use my head much.''

Mizasaki immediately agreed. Both arguments made sense. Iwama nodded.

``Breakfast is at seven tomorrow, so if we want seven hours of sleep, it seems like we should go to bed around eleven tonight.''

``Then let's make lights-out at twenty-three hundred. Until then, we'll just go over tomorrow's plan. If you're sleepy, of course it's fine to get in the futon first.''

Everyone agreed with my suggestion.

``I think there are three big things we need to do tomorrow.''

Iwama held up three fingers.

``First and foremost, we interpret tonight's phenomena scientifically. On top of that, we look for evidence to support it. And finally, based on all that, we find some way to record the phenomena themselves at night.''

It was simple and sufficient. No one objected.

``Leave figuring out what the tengu laughter is to me. I'll look into it in my spare time.''

``Yeah, Del-chan might be the perfect person for that! I was thinking I'd look it up on my phone tonight too if I couldn't sleep---under the futon, with my earphones on.''

``Then we'll each do that. If we narrow it down to nocturnal birds and mammals, it shouldn't take that long. We should be able to get a good idea before we go out tomorrow.''

``So based on Delta and Gamma's analysis, we'll spend the day tomorrow collecting evidence, huh. I wonder if there's a nest. If it's a bird, maybe there'll be feathers on the ground?''

``If we search on social media, maybe we'll find records from birdwatchers or people who like taking photos.''

``Nice! Approaching it from both the site itself and data sounds like it'll be fun!''

``Alright! I'm starting to feel like this is gonna go well! Then tomorrow during the day we work like that, and from evening into night we think about how to record the weird phenomena.''

``Video would be best, but there's no guarantee it'll come out clean in the dark. If we use red flash, it might negatively affect the animals, so I think we'll basically be filming under red light. I want to think through where we'll lie in wait, how we'll light things, and how we'll frame the camera.''

``We should fully charge our phones and wait with multiple cameras set up.''

Maybe it was just what you'd expect from Tsunanagai High School---or maybe it was because we were fundamentally the same kind of people---but even with all four of us talking, the discussion never wandered, and it felt good to have the plan click into place so smoothly. \textit{If anything, I thought talking with just Mizasaki took way longer because we'd go off on tangents.}

By the time fatigue and sleepiness made it hard to focus, the clock pointed to twenty-three hundred.

After we finished getting ready for bed---brushing our teeth and going to the bathroom---we decided to turn the lights off.

``Alright. Good night!''

Iwama said that with a gentle smile, and Kannabi gave a small bow as well, and the two of them went in beyond the fusuma.

``Lights out.''

When Mizasaki clicked the switch, the room went pitch-dark.

I didn't know how things were set up in the girls' room, but I'd lost rock-paper-scissors with Mizasaki, so I was the one sleeping on the futon laid closest to the divider. When I crawled in and closed my eyes, I could hear rustling movement from beyond the fusuma.

\textit{It wasn't exactly an out-of-body experience, but my awareness drifted on its own, looking down at the room from above. Seen straight down from overhead, the vertical divider was almost invisible. The futons were laid in the same direction, and right next to me---at a distance no different from Mizasaki---either Iwama or Kannabi was there.}

\textit{There was no way I was going to sleep.}

``Alrighty.''

A pure voice came from extremely close by, and that settled it---my neighbor was Iwama. \textit{Why would you say ``alrighty'' when you're alone? If you hadn't said that, it wouldn't have become a fact that you were sleeping right next to me}---and while I was thinking something like an idiot's Schrödinger's cat, I let out a sigh.

``Ah.''

I heard a voice. With the timing, it could only have been Iwama reacting to my sigh.

Holding my breath, I stared at the fusuma. \textit{It felt like my gaze was meeting someone else's from the other side.}

By the way, this half of the four-person room was barely big enough for Mizasaki and me to lay out our futons. In fact, our comforters were slightly touching each other, and my comforter was pressed right up against the divider. It had to be the same on the girls' side---which meant I could assume Iwama's comforter was also pressed against the divider. Symmetry. Which meant Iwama was basically at the same distance as Mizasaki.

\textit{If the fusuma vanished right now---no. I shouldn't think about that.}

\textit{In the first place, the fusuma wasn't going to vanish. The law of conservation of mass guaranteed as much.}


\fullpageimage[angle=90]{3.jpg}


\thispagestyle{plain}
\vspace{1cm}

I pulled myself together, unplugged my phone from the charger, and unlocked it.

There was a ``1'' notification badge on the LINE icon. When I opened it, it was from Iwama.

---So close

Just those three characters. Not even a minute had passed since it was sent. \textit{While I was still wondering what kind of state of mind had made her send something like that,} the three-character message was unsent and disappeared.

---Sorry, it's nothing

A new message came right after. \textit{Thinking that she was probably in her futon too, with the chat screen open the same way I did, gave me a strange feeling.}

---Got it

After agonizing over it, I sent back just two characters, and it was read immediately. \textit{More than the ``Got it'' I'd typed, the little ``Read'' next to it felt like it carried far more information.}

\textit{You don't have to keep the chat open the whole time.}

\textit{No---neither do you,} I retorted to myself, and hurriedly closed LINE and opened my browser. I was pretty sure Suntory's site had a feature that let you search wild birds by their calls. I found the page I wanted. I figured I should tell Iwama too, just in case, and sent her the link---only to have it be read immediately again.

---Just in case

---Thanks!

\textit{If I kept this up, I'd look like a giddy high school couple flirting over messages. A proper Japanese man, yadda yadda---my little sister's scolding voice echoed in my head,} and I decided to focus on the call search. Once I put my earphones in, the sounds around me disappeared.

Herons, nightjars, cuckoos, scaly thrushes\dots\ I checked one after another, but none of them seemed to match.

The one thing I did find interesting was a note saying the scaly thrush, which cries in a mournful ``hee, hee,'' had once been called a nue because it sounded so eerie. That reminded me---when Kannabi had listed the nue's traits, she'd said, ``Its voice is a scaly thrush.''

Running into the nue while searching for tengu really was a strange story.

Now then---maybe I should look into mammal calls next, I thought, when---

From somewhere, I heard a strange sound: \textit{katakatakata}.

Had some video started playing in the background by accident?---I checked, but nothing like that was happening in the browser.

The unsettling \textit{katakatakata} continued---and it was getting louder.

No way.

When I cautiously took my earphones out, the odd noise immediately became clear. I stuck my face out from the futon.

From the pitch-black ceiling, hard sounds rained down.

It sounded like small stones were tapping against the roof.

% ==============================
%        Chapter 2–
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 2–\\
    The Writhing Stone Wall
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch2m}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 2–}
\vspace{1cm}

There was no way I could sleep---or so I'd thought, but it seemed I'd fallen asleep before I knew it.

I woke up to the sound of a door closing. Someone had apparently left the room. Mizasaki was sprawled out beside me, so it had to be one of the two girls---or maybe both. When I checked the clock, it was still only five-thirty.

I didn't feel like I could fall back asleep, so I left Mizasaki behind and slipped out of the room to go for a morning bath. Even though Mizasaki was asleep, he was still inside, so I left the door unlocked.

Our room was in a detached building, so to get to the large bath, I had to go through the main building first. Even after I crossed the covered corridor into the main building, the inn was still quiet. With a basket on my arm holding a bath towel and face towel, I padded along in slippers toward the bath.

``Morning, Delta-kun.''

I ran into Mikage Aya on the way. Like me, she was carrying a basket and wearing a yukata. \textit{Maybe it was because she'd just woken up, but her hair was down, her collar loose, and she looked strangely alluring}---so I avoided looking at her directly.

``Ah---yeah\dots\ morning. Morning bath?''

``Yeah. I woke up, and I'm done with what I needed to do.''

\fullpageimage{4.jpg}


\thispagestyle{plain}
\vspace{1cm}

``What you needed to do?''

``Getting up early and solving three problems is part of my daily routine.''

``That's way too admirable.''

Maybe because I'd only just woken up, I'd missed my chance to respond properly even though Mikage had said something pretty clever.

``I didn't see much of the Chemistry Club yesterday, but what do you do on a training camp?''

As if to fill the time on the way to the large bath, I asked, and Mikage answered with a somehow cheerful tone.

``A little ways down the mountain from here, there's an old cooking facility that isn't used anymore. We use that and run experiments all day. They give us a checklist with a hundred different reactions and phenomena listed out, and everyone works together to reproduce them ourselves.''

``Sounds fun. Reproducing things in a cooking facility---you mean stuff like the Maillard reaction and the Tyndall effect?''

``As expected. Both of those were on the list. Of course, we do more dangerous ones too.''

``Dangerous?''

``\dots\dots And as entertainment, there was also a game where we tried to figure out how the seniors' magic tricks worked. Like popping a big balloon from far away without touching it.''

It felt like she'd changed the subject, but I was more curious about the magic trick than that.

``Was it because they put limonene on the balloon? The stuff in lemon essential oil and the like. It has properties that dissolve rubber and styrofoam.''

``Correct.''

While we kept the chemistry talk going, we arrived at the large bath.

The men's and women's baths were split, the right side labeled ``Men: Seiryu no Yu,'' and the left side ``Women: Aozora no Yu.'' I'd been hoping---just a little---that they might switch sides between men and women so we'd get a different one than last night, but when I looked closely, the decorations around the entrances didn't look like they could be moved, so it seemed the layout was fixed like this.

``See you later.''

``Yeah, Hokatera.''

What the hell is ``hokatera,'' I thought to myself as I parted ways with Mikage.

There was only one pair of slippers in the men's bath. I'd been hoping we might get lucky and have it all to ourselves, but it looked like someone had beaten us to it. Well, it couldn't be helped. If it was just the two of us, we could probably have either the indoor bath or the open-air bath to ourselves.

I stripped, took off my glasses, covered my crotch with a face towel, and entered the bathing area.

The hot spring felt completely different at night versus in the morning.

Through the wall of glass windows that had looked like a dark mirror last night, I could see a vivid, fresh-green forest. Steam rose in slow, wavering strands off the indoor bath's surface, and in the morning light it was beautiful.

The person who'd gotten there first seemed to be soaking in the indoor bath and looking out at the scenery; I could only see the back of his head. Without my glasses, I couldn't be sure, but he didn't seem like anyone I knew, so I ignored him and headed for the showers.

At Senzawa-so, the indoor bath was a clear, colorless bicarbonate spring, while the open-air bath was a brown sulfate spring. Like Iwama and Mizasaki had mentioned yesterday, the open-air bath's mineral content was stronger, so I really wanted to start with the gentler indoor bath---but with someone in there already, it couldn't be helped. I'd go to the open-air bath first.

``Good morning. You're Izuta, aren't you?''

The first bather called out to me as I was about to head outside. It was a calm, unhurried voice I didn't recognize.

He got out of the bath and walked over to me. Even through my blurry vision, I could tell his wet hair was slicked straight back, and he was wearing glasses that were completely fogged up.

He seemed to be a student from Tsunanagai High School. Probably Chemistry Club or Physics Club.

``Yeah. I'm Izuta.''

Even as I stood there thrown off, he kept closing the distance until he was close enough to reach out and put a hand on my shoulder.

``Knew it. My name is Hijiri Mitsuyuki. I was in the same middle-school year as Kannabi-san, and I'm in the Physics Club. I've been wanting to talk to you for a long time. It's a pleasure.''

He spoke with a strange number of commas. Like a politician giving a speech.

And as if to match, Hijiri extended his right hand toward me like a politician. He was asking for a handshake. I didn't mind in principle, but unfortunately, we were in a bathhouse. We were both naked, and unlike me, Hijiri wasn't covering anything at all. As if proclaiming that there wasn't a single thing in his life to be ashamed of, he bared his well-trained body with complete confidence.

``\dots\dots I see. Nice to meet you.''

Please forgive me for sounding a little unenthusiastic for a first greeting.

When I took his hand, Hijiri gripped mine firmly---right up to the edge of pain, without crossing it. His fingers were long, and his hand was big. It felt like it had been optimized for handshakes.

``Since we're here anyway, if you have time, do you want to talk for a bit?''

I nodded at his suggestion. I'd been wanting to get into the indoor bath anyway, so it worked out perfectly. I lined up beside Hijiri, sank into the tub, and looked out at the bright morning mountain through the window.

Hijiri Mitsuyuki. The guy Mizasaki and Mikage had mentioned before.

A first-year in Class D who openly said he was aiming for Law, yet still joined the Physics Club---a prodigy. An ``exception case'' who, despite being a local, was the only one who ever broke through the ``outside talent'' recruited from out of prefecture---purely by entrance-exam score.

I remembered, belatedly, what Mikage-senpai in the Physics Club had once whispered to me.

---\textit{By the way, on the entrance exam, among the local science-track students, Delta-kun should've been the top score}

The ``science-track'' qualifier had obviously been there because Hijiri existed. Hijiri, who aimed for Law, was technically humanities by definition---even if he'd joined the Physics Club.

``Did you hear about me from Kannabi?''

``No. I hear about you a lot from Wari in the same Physics Club. Were you close back in middle school? He's always talking about you.''

It left a bitter taste in my mouth. A complete misunderstanding.

``We weren't that close. Our grades just happened to be similar, so everyone around us compared us on their own. He got swept up in it too, and I guess he sees me as his enemy.''

``I see. So you were good rivals.''

That was also a little off. \textit{Even if we'd been rivals, it wasn't a good thing.}

``Did he only tell you bad things about me? Well, take whatever he says with a grain of salt.''

``Using that kind of vocabulary to insult you is a feat you can't pull off unless you're close.''

So it really had been insults.

\textit{I couldn't stand the idea of talking about Watari on such a pleasant morning.} For a change of pace, I made a suggestion.

``Want to try the open-air bath? I want to breathe some outside air.''

``Sure. I haven't gone in the open-air bath yet either.''

By the way, using ``boku'' as your first-person pronoun in an equal relationship at this age was pretty rare. Even though his tone was gentle, there was a stubbornness---like a core of strength---I could feel in it.

When we went out to the open-air bath, I was surprised.

It had been too dark to see yesterday, but there was a huge waterfall off to the right. Or rather, it was a wide waterfall formed by an artificial sabo dam. A massive volume of river water flowing down from upstream hit the concrete drop and spread out like a curtain as it fell. The deep, roaring bass was soothing to the ears.

When I took a deep breath, the damp early-morning forest air filled my lungs.

``Nice view.''

``Morning baths really are worth trying.''

The two of us sank shoulder-deep into the ocher-brown cloudy water. The temperature was on the lukewarm side, and with the cool breeze brushing my face, it felt like I could stay in there forever.

``So what is the Biology Club doing on this camp?''

While I stared blankly at the waterfall, Hijiri asked.

``We're verifying the contents of an old investigation report. We're trying to scientifically uncover the truth behind the tengu legend, and that so-called nue mummy.''

``Sounds interesting. A science club investigating legends. Kannabi-san would love that.''

Apparently, he knew Kannabi's tastes fairly well. \textit{I found myself wondering what kind of relationship the brilliant Hijiri and the gloomy, shadow-dwelling Kannabi had back in middle school, but asking outright felt tactless,} so I went with a safer question instead.

``What's the Physics Club doing?''

``That's interesting too. They handed us a list with a hundred physical phenomena and effects and laws written on it, and told us to actually visualize them---and record ourselves explaining them on video.''

``Huh. Like, for example?''

``For the basic ones, things like the law of conservation of momentum and Fleming's left-hand rule. For ones I'd never heard of, the Leidenfrost effect and the Coand\u{a} effect. And beyond that, everything from something `everyday' like dust explosions to something like nuclear fusion that doesn't seem remotely achievable.''

Dust explosions being ``everyday'' was debatable, but I nodded along anyway.

``That's insane. There are really a hundred of those?''

``I was surprised too. I could list them all right now, but I'd probably wear my mouth out this early in the morning.''

``You can't possibly say all of them.''

``Really? \dots Maybe you're right. If you're curious, I'll show you the list later. The Physics Club booked a gym in a place called Jagasawa---about five minutes by car from here. We're bringing in everything from enamel wire to liquid nitrogen and running experiments.''

``That's incredible\dots''

This time, I meant it. As expected of Tsunanagai High School's Physics Club---they operated on a different level. \textit{Maybe someone really was trying to build a fusion reactor in the mountains.}

``The upperclassmen are genuinely on another level too. Some of them can produce the exact physical phenomenon they're aiming for just by eyeballing it---and do it precisely. I couldn't believe we were using the same Homo sapiens brain.''

If Hijiri---the ``exception case''---said so, it had to be true. \textit{If there were gals who could manipulate humans like puppets, then having someone who could manipulate physical phenomena didn't seem impossible.}

``Huh?''

Hijiri looked toward the forest, like he'd noticed something. Unfortunately, without my glasses, I had no idea what he was reacting to.

``What is it? Something there?''

``No. Well\dots\ wow. That ghost story was real.''

``Ghost story?''

``The one about the writhing stone wall. Haven't you heard it?''

``Yeah, I think a friend mentioned something like that in passing\dots\ but I don't really know the details.''

``I heard it from an upperclassman. There's supposed to be a cursed stone embankment near Senzawa-so. A whole lot of people who were involved in its construction died, and their grudges try to crawl back into this world---making a stone wall that shouldn't move, writhe and squirm. That kind of ghost story.''

``\dots And you saw it?''

``Yeah. That sabo dam---over there---it looked like it was writhing.''

``That can't be possible.''

``I thought so too. But once you've actually seen it, you can't deny it.''

He didn't sound like he was joking. \textit{I squinted, but all I could make out was a blurred landscape.}

Just then, from beyond a wooden wall, I heard a door open with a thud. Then came a soft splash, someone sinking into the water, and finally a restrained, feminine sneeze---\textit{kushun}.

So the women's open-air bath must be on the other side of that wall.

``Kannabi-san?''

Hijiri called out toward the wall.

``Huh\dots\ Hijiri-kun?''

The voice that came back was unmistakably Kannabi's. \textit{How did he know from just a small sneeze?}

``Morning. You came for a morning bath too, Kannabi-san.''

``Yeah. Morning. Something kind of startling happened last night, so I couldn't really sleep. I didn't want to bother everyone, so I thought I'd wake myself up.''

``Something startling?''

``There were sounds from the ceiling. I'm pretty sure it was footsteps---some animal with hard claws---but since I knew the tengu legend about pebbles falling on the roof, I started thinking\dots\ maybe\dots''

``You've always liked scary stories, but you've also always been really scared of them.''

``Let me be. It's because I'm easily scared that I want to know the truth behind scary stories.''

Kannabi's tone was a little different from usual. It felt natural---like she'd let her guard down---and that heavy, gloomy edge wasn't there. \textit{Was this how you talked with an old friend?}

\textit{I'd missed my chance to announce myself, and I felt awful that it had turned into accidental eavesdropping. But it was tricky: If I boldly declared, Izuta Sho here, it would also mean telling her I'd heard everything up to now.}

I figured retreating was the best move, so I started to get out of the bath. But heaven wouldn't allow it---immediately blasting a cloud of lethal fine particles straight into my nasal cavity.

Cedar pollen. I couldn't hold it back; a huge sneeze burst out of me.

As if in response, there was a loud splash from the women's bath.

``And---Delta\dots?''

\textit{How did she know from one sneeze?}

``\dots Diving.''

``Diving?''

``Just now I was doing a dive in the open-air bath, and the instant I surfaced, cedar pollen took me out. Looks like there's wind today, so it might be flying around pretty hard.''

I was trying to imitate Kannabi's specialty---throwaway lies---but had my intent gotten across?

\textit{I'll pretend I didn't hear what you two were just talking about, so relax. It'd help if you also act on that premise.} That was what I meant.

``By the way, Kannabi-san---there's something I've been wondering\dots\ Have you heard the ghost story about the writhing stone wall?''

I didn't know if it counted as a save, but Hijiri changed the subject for me.

``\dots I have, but what about it?''

Hijiri explained what he'd just told me. A question came back immediately.

``Can you see the waterfall from over there?''

``Huh---can't you see it from the women's bath?''

``I can't. Maybe because the privacy wall is high---I can only see the forest. I can hear the water, though.''

``I see. So the women's side is designed so it's harder to peek in from outside. Over here, you can even see an old-looking suspension bridge hanging deeper in the valley.''

``Hm. Moments like this make me a little jealous of boys.''

After murmuring that, she must've remembered I was here, because Kannabi hurriedly changed topics.

``That thing you saw is probably\dots\ motion aftereffect.''

``After\dots what?''

``Aftereffect---as in, an effect that lingers. There's even an alias that's basically just `waterfall illusion.' That might be what caused it.''

I heard a door open and close with a thud on the women's side. Someone had come out to the open-air bath.

``D---''

She sounded like she was about to say something, then fell silent for a moment, and continued in a lower voice.

``When you stare at something moving steadily in one direction for a long time, your brain's visual processing forms a kind of `adaptation' to that motion. In other words, if you keep watching water flowing downward, your sensitivity to downward motion decreases. So when you then shift your eyes to something stationary---''

``Relatively speaking, it looks like upward motion is happening. That's what it is.''

``Come to think of it, I've had something similar happen in a movie theater.''

``Ah---right. During the end credits.''

While Hijiri and I were admiring that, Kannabi had gone oddly quiet. I said it anyway.

``Still, it's surprising that you're so knowledgeable about visual illusions, Kannabi.''

``It's more psychology than anything. Kannabi-san is impressive. And in that area, it's not just knowledge---she can actually put it into practice.''

``Huh. Put it into practice?''

Just as I leaned in, another voice suddenly came from beyond the wall.

``That sounds interesting. What kinds of things can you do, Kannabi-san?''

It was Mikage Aya.

\textit{I wish you'd told us you were there,} I thought---then remembered I was the last person with any right to complain.

By the way, what had ``D---'' been supposed to mean?


% ==============================
%        Fragment
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Fragment\\
    Pride of Youth
\end{center}
\label{sec:fragPride}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Fragment}
\vspace{1cm}

At Sunamizu Middle School, the one I attended, there wasn't a single person who didn't know Hijiri Mitsuyuki.

But maybe I was the only one who knew what he was really like---though that's probably arrogant to assume.

I ended up in the same class as Hijiri-kun in our second year.

Hijiri-kun was a genuine prodigy. Academically, he kept up a best-in-a-generation record that had the teachers calling him extraordinary; he'd apparently been begged to join the soccer team and played as their ace; and on top of that, he served as student council president. He was so extraordinary it almost looped back around to feeling ordinary---like it was just part of the scenery.

Everyone wondered why a genius like that was stuck in a small-town public middle school.

He was the exact opposite of me---someone who quietly faded into that same small-town public middle school without drawing anyone's attention. Normally, aside from that one incident, there shouldn't have been a single reason for him and me to have anything to do with each other.

That assumption fell apart at the end of the summer of my second year of middle school.

One unremarkable day after school, I went to the student council room to submit paperwork for the Astronomy Club I was in. ``Belonged to'' might be the wrong phrasing. I was the club president---and the only member.

When I opened the door, Hijiri-kun was inside alone. He sat in an office chair with his back to me, absorbed in a paperback. Even from behind, I knew it was him by his immaculate slicked-back hair.

Maybe because I was so quiet, Hijiri-kun didn't seem to notice I'd come in, and he kept reading. So I walked closer, trying to call out without raising my voice. Without meaning to, I ended up creeping along on tiptoe---maybe because some troublesome curiosity was stirring in the back of my mind.

What kind of book was the school's top genius reading?

When I leaned in, what caught my eye wasn't text---it was an illustration of a girl in a swimsuit.

By the time I realized this was bad, it was already too late. Hijiri-kun noticed me and turned around.

``Ah, Kannabi-san. What is it?''

With the book still open, he asked calmly.

Wasn't he even going to hide it? Doing my best to avert my gaze, I held out a single sheet of paper.

``Um--- I'm here to submit some paperwork\dots''

``I see. A budget proposal for the cultural festival, right? Thanks. I've received it.''

He put the document into a file, returned to his original posture with quiet movements, and resumed reading.

When I cautiously stole another look at his hands, he was right in the middle of turning a page. A bold line of text leapt into view:

``Don't stare, you pervert!''

Using a larger font size to convey the force of a line like that was, in its own way, a clever trick.

In stark contrast to the content, Hijiri-kun's expression was dead serious---like he was reading an introductory macroeconomics book.

I quietly showed what I'd witnessed to the deepest part of my mind and decided to leave the student council room. I quietly turned around and started walking toward the exit.

``\dots Kannabi-san.''

Hijiri-kun's gentle voice stopped me in my tracks.

I'd seen an unexpectedly bizarre side of someone so perfect it was almost frightening. What was going to happen to me now?


I hesitantly turned back and faced Hijiri-kun, who wore his usual faint smile. Still sitting upright, he'd rotated his office chair to face me.

``Kannabi-san, have you ever heard of something called a lion's pride?''

I simply shook my head.

``I see. That makes sense. From a human perspective, lion ecology is quite grotesque. It's not something people generally bring up.''

He let out a regretful sigh. With no idea what he was talking about, and no way to escape this room, I had no choice but to keep the conversation going. Awkwardly, I asked,

``W-what is it? A pride?''

``A pride is, simply put, a group of lions. A group of related females accepts one---or a few---unrelated males, and that's how a pride is formed.''

``\dots I see?''

``The leader is male. The leader forms relationships, equally, with all the females except the cubs. The male cubs that are born will eventually be driven out of the pride, and they'll go on to aim for leadership positions in other prides.''

In a tone that made it sound like he was explaining the structure of the Diet, Hijiri-kun spoke gently.

``This is a remarkably rational system---designed to avoid inbreeding. Males move between groups made up of females. That prevents the bloodline of a group from becoming too concentrated over long stretches of time. But that's not what I want to emphasize.''

When I gave a small nod, Hijiri-kun rose smoothly to his feet and held the paperback out to me.

``The truth is, this book is a novel built around that kind of pride. The title is Pride of Youth. People call it `Plaus' for short. It's a light novel series that even got an anime adaptation.''

I accepted the book, its cover a deep crimson.

``Take the book cover off. There's a blurb on the obi.''

He told me that in a calm voice, so I braced myself and revealed the actual cover.

``\dots\dots\dots\dots\dots''

I froze for a while. I was trying to organize my thoughts---and failing.

On the cover illustration, a slightly pathetic-looking boy was surrounded by three beautiful girls fawning over him. And on the obi, in round, bubbly lettering, it said:

\textbf{A tsundere, a klutzy girl, a glasses girl}

\textbf{I'll befriend three beautiful sisters and build my pride!}

It was such a puny ambition that even Luffy or Satoshi would be left speechless. Whoever wrote that obi copy was clearly phoning it in.

I put the book cover back on and returned the book. Hijiri-kun accepted it with a beaming smile.

``So? Sounds interesting, right?''

Was he for real? It was hard to believe this was coming from the same person our Japanese teacher had showered with praise for his book report on Natsume Soseki's \textit{Kokoro}.

Even so, my intuition told me he meant it. Besides, he wasn't some two-bit clown who'd pull a joke like this on a plain girl like me.

``I-I think the theme is\dots\ unique. Interesting.''

At the best compliment I could squeeze out, Hijiri-kun nodded happily.

``Yeah. It's really unique. If you're going to talk about prides, I would've liked it to go into more detail about how it differs from a real pride---but still, it has this distinctive thematic core you wouldn't catch at first glance, and I think it's more than enjoyable.''

Hijiri-kun's hand stroked the book cover with near-affection. That was when I knew. He was a pervert.

After that, by sheer momentum, I found myself promising Hijiri-kun I'd read the ``Plaus'' series. I figured it was the kind of thing he'd forget by the next day, but instead, the very next day he lent me the first volume of ``Plaus,'' the one he'd already finished. At that point, I couldn't exactly not read it.

I was genuinely shocked to see the heroine's underwear in the full-color opening illustration, but as I kept reading, it was surprisingly entertaining. Not only did it portray the protagonist's inner life with a kind of beastlike intensity, but between the ruthless strategies he devised to win, the meticulous structure of the overall story, and the cuteness of the heroines, it really did have an appeal you wouldn't grasp until you read it.

That said, it was just a straight-up harem series.

Even so, the reason I got hooked on ``Plaus'' was probably because Hijiri-kun's way of explaining it---so convincingly, so authoritatively---poisoned me.

After that, Hijiri-kun and I fell into an utterly bizarre relationship: the two of us would tear through harem light novels and then trade opinions about them. Yet outside the times we were coolly analyzing a story's appeal and debating it, Hijiri-kun remained the same perfect superhuman he'd always been. Thinking, \textit{So this is what it means to have boundaries}, I enjoyed this new kind of relationship.

But then exam season arrived. For Hijiri-kun, maybe it was different---but for me, I finally lost even the slightest bandwidth to enjoy novels, and our strange relationship faded away\dots

The first time I heard from Hijiri-kun again in ages was on the day Tsunanagai High School posted its acceptance list.

\begin{quote}
Kannabi-san, I heard you got in.

Congratulations---seriously!

I knew you'd pass. I really did.

So we're going to the same high school.

I'm truly happy we'll be attending the same school again.

Tsunanagai High School has the best environment in the whole prefecture.

I hope we can both make these three years count.

I'm praying you'll be able to take a new step forward in your new environment.

I want to challenge myself in all kinds of ways, too.

I hear Tsunanagai High School is big on clubs as well.

If you find something that feels right, you should absolutely join.

Getting inspired by excellent peers will be a catalyst for huge growth.

Oh, right---Kannabi-san, you're planning to go into the sciences, aren't you?

You said physics wasn't your strong suit, so what are you thinking for your science subject choices?

It might be good to think about that early, too.

\dots Maybe that wasn't something I should be saying right after you got accepted.

Anyway, let's enjoy these next three years to the fullest.

See you at Tsunanagai High School!
\end{quote}

He still sent long, email-like messages on LINE. I couldn't help laughing at how the message assumed he'd gotten in too, but honestly, there probably wasn't a single person at Sunamizu Middle School who thought he might fail. I didn't, either.

That nostalgic feeling washed over me. Even though it was basically a sermon that could read as condescending, I didn't feel even a hint of irritation. Hijiri-kun had always been like this.

A person who, with ridiculous seriousness, would always talk about ridiculously serious things with complete sincerity.

Still lying on my bed, I reread it a few times, then slowly typed my reply.


% ==============================
%        Chapter 3+
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 3+\\
    The Homology Between Nue and Tengu
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch3p}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 3+}
\vspace{1cm}

Breakfast was a buffet.

There were so many options it was hard to choose---from Western staples like scrambled eggs and sausages to Japanese fare like grilled salmon and natto---but that was the least of our concerns right now.

Thanks to the one clue Iwama had found, all four of us were buzzing.

Musasabi.

It's a nocturnal mammal in the order Rodentia, classified among squirrels.
It has a gliding membrane between its hands and feet, which it spreads to soar.
With its long, fluffy tail and big, round eyes, it's a ridiculously cute little animal.

The call Iwama had found online was a perfect match for the laughter-like sound we'd heard yesterday.

``I feel like we can answer most of yesterday's questions with the musasabi. Scientifically!''

Iwama said, happily cramming her cheeks full of raw vegetables.

``Sounds like today's gonna be busy. Should we start by building hypotheses back at the inn?''

After making that suggestion, Mizasaki polished off his third hot-spring egg in one smooth gulp.

``Now that we know what it is, it doesn't seem impossible to get to the bottom of the strange nighttime phenomena.''

Even Kannabi, for once, seemed eager as she took a small bite of white rice topped with kombu tsukudani.

I replaced what I'd sweated out in the long bath with milk and said,

``Let's get moving right away.''

No one objected.

We finished breakfast quickly and, once we were ready to go, left our room and decided to hold our meeting in the lobby lounge of Senzawasou.
We sat on the sofa around a small round glass table.

Iwama set a loose-leaf notebook on the table and took out a mechanical pencil.

{\bfseries

Yesterday's questions (in chronological order)

\textcircled{1} What is the true identity of the nue mummy?

\textcircled{2} What animal caused the ``shrimp-fry'' pine cones and the holes in the Japanese evergreen oak leaves?

\textcircled{3} What is the true identity of the ``tengu pebble-throwing'' we heard under the cedar tree?

\textcircled{4} What is the true identity of the ``tengu pebble-throwing'' we heard at the shrine building?

\textcircled{5} What is the true identity of the ``tengu laughter'' we heard near the Okunoin?

\textcircled{6} What is the true identity of the ``tengu pebble-throwing'' we heard in our room at the inn?

\textcircled{7} Does the ``tengu toppling'' described in the legend and the report from fifty years ago actually exist? If so, what is it?
}

Sometime before then, she'd already prepared all of this---the agenda was neatly compiled on the loose-leaf page.

Once we'd finished looking it over, Iwama leaned in.

``Does it look like we missed anything? If it's good, then based on what we know so far---and whatever literature research we can reasonably do---we'll build hypotheses one by one, and today, while we test them, we'll also gather whatever information we're still missing.''

``No objections.''

At that, Mizasaki and Kannabi nodded too.

``Okay---starting with the obvious, I think it's safe to say \textcircled{5}, the tengu laughter, was a musasabi. That should be clear if we compare yesterday's recording with musasabi call recordings.''

Iwama wrote ``$\rightarrow$ musasabi call!'' on the \textcircled{5} line of the loose-leaf page.

Then Kannabi raised a hand slightly.

``Just to be thorough, a lot of the tengu laughter legends say, `If you laugh back, it laughs back at you.' So if we have time tonight, I think we should check whether a musasabi responds to our laughter.''

``I see. That would strengthen the musasabi-equals-tengu-laughter theory.''

``Nice! Then let's add that as a check.''

Iwama jotted a note: ``※Check whether it laughs back.''

``Next---can I take this one? I found something about \textcircled{2}.''

I pulled up a relevant article on my phone and explained as I showed it to the other three.

``This is an investigation report by a university professor in Tokyo. It's a blog, but the author is a professional researcher. He explains here that pine cones that look like shrimp fry, and broadleaf leaves with only the center gnawed out, are characteristic feeding traces of a musasabi. I also found photos of it folding a leaf and eating it.''

When I scrolled, photo after photo popped up of the exact same things we'd collected yesterday.

When I searched online for musasabi feeding traces, I found this article almost immediately.

``Ugh\dots\ so yesterday, the clue was basically shoved in our faces.''

``We figured it was a squirrel and totally ignored it. We should've tried searching.''

While Mizasaki and Iwama stewed, Kannabi kept staring at my phone.

``Is there something that doesn't add up?''

``No. It's not that. I'm wondering why the musasabi only eats the middle of the leaf.''

``Good question.''

That was more in the realm of plant ecology.

I wasn't particularly knowledgeable about the finer points of animal ecology, but when it came to plants, I knew a fair amount.

``Plants' natural enemies are insects and herbivores that eat them, and fungi that rot them. Plants can't run away, so they carry defensive compounds to protect themselves from them. Tannins in tea are one example---they bind to proteins and inhibit their function.''

``Pine resin's like that too, right?''

Iwama added, as expected of her. I nodded and continued.

``Most likely, Japanese evergreen oak leaves have more of those defensive compounds around the edges. Insects often start chewing leaves from the perimeter. So to avoid that, the best option is to fold the leaf and eat only the middle. Of course, that's still just a hypothesis.''

``I see. Musasabi are smart.''

I was glad that seemed to satisfy her.

``Apparently, musasabi feeding traces aren't limited to pine cones and leaves. Things like small branches stripped of bark to eat the cambium layer, or acorns gnawed down pretty aggressively. Today, keeping that in mind, let's search for as many musasabi feeding traces as we can, and record where they're most abundant.''

Iwama added to the \textcircled{2} line: ``$\rightarrow$ musasabi feeding traces! ※Want to look for other traces too, if possible.''

``Next---can I go?''

Kannabi volunteered, and the other three nodded. Kannabi continued.

``For \textcircled{4}---the tengu pebble-throwing we heard at the shrine building---I think it might've been the sound of musasabi footsteps. This is just my personal impression, but that sound was a little too heavy for a squirrel. Like something hard clicking against roof tiles.''

``Yeah. It's hard to tell from the recording, but yeah---it was a sound I'd never heard before.''

As Iwama listened and gave small nods, she tapped her phone like she'd had a flash of insight.

``\dots Hey, look!''

A photo of a musasabi's feet was on the screen. I immediately saw what Iwama was getting at. At the ends of its long, monkey-like fingers were hooked claws that were no joke---even compared to a bear's.

``Whoa\dots\ it's got a cute face, but those are some brutal claws\dots''

``Musasabi are herbivores, aren't they? Why would they need claws like those?''

Now that she said it, she was right. If it hunted, that would be one thing---but no, that wasn't it.

``Gliding. Musasabi travel by gliding from tree to tree. When they land, they have to cling firmly to the trunk. That's probably why they need long, sharp claws that can bite into bark.''

``Del-chan, you're amazing---yeah, that totally makes sense!''

I checked several sites that seemed reliable, and I found multiple statements linking claws and gliding. For now, this explanation would suffice.

Mizasaki raised his hand.

``Then, like---there's no recording, but maybe the sound we heard in the room for \textcircled{6} was a musasabi too. I doubt roof-tile sounds would carry all the way into the room, but if a musasabi got into the attic, it could explain it.''

``Yeah. We're still at the hypothesis stage, so let's think about it that way!''

Iwama drew a line from \textcircled{4} to \textcircled{6} and wrote: ``Musasabi footsteps! ※Photograph the actual animal?''

Things were progressing so smoothly it was almost satisfying, and now only \textcircled{1}, \textcircled{3}, and \textcircled{7} remained.

``For \textcircled{3}---the tengu pebble-throwing under the cedar---I have an idea, but\dots''

Iwama zeroed in on the point I'd intentionally left unsaid. When I prompted her to continue, she showed me her phone.

``Read this part!''

It was an explanatory article about musasabi from a visitor center site.

\begin{quote}
Musasabi build nests in trees and even in wooden buildings, but it seems they often use holes that form in large trees---``tree cavities''---as nests. If you want to observe them, start by looking for tree cavities. Because they often poop when they leave the nest, droppings under the tree can also be a clue. They're small, round droppings---about the size of black peppercorns. If you crumble them, you can see they're made of plant fibers.

Musasabi are nocturnal. About thirty minutes after sunset, they emerge from the nest, and before sunrise, they return. They're active for one to two hours after sunset, so if you want to see them gliding, aim for that window. It depends on the region, but June and December are breeding seasons, when their activity increases. The young are born about seventy days after mating, and about seventy days after that, they leave the nest---so four to five months after the breeding season, during spring's fresh greenery or autumn's season of plenty, you may be able to observe parent and child together.
\end{quote}

``And this has something to do with the tengu pebble-throwing?''

Mizasaki still didn't seem to follow. Kannabi murmured,

``If they poop when they leave the nest, then\dots''

``Ah, I get it---so the tengu pebble-throwing under the tree wasn't footsteps, it was poop!''

``Yeah---that's what I think! Apparently musasabi drop a whole bunch of little droppings all at once. If they hit the leaves, wouldn't it sound like pebbles falling? And this theory also explains why tengu pebble-throwing happens more under big trees, right?''

I nodded. That was the crucial point.

``The tree cavities musasabi prefer for nests form in the trunks of large trees. After all, it's the scars where branches have fallen off, or the nest holes woodpeckers make, that turn into tree cavities over time.''

``Holy crap, it all lines up!''

``Right? You don't get something this satisfying very often!''

Seeing Mizasaki and Iwama bounce around with such delight, I couldn't help but smile. One of the great things about ecology is that if you think it through logically, even without being a specialist, it's easy to feel that thrill of discovery.

Iwama wrote on the \textcircled{3} line: ``$\rightarrow$ musasabi droppings! ※Photograph the actual animal?''

\textit{Even as it felt like things were going a little too well,} I tried to reason it out.

``To survive, musasabi would need both---large trees to build nests in, and broadleaf trees for food. Surrounded by broadleaf forest, with cedars planted and the forests protected while still leaving big trees standing, the environment around Tsunanagai Shrine might've been pretty convenient for musasabi.''

``And they say they even build nests in wooden buildings, so maybe it's inevitable that so many tengu ghost stories crop up around shrines!''

Framed that way, it was pretty interesting. You could say the bond between shrines and tengu took shape here because human activity and musasabi ecology happened to mesh so well.

``Hey\dots\ I know this sounds crazy, but\dots''

Kannabi cut in.

``Could the nue mummy be explained by musasabi too? That mummy had sharp claws. And the size might match, too, don't you think?''

``Yeah, yeah---this has to be it!''

It was an uncharacteristically forceful conclusion from Mizasaki, but with the pieces fitting together this neatly, I couldn't blame him for thinking that way. When we saw the nue mummy, I did remember thinking its claws were huge. It didn't seem unlikely, either.

Iwama said,

``Then let's take another look at the mummy and confirm. We can't take pictures, but we should be able to compare it to photos of musasabi claws. If it turns out even the nue---not the tengu---was a musasabi, that'd be really interesting!''

On the \textcircled{1} line, she added: ``$\rightarrow$ musasabi? ※Look again to confirm.''

That left \textcircled{7}, the tengu toppling, but since we hadn't experienced it ourselves, we decided to put it off for later.

``They say something bad happens if you learn all seven mysteries, so\dots''

Kannabi was saying things like that, but we probably didn't need to worry too much about it.

``Haha, I see. Good, good. You're eating out tonight too, right? I'll be eating here at the inn. Tonight's wagyu shabu-shabu, apparently. My wife went ahead and booked it and everything. Don't stay out too late---be careful.''

When we ran into Vice-principal Nihitsu after breakfast and gave him a quick progress report, just in case, he brushed it off lightly. He seemed more distracted by the footsteps coming from upstairs.

``Is something wrong?''

When Iwama asked the vice-principal, staring up at the ceiling, he just shrugged.

``Well now, last night, when I was having a drink by myself, I heard footsteps---like a weasel or something---so it startled me and I spilled my hard-earned booze. Sobered me right up, and my yukata reeked of alcohol---so I've been nursing a grudge against the insolent beast.''

``Ah, that might've been a musasabi. We heard footsteps in our room too.''

At Mizasaki's explanation, the vice-principal's eyes widened.

``In that case, I can't very well hold a grudge. It's a valuable research subject, after all. Still---this isn't a weasel or a musasabi. That sound is someone in slippers. The Chemistry Club and the Physics Club will be coming down any minute. Why don't you head out early, so you don't run into them?''

Saying, ``Have something tasty like beef skewers for dinner,'' the vice-principal handed us four thousand-yen bills, then waved off our attempts to refuse with ``It's fine, it's fine,'' and sent us off like he was chasing us out.

After that, our investigation moved along with surprising smoothness.

First, when we looked at the detached annex we were staying in from outside, we found what looked like a gnawed hole under the eaves. It was clearly an animal's work. When we examined it with binoculars, we saw brown, coarse hair caught on the frayed edge of the hole. Some beast was going in and out of it.

Under the huge cedar where Iwama and I had heard the `tengu pebble-throwing', we found musasabi droppings, just like we'd read. If we stepped back a little, we could also see a tree cavity that was likely serving as a nest.

At the worship hall where Mizasaki and Kannabi said they'd heard `tengu pebble-throwing', we found a small hole that had likely been gnawed open in the wall near the roof---just like the detached annex at Senzawasou. And beneath it, as if on cue, there were droppings as well. According to Mizasaki, they were still fresh, so we could reasonably infer that last night's strange noises were almost certainly caused by musasabi.

And when we revisited the treasure hall and faced the nue mummy again, it became clear that its claws matched musasabi claws exactly. Its body length, not counting the tail, was about forty centimeters. Since it was wrapped round and round in cloth, we couldn't make out anything but its limbs, but nothing contradicted the idea that it was simply a musasabi.

``You're very diligent.''

A hoarse voice called out to us from the darkness as we observed the mummy and tried to keep our excitement in check. It was the old man who'd been sitting there yesterday, too. I didn't sense any sarcasm in his tone.

``We're researching the nue legend.''

Iwama answered with care. \textit{She was smart enough not to add ``Scientifically!'' at times like this.}

``I see, I see. You're students from Tsunanagai High School, aren't you? I haven't seen much of you lately, so I worried you might've lost interest.''

As I wondered what he meant, Kannabi immediately asked,

``Do you know something about the nue mummy?''

I'd always had the impression Kannabi was shy around strangers, so I was surprised to hear her ask so proactively. The old man, as if he'd been waiting for it, rose unsteadily to his feet, stood beside us, and gazed at the mummy.

``It's said that this nue was subdued in the Edo period by Gyounin-sama. Gyounin-sama was one of the ascetics who lived in this land. I've heard his character was wonderful as well, but above all, he was exceptionally skilled with a bow---and he slew the nue that had been troubling the people of the settlement.''

Then the old man, with unsteady steps, showed us illustrated diagrams. The room housing the mummy was almost entirely dark, but many old paintings hung on the walls.

In the faint, shaky light cast by the old man's small flashlight, we viewed them.

They claimed nue had existed since the Heian period. The oldest ones were reproductions, but framed illustrations of nue, drawn from the Heian period through the Azuchi-Momoyama period, were lined up in a row. Perhaps because it was said to have been exterminated in the Edo period, there were none after that.

A bright red monkey face, a shaggy raccoon dog body, well-muscled tiger limbs, and a long, slender snake tail. Some even had a snake's head on the end of the tail, baring fangs. None of them looked even remotely like a musasabi---they all had the terrifying form of a chimera. \textit{I couldn't come up with a good explanation for why long ago had equated an adorable musasabi with that bizarre monster.}

Last, the old man showed us a painting of Gyounin-sama, who was said to have exterminated the nue.

``\dots!''

The instant it was lit up, I almost made a sound. The other three couldn't hide their unease either. \textit{What is this? Seriously---this went beyond a bad joke.}

The ``Gyounin-sama'' in a fine kesa had a skull for a face, its eye sockets sunken into pitch-black hollows.

We left the treasure hall and figured it was time for an early lunch, so we went into a small Chinese diner in town.

No one mentioned the creepy painting. While eating an absurdly huge extra-large fried rice, Mizasaki said,

``The problem is whether that mummy is all a musasabi, right? We're sure the hands and feet are musasabi, but the rest is wrapped in cloth so we can't tell.''

The other three all had ramen. The menu just said ``ramen,'' but it was a simple, light shoyu ramen. After swallowing a mouthful of soft, curly noodles, I spoke.

``If we really want to know the truth, it comes down to either dissecting it, or doing a CT scan. Assuming we can get the shrine's permission, of course.''

``I'm satisfied just knowing the visible claws are musasabi claws. That alone is a big find.''

``Yeah. But I'd still be curious. That mummy was weirdly skinny, wasn't it? Even if it was just a musasabi, I wonder if it would end up that shape---and if they used things other than musasabi to recreate a nue, why didn't they try harder to make it look like one?''


After that, Mizasaki slurped his noodles. Saying the three of us besides Mizasaki ``ordered ramen'' might've been a little misleading. Mizasaki had ordered a fried rice-and-half-ramen set---and on top of that, he'd made the fried rice extra-large. He'd even hesitated over whether to do a large ramen with a half fried rice set, or just order one ramen and one fried rice outright. Either way, it was too much food---but that was normal for him.

``I looked up the etymology of musasabi,'' Kannabi said quietly.

``According to one theory, it changed from misasabi. Their body looks stocky, but it only looks that way because of the gliding membrane---apparently the torso is actually quite slender.''

``Whoa, seriously!?''

Mizasaki got excited with his cheeks full of fried rice. Like that, he was liable to spray grains everywhere, so I continued for him.

``Then the theory that it was simply a musasabi, mummified as-is, looks strong. I don't know how much of the tradition is true, but it's natural to think a musasabi that people believed was a nue got killed and turned into a mummy.''

``Musasabi are nocturnal, and back then they wouldn't have had flashlights, so maybe people at the time didn't actually see real musasabi all that often.''

Iwama's explanation was persuasive. With flames and washi paper, you couldn't create a strong, directional beam of light. Observing nocturnal animals must've been a lot harder than it is today. \textit{I figured that line of reasoning was enough,} but Mizasaki still looked dissatisfied, tilting his head.

``But like---why did they think a musasabi was a nue in the first place? They don't look alike at all. The only thing they have in common is that they fly at night.''

``Is that so?''

It was the same faint discomfort I'd had too, but Kannabi countered with a calm question.

``Can you describe the defining features of a nue, Sakkii?''

\textit{Who the hell was Sakkii?} When I thought about it, the only match was Mizasaki. \textit{When did you two get close enough to call him that?}

``Uh\dots\ what was it, again? Monkey face, raccoon dog body, tiger limbs, snake tail?''

``Yes. Now, keep that in mind and look at this photo.''

Kannabi pulled a tablet from her backpack and turned it toward us. On the screen was a full-body photo of a musasabi.

``What are the key features of a musasabi's face?''

At Kannabi's question, Mizasaki thought for a moment.

``First off, it's cute. Big eyes. And it's got a white pattern that outlines the face.''

``Big eyes because it's nocturnal, right! And since it glides between trees, it also has forward-facing eyes suited for stereoscopic vision---which is unusual for a herbivore. That's a feature too, I think.''

Nodding at Iwama's addition, Kannabi summarized.

``Face outline, fairly large, round eyes that face forward. Is it really that strange to liken that to a monkey?''

No way. No\dots\ she was right.

\textit{Annoyingly enough, it felt like scales fell from my eyes. I'd seen musasabi photos countless times, but I'd never noticed. But once she said it, it was the only thing that made sense.}

``Amazing! Hey---doesn't it all match? That stocky silhouette when it folds up its gliding membrane, and that dark-brown body fur. Those big, sharp, hooked claws for landing on trees. And that long tail---almost as long as its body---used to control its direction in flight. Those are raccoon dog, tiger, and snake!''

I had no words. If it matched this perfectly, it couldn't be a coincidence. That every phrase describing the nue's appearance contained musasabi traits---It wasn't just the tengu.

The true identity of the nue was musasabi too.

``Hogee\dots\ we were way too hung up on those chimera-like paintings. If you just think about it as words, it really can be explained this cleanly, huh.''

``In the Edo period, especially when it came to rare things, plenty of pictures were drawn based on nothing but hearsay. Even Perry---just hearing that his eyes were blue, people painted the whites of his eyes blue instead of his irises, didn't they?''

``Then it's not implausible that, back then, people simply thought musasabi were nue.''

Kannabi nodded with a satisfied smile, then put away her tablet, pleased.

That afternoon, we decided to walk around the shrine area again, partly to collect feeding traces. The plan was to raise our odds of successful night filming even a little: identify places where musasabi seemed likely to appear while it was still light out, and, while we were at it, run a simulation of how we'd film them.

But as we walked the mountain path, our main topic was the relationship between nue and tengu.

``Why are there two kinds---nue and tengu?''

That question from Kannabi---who looked unusually serious---was our starting point.

``Why\dots? Is it not allowed to have both?''

When Mizasaki asked, Kannabi gave a small shake of her head.

``It's not that it isn't allowed, of course. But think about it. In a culture where the nue legend was already being told, is there really room for tengu to slip in?''

To everyone but Kannabi, it wasn't very clear what she was getting at. Iwama thought for a moment.

``You mean they overlap as characters?''

``Something like that\dots\ character, or the root of it.''

That's when it clicked for me.

``Kannabi is saying nue and tengu are homologous. Trace them back far enough, and they're both the same musasabi.''

``Homolo\dots\ homologous\dots\ maybe?''

This time my point didn't seem to land, so I explained.

``Homology is the opposite of analogy. A common example: an insect's wings, which come from an exoskeleton, and a bird's wings, which come from arms, look and function the same---but their origins are different, so that's analogy. On the other hand, a bird's wings and a human's arms look different, but their origins are the same---so that's homology.''

``Oh! If both nue and tengu originated from musasabi, then you can call them homologous. And `character overlap' is more like analogy, I guess.''

\textit{That was fast, thank goodness.}

``So what Kannabi's saying is: in a culture that already interpreted musasabi as nue, is there room for a new theory to slip in and interpret musasabi as tengu instead?''

``Yeah\dots\ probably that. If it's a place where the nue legend---born from musasabi---has already taken root, then all the strange night phenomena caused by musasabi would just get folded into nue. It's a little strange for tengu to suddenly appear.''

I could see what she meant, but I also wondered why she was fixating on something so trivial.

``So what---you're saying present-day Oku-Tsunanagai is like a human with wings growing out of their back?''

Mizasaki used a confusing metaphor that only made things worse.

``I get curious about that sometimes too! When I see paintings of angels, I wonder where those wings come from. Evolutionarily, land vertebrates are only supposed to have four limbs, so if you've got wings that are homologous with arms, isn't that kind of weird?''

``If it's not a homeotic mutant, maybe they drank Red Bull.''

``Or it's Shiyouken.''

The conversation had gone off track. Since I was the one who'd started joking around, I took responsibility and steered us back.

``If we're going to propose something, then it's probably that the nue legend died down. Then tengu came in as a new interpretation---with the Edo period as the dividing line.''

``Yeah. With the illustrated diagrams and art pieces, it was neatly split\dots\ pre-Edo works used nue as the motif, and post-Edo works used tengu as the motif\dots''

``So what happened in the Edo period, I wonder.''

We were supposed to be doing a biology investigation, but at some point it had started to sound like folklore.

In the end, that topic petered out without a clear answer, but as we walked around searching for feeding traces, Kannabi looked troubled the whole time.

As evening approached, we went back to the inn for a bit and prepared equipment for the night investigation. ``Equipment'' was just a light tripod and a DSLR. We'd brought two high-sensitivity $\alpha$7 units suited for night shooting---one that had been sitting at the back of a shelf as a Biology Club item, and one I'd borrowed from my father. Both were older models, but functionally, they were more than good enough. So we wouldn't startle the musasabi, we wouldn't use a flash---only a red light.

Only Iwama and I were used to operating cameras. The plan tonight was to split into two groups again, which meant Iwama and I would inevitably be separated. We decided that ``in terms of firepower'' (?) it would be better to have mixed-gender pairs, so it was settled: Iwama with Mizasaki, and me with Kannabi.

Our targets were the cedar tree and the worship hall where `tengu pebble-throwing' had been observed last night. The cedar had a tree cavity that was probably being used as a nest, and the worship hall had a hole that allowed access into the attic. We'd watch those two spots as the key points and wait for the musasabi to come out. The Iwama--Mizasaki pair would cover the cedar, and the Kannabi--Izuta pair would cover the worship hall.

Not being noticed was the most important thing. We looked for a place where we could keep some distance and hide in the shadows, then set up the tripod there. Until the musasabi appeared, we wouldn't use the light. Our eyes and ears were all we could rely on.

I had Kannabi hold Iwama's custom red light, and I peered through the viewfinder, aiming at the hole near the worship hall's roof. With no obstacles, autofocus would be enough. The bare minimum was photos and audio, but I set us up so we could also try for video.

``Somehow\dots\ it feels like being a sniper.''

Kannabi muttered. Hiding in the shadows and aiming at a nest hole really did make it look like a sniper. \textit{Which made Kannabi beside me the spotter, I guess.}

``Structurally, it's the same. Whether you point a muzzle and take a life, or point a lens and take a photo. A rifle hits the target with a bullet. A camera captures the light reflected off the target through the lens. A sniper cares about wind speed, and a photographer cares about light.''

``Light matters, then.''

``You'll see if you mess with the camera a little. Especially in dark places, most things come down to trade-offs around light. You want to bring in more light for image quality, but if you zoom in, the amount of light drops---and if you slow the shutter speed, it's easier to get blur. If you push the sensitivity too hard, the noise goes up.''

\fullpageimage{5.jpg}


\thispagestyle{plain}

\vspace{1cm}

``Are you a camera otaku?''

``Not enough to call myself that. If you're going to handle a camera, this much is just common knowledge.''

``Hm.''

The conversation fizzled out. There was no need to force it to continue, but in this dusk-lit shrine, it was too quiet to sit in silence. The approach was lively with food stalls, but there were hardly any people on this side of the second torii gate. Even when someone did come, they headed back right away as soon as they finished praying.

Maybe she read the look on my face as I thought about that, because Kannabi lifted the corner of her mouth in a mischievous little grin.

``What, you dissatisfied because you got stuck with me?''

``Two wallflowers together don't exactly make for lively conversation.''

``I agree with that.''

Iwama and Mizasaki were probably over there, all kyakya, ufufu, tossing jokes back and forth. \textit{Those two could do that. We couldn't.}

``You're not worried Rio might get stolen by Sakkii, are you?''

I still wasn't used to Mizasaki being called ``Sakkii,'' so it took me a moment to register what she meant. Once I understood why Kannabi was grinning, I shrugged.

``He looks like a womanizer, but he's a gutless bastard who's never once managed to take a real step forward.''

``If you care about whether he's a gutless bastard, that means you do have the concern that if he wasn't, she might get stolen.''

``\dots That's sophistry.''

I answered on reflex, but it came out like I was getting worked up.

``I'm not so free that I can sit around worrying about who likes who. I thought you were the same kind of person as me, Kannabi.''

``Heh. Sounds like we'd get along.''

The sun sank lower, and the shrine grounds grew darker. \textit{On a spring evening this pleasant, why did I have to be talking with Kannabi about romance like this?} I changed the subject.

``By the way, why is Mizasaki `Sakkii'?''

``It's fine, isn't it. Let me call him what I want.''

``I don't care if you do. I was just curious. I thought there might be some reason.''

``\dots Fricatives.''

``Enough with that. If you don't want to say, just tell me so normally.''

``We're the Biology Club. We're probably going to be together for a while, right?''

``Yeah.''

\textit{So you'll explain it normally?}

``When I think about building my own character, I stopped being sure what I should call someone like that. If it's a girl, using her given name is the safe choice, but when it's a boy\dots\ I don't have the clean, refreshing vibe where I can call him by his surname with no honorific. But I also don't have the tidy, wholesome vibe where I can keep adding -kun forever.''

It surprised me that she decided how to address people by thinking about her own ``character,'' but I did understand what she meant. I ended up calling people by name with no honorific too, but it wasn't uncommon for me to hesitate over what to call a girl my age.

``But---''

I almost said, \textit{`You call me Delta, don't you?'}---and then clamped my mouth shut. \textit{That was close. I'd gotten so used to being called Delta that I almost forgot my own surname. Not Delta---Izuta.}

``---Then what did you do until now? Back in middle school, for example.''

``Do you think I ever had a boy I needed to get close enough to call by name in the first place? In cases where I had no choice---classes or events---I just used -kun normally. Worrying over what to call someone is genuinely unproductive. Though in the end you still have to worry, just to pretend you're a normal person.''

``Yeah.''

Outwardly I brushed it off lightly, but inside, I felt like giving Kannabi a high-five. \textit{She really understood the annoying thought patterns of a wallflower. And she put them into words with precision.}

It was frustrating, but we might actually get along.

``By the way, why did you teach Iwama that utterly nonsensical way to call me `Del-chan'?''

When I asked, Kannabi gave me a fearless grin.

``I worked hard to come up with the name that would make an introvert happiest to hear. You should be grateful.''

``Like hell I will.''

\textit{As if being the only one a popular girl called -chan could possibly make me happy.}

The filming was a success.

The instant the Japanese giant flying squirrel poked its face out of the hole in the wall, I snapped a photo---then I captured video of it running over the roof tiles, making a rattling \textit{katakata} as it went. I hadn't brought a mic, so the audio was mediocre, but there was enough that you could hear it even through the camera's tiny speaker. Comparing it to the data Mizasaki and the others recorded last night would be easy enough.

Iwama could apparently do it all. I had a fair amount of confidence in my camera skills, but Iwama was in another league. In the dark among the cedar trees, she'd not only filmed the Japanese giant flying squirrel peering out from its tree hollow---she'd even caught the moment it shot out of the nest and, immediately after, took a massive dump, clear as day in the red light Mizasaki must have been shining. With a 400mm lens, she'd zoomed in to the point where---gross as it was---you could practically see its butt. If she could handle that kind of telephoto work at night, she was basically pro level.

On top of that, Iwama had taken photos of two baby flying squirrels that came out after it. Meanwhile, I staked out the tree the one from the worship hall climbed and barely managed to capture it gliding. Since hitting it with a light all of a sudden would startle it, the only illumination was the lanterns in the shrine grounds and the town's faint glow. I didn't have the guts to shoot close-up, so it ended up looking almost like an UMA photo. At least you could make out a boxy silhouette and something like a tail, so I called it good---but after seeing how adorable the baby flying squirrels in Iwama's shots were, I stopped caring.

``That's amazing! I wanted to get gliding too, but I couldn't even see it with my own eyes in the end.''

``Well, that was optional anyway. It's fine even if there isn't much to show.''

I covered for myself like that, quietly grateful for Iwama's kindness.

``I got the Tengu Laugh too, loud and clear.''

Mizasaki held up his phone. On the almost pitch-black screen, you could see another phone's display---probably Iwama's---reflected in it. When he played the video, Mizasaki's finger in the frame pressed the play button on the other phone. \textit{Kyakyakya}---the flying squirrel's call played. Then, a little softer, \textit{kyakyakya} echoed back. Even after the finger in the video hit pause, the smaller voice kept ringing out.

``When we played audio from our side, it reacted. That story about Tengu Laugh `laughing back' could probably be explained by flying squirrels exchanging calls with each other.''

Kannabi had tried the same thing on her side too, but unfortunately, the flying squirrel hadn't responded. Well, that was just luck.
