We'd been on standby since 18:00, and now it was 20:00. Apparently, after an hour or two out of the nest, flying squirrels settled down and became less active. We'd hit every target for today. It was fine to call it a day.

``We started early this morning too, so\dots\ should we just go get dinner already?''

At Iwama's suggestion, everyone agreed.

We packed up the equipment, split up the load, and headed outside the second torii gate.

With Iwama striding proudly out in front, the approach road was overflowing with the bustle of the Tengu Festival.

% ==============================
%        Chapter 3–
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 3–\\
    Forbidden Zone
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch3m}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 3–}
\vspace{1cm}

According to the old man running the stall where we bought beef skewers, the festival was about to end, and all the food stalls would be packing up by 21:00. So we (especially Mizasaki) hurried to buy and stock up on food.

It seemed the Shinto rites had ended during the day. At this point it was basically a neighborhood festival that just happened to have stalls, and despite being called the Tengu Festival, it didn't really have any tengu elements. If you really wanted to count something, there was one food item called a ``Tengu Dog,'' but all it did was use a big red sausage meant to resemble a Tengu's nose.

We sat in a row on the riverside benches lining the street in front of the shrine and decided to eat what each of us had bought. There hadn't been anywhere on the approach road where you could settle in, so the benches around us were packed with similar customers. There were even students from the Physics Club and Chemistry Club.

The river at night was dark, flickering as it reflected the faint white glow of the town lights on the opposite bank. It was a warm night that felt like you could laze around forever.

``Our investigation was a huge success!''

Iwama said it happily, and we even stood up and toasted with local soda. When I sat back down, I was close enough that my elbow could've brushed Iwama's.

The bench was meant for three, but with our bags on it, only two people could sit. \textit{The reason was obvious, and yet for some reason Mizasaki and Kannabi had ended up on the same bench, leaving me and Iwama no choice but to sit side by side on the one next to them.}

``There's basically nothing left for us to do tomorrow, huh.''

Mizasaki said with satisfaction as he bit into a Tengu Dog he'd already been gnawing on during the walk. Apparently the big red sausage had a nostalgic, old-fashioned taste and was really good. \textit{Not like he knew that much about ``old-fashioned,'' though.}

``Looks like the weather's going to change too, so we could probably wrap things up early,'' Iwama said---of course she'd checked properly.

``We could eat lunch, then head back on the early-afternoon bus.''

``\dots I'm going to get carsick.''

Kannabi muttered at that suggestion. Fair point.

``Then how about lunch at the family restaurant by the station? Breakfast was pretty solid, so it's fine even if lunch is a bit late. Let's do a drink bar reflection session!''

``Nice! I'm in!''

When Mizasaki said ``reflection session,'' he probably just meant he wanted to shoot the breeze, but it wasn't a bad idea. It would make it easier to adjust our timing getting home, too. Both Kannabi and I agreed.

As we chatted and ate our stall junk food, I noticed four familiar figures walking toward us. Or rather, three familiar ones and one I'd only met that morning. Osawa, Watari, and Fujito---the ``Ototo Trio,'' who'd had bad blood with Mizasaki and me since middle school (their surnames start with o, to, and to, making ``ototo'')---and Hijiri, who was the exception.

The four of them must've been moving around together with the Physics Club. Watari made an openly sour face the moment he saw me, but Hijiri waved cheerfully.

``So the Biology Club is eating out tonight too.''

Leaving Hijiri---who'd stopped to talk---the Ototo Trio kept walking. Just the other day, those three had shown up in the biology lab, tossed out some snide remarks, and then caused trouble. They probably didn't want to face Iwama and Kannabi. As he left, Watari mouthed ``idiot'' at me.

\textit{Back in middle school, I would've flipped him off in response, but I was a high schooler now, so I let it slide with a cool look.}

Iwama and Mizasaki both had a \textit{who-is-this-shady-slicked-back-glasses-guy?} look on their faces, and since Kannabi wasn't saying anything at all, I answered for us.

``We couldn't predict how long the investigation would take tonight. Also---this is Hijiri from the Physics Club.''

``Right. I suppose I'm late introducing myself. I'm Hijiri. It's a pleasure.''

Hijiri's name was famous. Looking satisfied, Mizasaki and Iwama introduced themselves and greeted him too.

``Huh---Delta, you already knew him?''

At Mizasaki's question, Hijiri smiled faintly.

``This is actually the first time we've met fully clothed.''

The air froze. I couldn't tell if he was joking, or if he genuinely meant no harm, and that made it worse. Iwama was visibly flustered. \textit{No---look, that wasn't wrong, exactly, but still.}

``I ran into him in the bath this morning.''

When I explained, whatever Iwama had imagined, I could feel the tension ease out of her beside me.

``Right, right. That might've been a bit misleading.''

``A bit misleading'' didn't even begin to cover it. \textit{I hoped they'd forgive me for how sharp my reply came out.}

``So, what do you want?''

``Ah, just something. It didn't have to be right now, but\dots\ actually, maybe now is better.''

After nodding to himself like he'd reached some conclusion, Hijiri dropped his gaze to my hands. We'd already finished our stall dinner. I saw him flick a glance toward Kannabi too, but she was still battling a mountain of yakisoba.

``There's something I want you to see, Izuta. If you have time, could you come with me?''

``Something you want me to see?''

``It's not that far. It's in the shrine grounds.''

Even if we were done eating, we were still moving as the Biology Club. When I checked the others' reactions, Mizasaki held up both hands.

``I'll hold your stuff. Once we're done eating, we'll head back first.''

``Can I take you up on that?''

``Of course!''

I gathered up the trash and stuffed it into my backpack, then handed Mizasaki the camera bag.

``I'll be back.''

As I left the bench, \textit{was it my imagination that Iwama looked a little lonely?}

\textit{It had to be my imagination.}

``Sorry. It's not that urgent, but the Physics Club's schedule is packed tomorrow too.''

``Don't worry about it. All we've got left tonight is heading back to the inn anyway.''

The two of us passed under the gleaming first torii gate. The stalls lining the approach beyond it had already started packing up. It was almost 21:00. We didn't stop, walking briskly onward.

As we walked down the approach that looked, in every sense, like the aftermath of the festival, I brought up something to fill the silence.

``Do you get along with the Physics Club guys? You're more of a humanities type, aren't you, Hijiri?''

``Yeah. Surprisingly. The club president really pushed me to join, so I only meant to drop by and take a look, but\dots\ the training camp is a lot of fun too, and I think I want to keep going.''

``That's a pretty serious willingness to try things.''

``Is it? Actually, I've wanted to go into law for a long time. And since both jurisprudence and physics are `law' disciplines, maybe they have more in common than you'd think.''

\fullpageimage{6.jpg}

\thispagestyle{plain}

\vspace{1cm}

Hijiri laughed at his own joke---``Hahaha''---and I gave him a wry smile. \textit{I couldn't believe there was someone else who'd come up with the exact same line.}

``But keeping up with Watari and the others has to be rough. Even for me, and I'm on the science track, there are times I can't follow their physics talk.''

``Yeah. I've only ever studied the bare minimum of the science subjects we covered in school. Still, it's something I can learn from here on out. I'm really looking forward to adding new vocabulary to my dictionary.''

``Does that dictionary have the word `impossible' in it?''

``Of course. Not that I intend to use words that negative, though.''

We climbed the stone steps and passed through the weathered second torii gate, and the area around us fell silent in an instant. Hijiri, walking a little ahead, kept going without hesitation.

``So where's this thing you want me to see?''

``A little deeper in. Don't worry. I'm not going to catch you and eat you.''

``I'm not worried about that.''

He didn't seem to have any business at the worship hall. We walked past it and went through the third torii gate beside the main sanctuary. This was the mountain path leading to the inner shrine.

Not long after we entered, Hijiri stopped. He glanced around restlessly.

``Hey---did you hear something just now?''

``A sound? No\dots\ I wasn't really listening.''

``Then maybe it was an animal. It sounded like footsteps.''

With a shrug, Hijiri pointed to something that looked like a bulletin board behind the third torii gate.

``This is what I wanted you to see.''

Since I couldn't make it out well, I pulled out my phone and lit it with the flashlight. It was an old neighborhood map put up by the tourism association. It was drawn in detail, down to contour lines and even the names of streams, and it included the mountain path to the inner shrine and hiking courses that looped around the shrine area, with smaller trails marked as well.

And a piece of graffiti, sprayed in black paint, cut across it diagonally from the upper left to the lower right.

``And\dots\ what about it?''

``To put it simply, I want to try erasing the graffiti.''

``Erasing it? Why?''

``I want you to look at this part.''

Hijiri's finger pointed to the lower right---right where the spray paint crossed over.

``This is a small river called Jagasawa, near the gym our Physics Club uses. It's written with the kanji for snake, then that small katakana-like \raisebox{0.2ex}{\scriptsize ヶ}, and then `stream'---so,『蛇ヶ沢』.''

``So you want to confirm that on this map?''

\textit{I still didn't really get what Hijiri was trying to do.}

``That's right. This map even lists the names of the streams, doesn't it? But somehow, the place-name『蛇ヶ沢』seems to exist only in my memory. That can't be right, and it's\dots\ unsettling.''

``What do you mean? Didn't the Physics Club do its activities at that gym?''

``Yeah. But there's no place-name anywhere. And the gym that used to have the name『蛇ヶ沢』---it got partially renovated in 2014, and the name changed. But I definitely saw the characters『蛇ヶ沢』 11 years ago.''

\textit{A tiny stream's name disappearing didn't seem all that strange to me,} but something else bothered me.

``\dots 11 years ago?''

``Yeah. My parents took me around here for sightseeing once. Back then, behind the gym---right by the stream---there was an information sign. It had the word『蛇ヶ沢』 on it, and it explained the origin of the name. I can't find that sign anymore, though.''

``But 11 years ago means you were, what, about four? Couldn't it just be a false memory?''

\textit{At that age, it was questionable whether he could even read kanji in the first place.}

``No. I remember that part clearly.''

``I see\dots''

Still not quite convinced, I listened as Hijiri spoke gently.

``I don't really want to use strong words, but---''

A cold wind brushed past.

``When I say `absolute,' I mean it. Truly. Absolutely.''

``Alright. I'll believe you.''

\textit{Not that I actually believed him, but there was no reason to keep stubbornly doubting him here.}

``Thank you. That makes me happy.''

``So as long as we can erase even part of this graffiti, that's enough?''

``Yeah. If we can read what's written underneath, that's enough.''

I checked the surface. The black lacquer spray paint looked like the standard kind used for street graffiti. The map itself was resin---something that had endured years of wind and rain. The surface had faded from sunlight, but it looked like it had a coating; unless you sanded it down, it probably wouldn't come off.

``If we use lacquer thinner or a graffiti remover, we can get it off. You can buy either at a home improvement store.''

``So we can. Thanks. But a home improvement store\dots''

``By the way---why did you come to me about this?''

When I asked, Hijiri thought for a moment before answering.

``About that. Watari told me you know your way around chemistry too, Izuta. There's no home improvement store in this village. So I was hoping you might know something we could use instead. The sooner, the better.''

``So that's what this is.''

\textit{If it were as simple as ``use graffiti remover to remove graffiti,'' there was no way the guy ranked second in the grade wouldn't have thought of it.}

He probably wanted to resolve the question before we finished the camp and left.

``Then it might be best to rely on the Chemistry Club. They should have something suitable.''

``Really?''

``Yeah. I'll ask if we can borrow it.''

``Then please. I'd really appreciate it.''

Just in case, I took a photo of the map with my phone, and we headed back.

``Whoa---''

``---!''

Right as we passed through the third torii gate, I ran into someone I hadn't expected at all.

``Wait---Kannabi. Why are you here?''

In the dark shrine grounds, right beside the third torii gate, Kannabi was standing alone. She very clearly looked like she'd been eavesdropping, but she doubled down and said,

``\dots A white fox.''

``A fox?''

``I saw a white fox, and I chased it, and I ended up here. I lost it, though.''

``Huh. Maybe an albino. That's rare.''

This wasn't an albino-fox situation---this was obviously made up---but I didn't bother pointing it out.

``We were just about to head back to the inn anyway. Want to go back with us?''

``\dots Yeah. I will.''

As the three of us walked, I thought about why Kannabi had come. The ``footsteps'' Hijiri said he'd heard might've been Kannabi's, too. \textit{Had she been listening for a while?}

Well, I could understand the feeling. If Iwama---someone I'd only just met through the Biology Club---went off with Watari---someone I'd known since middle school---to talk about something, I'd be curious too.

\textit{Not that I'd actually follow them. But still.}

``I really did want to invite you too, Kannabi. But you were eating.''

Hijiri said it like he was trying to cover for her.

``Was I?''

``I hope it's just my imagination, but\dots\ I feel like there's a bad smell in the air. I thought you might be able to tell what it is, Kannabi.''

A bad smell---was a ``place-name that feels like it vanished'' really that big of a deal?

On the way back to the inn, Hijiri explained to Kannabi the same thing he'd told me. Kannabi listened seriously all the way through, then said with a solemn look,

``Actually\dots\ there's something I've been wondering too.''

``Really?''

``I thought I'd basically researched all the local legends in this area, using books and the internet. But when we came to this shrine, that was the first time I'd ever heard of something like a `gyounin-sama.'''

Gyounin-sama---the person who supposedly slew the nue. In the room where the nue mummy was kept, there'd been that illustration: a monk drawn as a skeleton. Of course, it was only the old man there who'd called him ``gyounin-sama,'' but still.

When Kannabi explained what had happened at the treasure hall, Hijiri rubbed his brow, looking just as serious.

``Gyou nin-sama, huh\dots\ If he used that name while pointing at that bizarre painting, it's\dots\ an ominous choice of words.''

``Right? And I'm thinking\dots\ maybe---''

``That possibility does seem likely.''

I had no idea what they were talking about.

``Ominous name? What do you mean?''

``Think about it. A gyou nin is just\dots\ someone who trains. It's a common noun, right?''

``So?''

``If a later generation uses a common noun to refer to a specific person, what kind of situation do you think that is?''

It was like a riddle. As I carefully made my way down the stone steps, I tried to think it through.

``In English, if you put `the' in front of a common noun, you can use it to identify one specific person. Like how `Komon-sama' in Hitachi basically refers to one person.''

``Then in a place like this, where Shugend\=o was thriving---what kind of situation would make `gyounin-sama' uniquely identify just one person?''

I thought about it. If Shugend\=o was thriving, there would've been tons of people training. A situation where you could point to just one of them and say ``the gyou nin''---that narrowed it down a lot.

``The only survivor\dots?''

Kannabi tilted her head slightly.

``Close. Remember how gyounin-sama was depicted.''

In an instant, the blood drained from my face. A chill ran up my spine.

\textit{Oh.}

``So he\dots\ `remained'\dots\ in death.''

Kannabi nodded, and Hijiri smoothly picked up the thread.

``In other words---sokushinbutsu.''

\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note:} Sokushinbutsu (即身仏) refers to the practice---most famously associated with certain Japanese ascetic traditions---of becoming a ``Buddha in one's very body,'' and in modern usage it often points to self-mummified monks. In other words, a sokushinbutsu is an ascetic who, through extreme training and ritual, dies and is preserved as a mummy to be venerated.}

% ==============================
%        Chapter 4+
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 4+\\
    The Night Bird
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch4p}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 4+}
\vspace{1cm}

I'd been able to procure what I needed from Mikage.

On the third day of camp, we slept in, checked out, and---with our luggage left in storage---headed out for our final investigation. Heavy, ominous clouds hung low in the sky, and it looked like rain could start at any moment. Building on what we'd accomplished last night, we split up and took photos around various spots.

Or rather, I was the one who'd suggested, ``Let's split up and take photos.''

I headed first for that map Hijiri had shown us---the old, graffiti-covered area map on the back of the third torii gate. It wasn't like I was about to do anything shady, but just in case, I checked that no one was around before pulling out what I'd brought.

It was aroma oil in a small glass bottle with a dropper---limonene.

I'd heard the Chemistry Club used this substance for a magic trick that pops a huge balloon without touching it. Limonene could even dissolve rubber, so it could easily remove lacquer-spray graffiti.

I dripped a generous amount of limonene onto a tissue and rubbed the lower-right edge of the graffiti Hijiri had been obsessed with. Slowly---yet unmistakably---the grimy black color wiped away.

The instant I understood what the newly exposed section implied, a chill ran down my spine.

The name of the stream had been clearly scraped off.

As if it were absolutely unacceptable for that place name to be there, the stream's name---and the gym's name---had been gouged out with something sharp, leaving obvious scratch marks where they'd been viciously erased.

The graffiti must have been there to camouflage that unnaturally blatant tampering.

With a creeping, indescribable disgust twisting in my gut, I took a photo and sent it to Hijiri.

---Thanks. That's really suspicious.

A reply came back right away.

---Is there anything else to look into? We've still got a little free time over here.

When I sent that, the response arrived with almost no delay.

---If you can, could you ask someone at the treasure hall about the ``gyounin-sama''? Kannabi-san was curious about it too. Only if it's not a hassle.

---Got it.

\textit{Impressed by Hijiri's high-school-girl-speed typing,} I headed for the treasure hall.

With our eleven o'clock meetup only fifteen minutes away, I didn't have much room to spare. The meeting spot was the second torii gate, not that far from the treasure hall. It wasn't exactly something I needed to hide, but if people found out I'd spent our photo-taking time inside the treasure hall, it'd be awkward---so I quickly paid the admission fee and went in.

There was no one else inside. Praying that the old man would be there again today, I stepped into the back room where the nue mummy was enshrined.

It was a dark space. Both to give my eyes time to adjust---and to put on an air of reverence---I first pressed my hands together before the nue mummy. \textit{I wasn't confident it was the proper etiquette, but at least it wouldn't leave a bad impression.}

It wasn't anything new by this point, but I wasn't good at starting conversations with people I didn't know well. Wallflowers were probably all like that. Especially when the other person didn't look friendly, I ended up overthinking everything---worrying about whether they'd make a face, whether I'd anger them, and so on.

But I didn't have time today, so I had to work up my courage and speak to him.

After bowing my head to the nue mummy, I slowly turned to the right, and the old man---just like yesterday and the day before---was sitting perfectly still on a folding chair.

``\dots Um, excuse me.''

``Ah. The Biology Club.''

His reply came out raspy, but it sounded relatively friendly.

``I was wondering about the gyounin-sama who defeated this nue\dots''

``I see.''

\textit{Gii}---the folding chair creaked as the old man stood. He started walking over toward me.

``Have you tried looking into gyounin-sama yourself?''

``N-no... Well, I did look, but I couldn't really find any descriptions that seemed to match.''

``That makes sense. It was a small secret passed down at this Tsunanagai Shrine.''

``A secret?''

The old man switched on a shaky flashlight and walked slowly over to the painting of gyounin-sama. I wasn't eager, but I followed after him.

A dead man in a dazzling kesa robe stared back at us with pitch-black eyes.

``In the Edo period, people always needed timber. Most buildings were wooden, and it was used as fuel as well. Especially in the early Edo period, when gyounin-sama was active, they say there was a remarkable increase in population. The areas around the city became covered in barren hills, and landslides occurred frequently.''

His hands trembled, but his voice was calm---like a gentle teacher's.

``While alive, gyounin-sama reportedly called himself `Yamasanki'---rendered as `returning to the mountain.' True to that name, he treasured the mountains greatly, and when officials tried to cut down the trees in this sacred precinct due to resource shortages, he opposed them. In modern terms, you might say he ended up being `blacklisted.'''

``Blacklisted\dots?''

``Which is why, on record, it's as though he never existed. And yet our shrine has continued to venerate gyounin-sama since the days of Edo---quietly, in secret.''

``Is he\dots\ still somewhere out there? This\dots\ gyounin-sama.''

It was a question couched in euphemism---asking whether the sokushinbutsu still existed. In the darkness, by the faint beam of the flashlight, I could see the old man turn to me with a suggestive look.

``Gyounin-sama is enshrined at the inner sanctuary.''

\textit{There wasn't supposed to be anything at the inner sanctuary,} but the old man stayed expressionless and refused to say anything more.

As I left the treasure hall, I ran into Kannabi, but she didn't really scold me for it---she just joined me, and the two of us headed for the second torii gate, our meeting spot. We arrived at the very last second before eleven, and Iwama and Mizasaki were already waiting. After we reported our findings to each other, we decided it was time to head home.

Maybe the exhaustion had finally caught up to us, because on the bus ride back, everyone dozed off---and before we knew it, we'd arrived in front of Tsunanagai Station. Because of that, Mizasaki---whose stamina had apparently fully recovered---enthusiastically led us to a family restaurant. It was midday during a long weekend, but by pure luck, we got a six-person table where we could set down our luggage.

It was the kind of place that had everything from Japanese food to Western food, even a bit of ramen. We each ordered what we wanted, but no one went for Japanese food or ramen.

After we'd filled our stomachs, a postmortem began: we nibbled at the mountain of fried potatoes Mizasaki had added on, and kept ourselves hydrated at the drink bar.

``Speaking of `postmortems'---there's something I totally overlooked.''

Mizasaki said it first.

``Remember how we'd already learned that the mountains in Oku-Tsunanagai were called `Bandori Mountain' or something?''

``The one where all you got when you searched was anime stuff?''

``Yeah, that. I'd mentioned it to Osawa---the blond Physics Club guy---and he found something for us. He said it was lying in the corner of the gym storage that Senzawasou has.''

Mizasaki held out his phone so we could see the screen. It looked like a scrap of paper, yellowed with age and coated in dust. But we could just barely make out the thick characters, written in what seemed to be ink.

\textbf{Bandori Mountain Protection Movement --- Recruiting Supporting Members}\\
\textbf{Never forgive unjust logging!}

``What about the rest of it?''

When Iwama asked, Mizasaki shrugged apologetically.

``He said he didn't bother reading it. Something about it being dirty and he didn't want to touch it. Even though he's a klutz, he's weirdly germaphobic.''

\textit{In my head, something felt like it was about to click into place.} For now, I tried to keep the conversation moving.

``So what is `bandori,' ultimately?''

``\dots A flying squirrel.''

Kannabi murmured it before Mizasaki---like she'd successfully looked it up.

`Bandori' is apparently an alias for the Japanese giant flying squirrel. Seems like some regions used to call it that.''

``Huh, seriously?''

``I'm really sorry. If I'd noticed sooner, we might've gotten to the truth a bit faster. My bad. That was completely on me---my research was lacking.''

Mizasaki bowed his head. Kannabi worked her phone at an absurd speed.

``Bandori... So it's written with the kanji for `evening' and `bird,' huh.''

``Looks like it. If they'd written it that way instead of in katakana, we would've gotten it right away\dots''

Iwama clapped her hands once.

``Ah! Is that what Rio was trying to say?''

I was wondering what she meant when Kannabi nodded.

``Yeah. Same as the nue.''

\textit{I still didn't get it.} Then Iwama pulled out a notepad and wrote a single kanji in big letters with her mechanical pencil.

\textbf{Nue}

Only then did it click for me. Mizasaki was the only one still lost.

``Wait---hang on. I think I'm the only one who's not following.''

``Try breaking down the kanji for `nue'! Look---see?''

``Whoa! Total blind spot!''

Was it just coincidence? No---whatever the root was, it had to be the same.

If you break down the kanji for nue, you get ``night'' and ``bird.''

Bandori---written as ``evening'' and ``bird''---shared that. A nocturnal beast that flies through the sky. The shared core between the nue and the flying squirrel was being made explicit by their names.

After that, we searched as much as we could about Oku-Tsunanagai's ``Bandori Mountain Protection Movement.'' But whether it was because it was a long time ago, or because it had been a small-scale movement, nothing of note turned up.

So I changed approach and searched again, dropping the ``protection movement'' part for the moment. That did get us somewhere---apparently, the mountains to the north of Oku-Tsunanagai were especially known as ``Bandori Mountain.''

In particular, the northern part of the forest has been designated as a restricted area by Tsunanagai Shrine, and even human entry has been strictly limited. As a result, many broadleaf trees and massive hinoki cypresses---favored by flying squirrels---have been preserved.

It was from a personal blog post from about twenty years ago. Maybe it had already slipped out of the administrator's hands, because right at the top, a huge banner read, ``This advertisement is being displayed because there have been no updates for a certain period of time.'' I shared the article and explained my interpretation.

``A tree hollow that flying squirrels use as a nest is something you usually see more often in broadleaf trees than in conifers. Near the shrine grounds, the nest was in a giant planted cedar, but maybe deeper in the forest, it's not cedars so much as giant broadleaf trees that get used.''

``That's why it's Bandori---'' Iwama said, her voice bright with excitement. ``A flying-squirrel mountain!''

Across from her, Mizasaki folded his arms.

``But, like\dots\ isn't this weird? If it's a mountain the shrine's protecting, it feels like you wouldn't need a protection movement in the first place.''

Mizasaki's point was fair. \dots\ Of course, that was only if that sacred land still existed.

Kannabi pulled up an aerial photo of the relevant area on her tablet and set it on the table.

``I wondered if the shrine really managed to protect Bandori Mountain.''

North of the shrine, the green forest had been cleared away, and deep indigo solar panels blanketed the ground.

This year's Golden Week was split in two by three weekdays.

The day after our camp, Tuesday, April 30, was a normal school day. Before the heat of our overnight, out-of-the-ordinary field investigation had even had time to cool, we were made to go to school and sit through regular classes.

Since Monday had been a holiday, the Biology Club activity day---normally set for Monday, Wednesday, and Friday---had been shifted to Tuesday. It seemed Iwama had already sent a simple message, but after school, we were going to formally report on the camp to Karato-senpai again.

With a long table between us, the four first-years sat on one side and Senpai on the other. Senpai held a single sheet of paper, checking it as they listened to what we had to say (mostly Iwama).

Senpai listened with nods and little murmurs of acknowledgment, and once our report was finished, they even gave us a round of applause.

``Well then---bravo. You kids did great. This is an outstanding result. Everything lines up beautifully. That means you had a good leader, huh. So who made the plan?''

``Basically the four of us talked it through, but... I think the reason the pieces lined up so well is thanks to Gamma. She summarized the key points in bullet form---real clean.''

Mizasaki really talked Iwama up, but I didn't disagree.

Not just when we were making the plan---right up through the course correction on the second day of the investigation---Iwama had organized the information in exactly the way we needed, and because of that, we were able to keep our direction clear.

``N-no, it's not like... I just did what anyone would.''

``Even if it's `what anyone would,' it's still impressive. Organizing information is the unglamorous part nobody wants to do, but when you're investigating with multiple people, it's actually the most important part. I'm terrible at that kind of thing.''

Only then did Iwama's expression soften into a small, pleased smile.

Once our report had wrapped up, Karato-senpai asked---as if it were an afterthought---

``So, for this investigation... is that everything you wanted to tell me?''

The four of us froze for a moment. We didn't understand what Senpai meant.

``I think we told you everything we found\dots''

As Iwama checked the notes in front of her again, Senpai nodded.

``Right. Of course---I don't think your findings are lacking in any way. That's not what I mean. What I'm asking is: about this investigation, is there anything else you all want to tell me? Just confirming.''

They repeated essentially the same thing twice, but I still couldn't grasp what they were getting at.

Only Kannabi, without flinching, met Senpai's gaze head-on.

``Navi-san, did you notice something?''

When Mizasaki asked in a low voice, Kannabi gave a small nod.

``Um... Karato-senpai. If there's something we noticed---can we say anything?''

``Of course. That's why I'm asking.''

It seemed like something had passed between the two of them. But I had no idea what they were talking about. Iwama and Mizasaki looked just as lost as I felt.

``Then\dots''

After a brief pause, like she was choosing her words carefully, Kannabi continued.

\fullpageimage{7.jpg}

\thispagestyle{plain}

\vspace{1cm}

``Is this a test?''

\textit{What?}

``Ooh, nice catch. That's right. This question is the last problem on the test.''

While the three of us stood there more bewildered than surprised, only Kannabi lifted her face as if everything had just clicked into place.

``Then I'll say it. The Biology Club's investigation of Oku-Tsunanagai was something that was carried out every year---no, at the very least, it was carried out every year until a few years ago, wasn't it?''

It came out of nowhere. But judging from Karato-senpai's delighted expression, somehow\dots\ it was the correct answer.

``When did you notice?''

``When we found the report.''

``Huh---right from the start. Guess my acting really was bad\dots''

``Your acting was\dots\ decent. I didn't suspect it from that alone. But I felt something was off about how dirty the pages of the report were.''

As I listened to that explanation, I remembered the time Kannabi had pulled me aside to make copies.

---\textit{Didn't you notice something when you saw that report, Delta?}

---\textit{Notice\dots\ I said most of what I noticed right there, but, well\dots\ I did think it was strange that there was a lot of tiny debris stuck in the gaps between the pages.}

---\textit{And what conclusion does that fact lead you to?}

---\textit{No idea. Did you notice something, Kannabi?}

---\textit{Not really.}

Even back then, Kannabi had already sensed the truth. I'd had a clue too, and I'd felt the discomfort. \textit{Why was it strange for there to be debris stuck in it? Because it was a report that was supposed to have been sleeping in the back of a shelf for fifty years. Of course, I couldn't rule out the possibility that it had been filthy fifty years ago too. That was why I couldn't draw a firm conclusion.}

``Wait---hold on. Navi-san, how do you get from `the pages were dirty' to `there was an investigation every year'?''

``That report had been opened frequently enough that the pages were wrinkled, and it had accumulated a lot of grime. Normally, if something is meant for storage, you wouldn't choose something that dirty. If it was printed, there should've been multiple copies. So why use one in that terrible condition---that was where my question started.''

Her voice was quiet and restrained, but even I could tell the confidence beneath it didn't waver.

``Nice, Navi-chan. Then what's your answer to that question?''

``That report was unearthed like that every year as a challenge letter to the new first-years, wasn't it? And that's why the grime built up over time. It might've gotten that dirty because the documents avalanched onto the floor again and again.''

``For my own learning---did you have any other clues to support it? Other than our little amateur theater?''

``I'd heard the Physics Club and Chemistry Club already had a training camp planned. And then, by coincidence, the Biology Club happened to have a perfect investigation topic fall into our laps on the exact same schedule. Little mismatches like that kept piling up.''

``Yeah, that's about right. Back then, apparently, the plan was a camp where all three grades went to Oku-Tsunanagai together, and it was assigned in the form of a task for the first-years. The second- and third-years did something like `find a hundred plant and animal species on this list' every year. Sounds like it was tradition.''

`---It's basically like an orientation camp. It's a regular annual thing for the science department.'

\textit{I remembered what my dad---an alumnus of Tsunanagai High's Biology Club---had said. Maybe I should've realized it right then.}

It seemed Iwama and Mizasaki were starting to understand the situation too. Iwama asked.

``Then... that report---was it originally written as a challenge?''

``Sorryyyy. Yeah, it was. And the `fifty years ago' part was a lie too. Apparently the current version was put together about ten years ago by this incredible senior named Carson-senpai, all by herself.''

\textit{So that really was it.}

For a report seriously written by students at a prestigious school, I'd thought there were too many oddities. But I never imagined the document itself wasn't a report at all---it was a challenge.

``But your investigation wasn't pointless, okay? I heard Carson-senpai was a country kid and still ranked first in her grade---a total genius. Answering a problem a genius like that put together with almost a perfect score... I bet that's never happened before.''

``...A perfect score?''

When Mizasaki asked, Karato-senpai showed us the paper in their hand. It was a checklist.

``This is the grading rubric. Supposedly Carson-senpai put it together---each item has points assigned. And since you kids even noticed that this test was a test, you got an incredible ninety-two out of a hundred.''

The four of us looked over the list.

{\bfseries
\begin{itemize}
\item Determine that the ``tengu pebbles'' on the giant tree are flying-squirrel droppings right after leaving the nest --- 8 points
\item Secure photo or video evidence of it --- 6 points
\end{itemize}
}

That was the idea---points awarded based on how far you'd unraveled the mysteries in the ``report.''

Looking at the last two lines, it said this:

{\bfseries
\begin{quote}
(※ At the end of grading, ask: ``For this investigation, is that everything you want to tell me?'')

--- Determine that this exam is an exam that uses a fake report --- 8 points
\end{quote}
}

``Navi-chan, nice one. Most grades can cover the ghost-story stuff to a certain extent, but apparently the serious, diligent types who are good at that kind of thing... tend not to notice this last item.''

Kannabi didn't look particularly pleased, but she quietly said, ``Thank you.''

Beside her, Iwama was still reading the rubric with a serious face. Her gaze was fixed straight on one line. \textit{I'd been a little curious about it too.}

\textit{Where had we lost points?}

I felt like we'd done everything we could. But in the rubric, points had even been assigned to something we'd given up on beforehand.

{\bfseries
\begin{itemize}
\item Solve the mystery of ``tengu toppling'' in the report --- 8 points
\end{itemize}
}

``Tengu toppling'' was the phenomenon where only the sound of a huge tree being cut down would echo. In the fabricated report, the students from fifty years ago were described as having actually witnessed the moment the tree fell---inside the restricted land.

``Yeah, tengu toppling. This is the one item where it doesn't even say what the correct answer is, and I've heard nobody's ever solved it. So in practice, your ninety-two is a perfect score. Congrats.''

Iwama gave an ambiguous smile, but her face clearly didn't accept that. Neither did mine. \textit{If I'd missed something on a test, getting through without knowing the right answer to that part just... didn't sit right.}

``Um... this Carson-senpai who made the problem---can we still get in touch with them?''

When Iwama asked, Karato-senpai folded their arms with a troubled look.

``I wondered too, but... it seems hard. Her real name, like it says on the rubric, was Shimomura Reichi. Shimomura, so `Carson.' True to the nickname, she was really passionate about environmental protection, and after she graduated, she went to the real deal---America---and apparently never came back.''

So, Rachel Carson. Was it coincidence that even the given name was similar? Maybe it was fate that this senior---called a genius---ended up on the path of environmental protection.

Wait... environmental protection?

``Was Shimomura-senpai involved in the Bandori Mountain Protection Movement, by any chance?''

At my question, Karato-senpai nodded.

``Yup, yup. It's probably because she used to go to that area all the time that she was especially fired up about this flying-squirrel investigation too. We're planning to pass everything on to you guys within this school year, but seriously---Carson-senpai's notes are amazing. They even specify, in detail, the method for getting the first-years to find the report.''

Kannabi twitched in response. She immediately asked Karato-senpai,

``All the way down to using the tree frog mummy?''

``Yeah. You talk about the frog-chan mummy, make it a thing where you're going to show them the real one, open the dedicated cabinet---then the document avalanche happens, and while you're cleaning it up, the report gets found. That's the procedure. The upperclassmen used to call it the Shimomura Method. Pretty diabolical staging, right?''

As I marveled at how far it had all been thought through, I noticed something even stronger than surprise crossing Kannabi's eyes---shock. Not in the sense of widening them, but in the way her pupils seemed to waver, her expression turning grave.

``Okay, then... I guess it's about time to move on to the cleanup. Seriously, good work, everyone. Since this is a test to get you used to field investigations, I'd be happy if you could also write up a report after Golden Week, just as a formality. It should be good practice going forward. Man, I'm so happy to have such excellent kouhai-kuns.''

Karato-senpai said it with satisfaction and stood up with a grunt.

``Yokata nee.''

Rohto-gelb cried out.

While I was in the bathroom, Mizasaki and Kannabi seemed to have vanished, so I ended up heading home with just Iwama. \textit{Can you seriously not even let your guard down enough to use the toilet in this club?}

Under a sky that stayed murky gray even as it darkened, I walked side by side with Iwama. It didn't feel real that I'd absentmindedly ended up holding hands with her for just a moment three days ago.

\textit{It might be a childish way to put it, but it felt like we were walking home from a theme park.}

And that feeling wasn't only because the camp was over.

I was worried about Iwama---about Iwama, who'd learned the truth that this field investigation, which had been so fun and had gone so well, was actually a test Senpai assigned.

About Iwama, who had been the one most fired up, the one who'd led the investigation.

``Part of me feels like that was kind of... harsh.''

I started by putting that impression into words as my own.

``Making us investigate that seriously, and then saying, `Actually, it was a test.' It's a letdown---or something. I want them to at least spare a thought for how we felt, staying up late and pouring ourselves into it.''

Iwama looked at me and smiled.

``Delchi, you're kind.''

\textit{I'd thought I was being considerate in a way that wouldn't make it obvious I was worrying about her, but it seemed pointless.}

``I'm fine. I was shocked, sure, but that doesn't change the fact it was fun. And besides... I'm kind of moved right now.''

``Moved...?''

``Because we re-investigated a Biology Club senpai's problem from ten years ago---without even knowing it was a problem---and we arrived at the exact same conclusion. Don't you think that's really... scientific?''

``Reproducibility.''

``Yeah! Reproducibility!''

In science, reproducibility is one of the most important concepts there is. If someone followed some special procedure and succeeded in turning lead into gold, but then other people tried the same procedure and it didn't work, you couldn't call that science. It would be magic---or a miracle.

The foundation of science is that anyone can obtain the same result, as long as the conditions are met. That it can be reproduced.

That's exactly why you can gather so much knowledge from around the world and make it stand up as a giant.

``Of course, Shimomura-senpai probably created that challenge so their kouhai could solve it. But even so, we didn't just think about it on paper---we actually went out and investigated real nature and culture, right? And then we ended up at the same conclusion as a senpai from ten years ago. I just think that's genuinely amazing.''

``Yeah. When you're dealing with living nature and culture, it's not like synthesizing an ester in a test tube.''

``The world is really put together so beautifully!''

\textit{I'd never thought of it that way. But maybe it was. Even this overcast sky was moving according to the laws of science. That was why it was beautiful---no less than a blue sky or a sunset.}

``But next time I want to do an investigation that's just ours.''

\textit{For a split second, I almost misunderstood what she meant, but by ``ours,'' she probably meant the four first-years.}

A club activity we tackled on our own, free from the upperclassmen's schemes.

``What do you want to try, Iwama?''

``Hmm, I don't know. If I start listing things, I could probably come up with a hundred...''

``If you could narrow it down a little, that'd really help.''

``Then... maybe the ocean.''

``The ocean, huh. That sounds good.''

It wasn't like I hated investigating mountains, but somehow, the sea felt... freer.

``I'll have to get my swimsuit ready.''

Iwama said it with a faintly mischievous look. \textit{Would a swimsuit really be necessary for an investigation?}

\textit{I shook my head to drive away the not-so-appropriate image that had flickered through my mind.}

---Could we talk on a call for a little bit? Somewhere no one can overhear.

That message suddenly came in later that night.

Kannabi had created a three-person chat with Hijiri and me in it, and called out to us there.

---Of course.

Hijiri replied immediately. \textit{I'd be in trouble if my little sister suddenly barged into room, but... I decided to trust that I wouldn't have that kind of bad luck tonight,} and sent back, ``That's fine.''

Before long, the call started.

【Sorry for calling so late. But I just couldn't stop thinking about it.】

Kannabi's voice came through the earphones plugged into my phone. Normally, I never thought anything of it, but hearing it like this, it was oddly... a really nice voice. \textit{There was no way she meant it that way, but it made me feel like she was whispering something sweet right in my ear.}

\textit{I considered taking the earphones out and switching to speakerphone. But weighing that against the risk of my sister eavesdropping, I ultimately kept the earphones in.}

【I'm really happy we can talk again, the three of us.】

Hijiri's voice followed. Feeling like I needed to say something too, I spoke into the earbud mic in a lower voice.

``Ahem''---a throat-clearing sounded, and Kannabi began to speak.

【About Oku-Tsunanagai. The story about Gyou nin-sama, and the story about the place name that disappeared... I think they might connect, a little.】

Kannabi explained to Hijiri about Carson-senpai---what she'd heard from Karato-senpai today. Even as I listened, I still couldn't understand how the vanished place name fit into it.

【Interesting. So even down to the part where the materials slide off the shelf, it was decided---precisely.】

``Sorry---I feel like I'm the only one who's behind here. What does it matter if they slid off?''

【Come to think of it, I never explained it, did I. About what the name Jagasawa means.】

【Now that you mention it, that might be true. Back in middle school, Kannabi Rei taught me about it. It didn't get passed on to Izuta.】

``The meaning of the name... Doesn't it just mean `a ravine with snakes'?''

There was a creepy pause.

【Place names sometimes have frightening meanings built into them. Place names with `dragon' or `snake' are one of those. Especially when it's a ravine.】

【Snakes and dragons can often indicate that there was a debris flow.】

``Debris flow?''

``In heavy rain, the way the earth and sand rushed down probably looked like the tracks of a giant snake slithering out. Even the sign I saw eleven years ago had an anecdote like that written on it. A long time ago, that was flat land created by a debris flow that came down from the ravine and killed a lot of people, and even though there's a sabo dam there now, please be careful during heavy rain---something like that.''

``Is that... reliable? That's a memory from when you were four.''

``It's my memory from when I was four. This is reliable.''

\textit{I couldn't imagine someone reading that, understanding it, and remembering it at age four,} but Kannabi took it in stride and didn't comment on that point.

【Shimomura-senpai, who took part in the nature protection movement in Oku-Tsunanagai, was particular about the tree frog sokushinbutsu, and the document landslide. In that, I feel some kind of message.】

A jolt ran through my brain. Sokushinbutsu...!

The escaped tree frog had died behind the ice maker and dried up. That alone was nothing, but if the corpse was being carefully kept, it was another story. It wasn't hard not to think of a sokushinbutsu.

It almost felt like it was hinting at Gyouin-sama.

Of course, if it were only that, it could've been coincidence. But if the documents were also being used to depict a landslide, that changed the story again. At the very heart of the introduction to investigating the legends that remained in Oku-Tsunanagai, it was as if---like foreshadowing---a sokushinbutsu and a debris flow had been worked in.

Even after all that, could you really dismiss it as coincidence?

A debris flow that supposedly left many dead---the fact that someone had tried to erase the place name that carried that memory. The existence of a sokushinbutsu kept secret. What was Shimomura-senpai trying to convey?

【A message, huh. There are a lot of angles we could consider, but first, I'd like to hear directly from this Shimomura-senpai person.】

Taking Hijiri's words as my cue, I used the PC in front of me and quickly searched ``Shimomura Reichi.''

``Nothing's coming up.''

``If she went to America, maybe romaji would be better?''

``That's it.''

This time I typed in ``Reichi Shimomura.'' Still nothing.

Thinking maybe she used a nickname, I tried searching ``Rey Shimomura'' and ``R. Shimomura,'' and this time got a few hits. But I couldn't find anything tied to research institutions or environmental protection groups.

``If she's someone from ten years ago, she'd be just under thirty. She might've gotten married and changed her last name.''

``If that's the case, we can't track her down... Well, I can keep digging on my end.''

【Could I ask you to?】

``Yeah. It's no big deal.''

After I said that, I remembered there was something I still hadn't told the two of them.

What I'd heard from the old man---in the treasure hall room where the nue mummy was.

``Speaking of which, I don't know if it's related, but---''

Halfway through, I heard a click in my earphones, like a door opening, and I stopped mid-sentence.

【Sorry. My little sister---】

The sound must've been on Hijiri's side. So Hijiri had a little sister too.

【My sister came by my room. Can we talk again tomorrow?】

It wasn't urgent, so we decided to end the call there.

By the way, something I heard from Kannabi afterward: apparently, Hijiri had no little sister at all.

% ==============================
%        Chapter 4–
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 4–\\
    Where Is the Buddha?
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch4m}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 4–}
\vspace{1cm}

On Wednesday, I left club early, met up with Hijiri, and we headed to the Municipal Central Library.

Kannabi wasn't coming along. At Hijiri's request, it would be just the two of us.

We walked side by side along the route home from school, still too early to call it evening.

``Kannabi-san said this whole thing reeks in a way that makes her sick, and I feel exactly the same. Not just unpleasant---I'd call it a sharp, stinging smell.''

``Stinging smell'' was a surprisingly stylish phrase. Hurrying to keep up with Hijiri's brisk pace, I said,

``But why aren't we bringing Kannabi?''

``I don't want to drag her into this. Because of me, I don't want her to have to feel awful anymore.''

\textit{Had something happened before?} Digging into it felt wrong, so I tried to lighten the mood.

``So I'm fine, though.''

``I need Izuta.''

He said it with those earnest eyes, and it made me oddly self-conscious.

``From here on, we might have to fight a massive monster. I can sense it. I was anxious about going in alone.''

The way Hijiri spoke---like he was biting down on each word---made me think of last night's call. Hijiri had used that lousy lie about a little sister who didn't exist stopping by his room to cut me off. Even then, he must've already sensed something. He'd done it because he'd decided Kannabi shouldn't be told anything more than that.

``Do you have some idea what it is? When you say `fight,' what are we even fighting?''

``Rot. The kind that, normally, isn't on a level that high schoolers should be fighting.''

Saying something that terrifying that casually, Hijiri gave me a faint, encouraging smile.

``I want allies, but more allies can just as easily mean more casualties. That's why we fight with as few people as possible, and with the most efficient lineup. I want to team up with Izuta.''

``And why's that?''

\textit{I couldn't imagine Hijiri knew me well enough to say that. If anything, most of what he'd heard should've been Watari's slander.}

But the answer I got was nothing like I expected.

``Ever since I heard the rumors about Izuta, I've wanted to work together. Among the students from this prefecture, I'm top of the humanities track, and you're top of the sciences. The top at Tsunanagai High School is practically the top in the prefecture. If the two of us team up, don't you think we'd be unbeatable?''

``\dots That's just talking about entrance exam scores.''

``Of course. But that's exactly why I can trust you. We're probably alike.''

Hijiri said it like it was nothing.

``This is my theory, but being good at studying, being smart, and being cunning are completely different things. I'm good at studying, but I don't think I'm smart---and if anything, I don't think I'm very cunning.''

``Then what we need this time is someone who's smart and cunning, isn't it?''

``No. What we're about to fight is far smarter than any high schooler, and terrifyingly cunning.''

``I don't know who they are, but you're saying we'll fight them with nothing but hard work?''

Hijiri nodded slowly, then smiled at me.

``We'll stand on the shoulders of giants. With two spears---letters and science---we'll bring down a rotten towering keep.''

Then Hijiri swept his gaze around, then headed down a straight, open road with no one on it. It wasn't the shortest route to the library, but he was probably planning to talk about something we couldn't let anyone overhear.

``Can you tell me what you were starting to say yesterday?''

Sure enough, Hijiri lowered his voice as he asked, so I answered in a hushed tone too.

That I'd learned the true identity of the gyounin-sama from the old man in the Treasure Hall. That he was a man named Sanki who'd tried to protect the forest in the early Edo period. That he'd been ostracized for it. That he was now enshrined at the Okunoin.

``I see. History repeats itself, huh.''

``Now it's my turn to ask. What are you thinking, Hijiri?''

Hijiri gave a small nod, glanced back once, then spoke.

``It's the same as in the Edo period. Ten years ago, there was a powerful group that tried to cut down the trees, and someone who tried to protect the forest and got driven out for it. The CHOSEI Group that built the megasolar site---and Shimokawa Reichi-senpai of the Tsunanagai High School Biology Club.''

``The CHOSEI Group?''

``A massive corporate group that's long expanded into a wide range of businesses across the prefecture, centered around this region. Well, to put it plainly, they're a mass of vested interests and rot. They use money and fear as a carrot and a stick, force their projects through, and rake in obscene profits. When I looked into the megasolar site in Oku-Tsunanagai, I ran right into them.''

Come to think of it, they were the group that had donated the massive first torii gate at Nagatsuna Shrine.

``You're really talking about them like they're evil.''

``My father is involved in local government. I've been hearing the rumors for a while. Apparently, the CHOSEI Group used some brutal methods to buy up land from Nagatsuna Shrine. It faces south, it's hard to spot from the surrounding area, and it's a large, convenient plot, right?''

As if he feared the walls were listening, Hijiri scanned our surroundings again.

``Here's my hypothesis. The megasolar construction is deeply tied to why the place-name `Jyagasawa' was erased. Jyagasawa is a place prone to landslides. The reason they erased a place-name that means that is---''

``To hide that they used that dangerous land as the construction site?''

``Yeah. For now, that's what I'm thinking. Deforestation reduces water retention and triggers landslides. And if they do something like dump fill, the danger only increases. It could be an issue of life and death.''

``Did you check the hazard map? If a megasolar site was built while ignoring the landslide hazard map, they wouldn't be able to avoid people calling that out.''

``Of course I checked. That area did have places that partially overlapped with designated warning zones, but legally, there wasn't a problem. It was probably just the result of a slope-based mechanical calculation.''

But\dots\ still. Did they know it was dangerous land and deliberately ignore it?

``At the megasolar construction site, there'd be on-site inspections too, right?''

``Right. Either there was fraud in those inspections, or they weren't conducted in the first place. With the place-name being erased, we have to be suspicious. And if we are, I'm sure Gyounin-sama will be the clue. The Biology Club method Shimomura-senpai left behind points to it.''

``But\dots\ how would Gyounin-sama\dots''

``You just told me, didn't you, Izuta. Gyounin-sama is enshrined at the Okunoin, right? Kannabi-san sent me a photo of the Okunoin.''

Hijiri showed me his phone. The Okunoin's double torii filled the frame. Beyond the torii was nothing---just the mountain, and then the megasolar site. With that much of a hint, I finally got it.

``This torii is pointing to Gyounin-sama's location, up in the mountains.''

``That's what I think. This is Kannabi-san's theory, but---when you look through it so the two torii, the nearer one and the farther one, line up perfectly like in this photo, doesn't it look like it's pointing at something?''

In the next photo, the double torii were perfectly overlapped. The angle you'd need to see it that way was fixed. And dead center in the scenery framed by the torii was the megasolar site.

``So it means Gyounin-sama should've been enshrined at that location.''

``Thanks---quick catch. On land where there may be buried cultural artifacts, you have to conduct a survey before installing any structures. Maybe Shimomura-senpai found evidence of fraud tied to that. And\dots''

But Bandori mountain had been cleared, and the megasolar site had been built. Shimomura-senpai vanished for some reason---leaving only that clue behind in the Biology Club's traditions.

``So now we're going to pin it down.''

``Yeah. Let's see how far we can get, just the two of us.''

Tsunanagai City Central Library had been rebuilt about fifteen years ago. It was a curving building with sweeping lines, defined by a massive roof that fanned out like a slope, and it was sizable---one basement level up through three floors above ground. That generous space went to an extravagant layout that made heavy use of open atriums, giving it more the feel of an art museum than a place meant to cram books into.

A daring design choice---putting a bookstore and café on the first floor, and a hamburger shop on a third-floor terrace overlooking the sea---had apparently drawn mixed reactions when it first opened. But seeing it bustling with citizens day after day now, even I, who would probably have been on the ``against'' side, couldn't help but admit it was a good place.

We headed for the local history corner on the basement level---the most inconspicuous section. It held books related to Tsunanagai City, works by people from the city, and back issues of local newspapers.

Hijiri would look into Jyagasawa, and I would look into Gyounin-sama---so we split up to research.

But Kannabi had said that no matter how much she looked into Gyounin-sama, nothing turned up. So I decided to approach it through Gyounin-sama's original name, ``Sanki.''

First I gathered background information through online searches, then pulled every potentially relevant book I could find and piled them on the desk. There were only a handful of search hits even remotely related to the name Sanki, but I did learn that a local amateur historian named Hirai Rosen had likely mentioned it in his writings. No parent would give their son the character 賤. So it was probably a pen name. It said he lived in Tsunanagai City, but based on the age I worked out, he likely wasn't alive anymore. For now, I tracked down every last one of his works that I could find.

And based on the background information---that in the early Edo period, a shugend\=o practitioner had tried to protect the forest in Oku-Tsunanagai---I skimmed through books in adjacent genres as well.

We were supposed to be doing this as the Biology Club, but at this rate it felt more like we were the History Research Club.

Watching Hijiri moving around in much the same way out of the corner of my eye, I kept at the literature search.

``Hey---got a second? I don't know if this'll help, but\dots''

Partway through, Hijiri brought over an old-looking hardcover titled \textit{Tsunanagai Place Names}. He opened it and pointed to a spot on the page where ``Teppouzeki'' was printed.

``This was the place-name on the bus stop we passed on the way to Oku-Tsunanagai, right?''

``\dots Was it?''

It might've been. I did remember thinking there were a lot of bus stops with names that sounded like something out of a folktale.

``Wait---didn't the Physics Club charter a bus to get there?''

``We did, but I caught a glimpse of it from the window.''

Well, it was easy enough to look up. When I checked the bus route map, sure enough, the name ``Teppouzeki'' was there. \textit{Between his sharp eyesight and his memory, what in the world was going on with Hijiri's nervous system?}

Impressed, I read the section Hijiri had indicated---and it gave an unexpected origin.

{\bfseries
\begin{quote}
Teppouzeki refers to a temporary weir built on a small river to float felled logs downstream. They would dam the river to store water, then break the weir open and use that current to transport the timber.
\end{quote}
}

``Then that would be proof that the timber felled in Oku-Tsunanagai was being shipped out of the area.''

``Yeah. Thanks. I get it.''

After that, I remembered the title of the book Hijiri was holding.

``How's your progress? That book looks like it could be useful.''

``Well---no matter how surprised you are, promise you won't make a sound.''

Somehow, Hijiri managed to open it to the relevant page on the first try.

Just like he'd warned me, I almost made a sound. A huge hole had been punched through the page.

``It's arranged in goj\=uon order, so if it's in here, it should be right here---right in the part that's missing. It's almost funny, isn't it?''

``That's a bad joke.''

``I wish it were.''

\textit{Who could've imagined someone would go this far just to erase a place-name? The sheer fixation---no, the horror of the method---made my whole body go cold.}

``Can't we borrow this book from another library? There's no way they can tamper with every copy of \textit{Tsunanagai Place Names} in the world.''

``I'll try. But it's an old book. I'm not getting my hopes up.''

With that, Hijiri went back to work. \textit{Watching his unruffled posture, I felt half reassured and half terrified. To see something like that and not even flinch\dots}

Pulling myself together, I went back to my own research.

I wouldn't say it was like looking for a needle in the desert, but it was like picking pebbles out of a sandbox. With almost no information, I did what I could to connect the few clues I had, and the timeline seemed to shake out like this.

\begin{enumerate}
\item With an official edict in Kanbun 2 (1662), itinerant practice was prohibited, and the settlement of shugend\=o practitioners progressed.
\item In the early Edo period, shugend\=o practitioners were active at Nagatsuna Shrine. They performed Kaji prayers for local residents, increasing their renown.
\item In the early Edo period, as the population increased, logging in Oku-Tsunanagai grew more active.
\item Perhaps because more people were entering the forest (the author's conjecture), tengu legends began to spread in Oku-Tsunanagai.
\item In Tenna 3 (1683), the entire forest area north of Nagatsuna Shrine became a restricted area.
\end{enumerate}

Shugend\=o activity in the region and the logging of the forests happened around the same time, though their causes differed---legal structuring on the one hand, and population increase on the other. Sanki was probably active during this period, too.

Then, as I looked into Hirai Rosen's \textit{Tsunanagai Kik\=o}, a passage caught my eye.

{\bfseries
\begin{quote}
Sanki also left behind a tengu illustration (Figure 18). It is among the earliest of the tengu images handed down at Nagatsuna Shrine; while the technique is crude, it may be called a piece with palpable force. What I wish to draw attention to is its pose, as if it were threatening the loggers. This is my conjecture, but perhaps what is called a tengu was a strange phenomenon that Sanki actively tried to spread in order to admonish those who cut trees. Sanki, who shot and killed the nue, instead introduced the tengu to admonish the populace. Can it not be called a splendid paradigm shift---from eerie animals to higher beings that wield supernatural power?
\end{quote}
}

{\bfseries
\begin{quote}
Sanki's works cease after the Tenna years, and around this time, the restricted area north of Nagatsuna Shrine was established. Speaking of Tenna brings to mind the Great Fire of O-Shichi, but there is also a record of a great fire in Tsunanagai. Rebuilding a burned city required a vast quantity of timber. I will refrain from writing, in my own hand, what occurred, but it is a fact that with the restricted area, logging was severely constrained, and the forest was protected.
\end{quote}
}

This passage wasn't directly related to Gyounin-sama's location, but it could answer Kannabi's question during the camp: ``Why did tengu get mixed into the well-known culture surrounding the nue?''

The tengu were deliberately spread by Sanki to threaten the loggers. That was a reasonable inference.

And this book also hinted at why Sanki became ``Gyouin-sama.''

\begin{quote}
When timber became necessary to rebuild the burned city, and at last the devil's hand was about to reach for the forest of Nagatsuna Shrine---what did Sanki do? He worked to have the entire area designated as a restricted zone. But you couldn't get people to accept it just by designating a restricted zone for no apparent reason. You needed a kind of sorcery that would shift the hearts of the masses in a single landslide. After pulling that off, Sanki could no longer leave works behind---
\end{quote}

\begin{quote}
At that time, Sanki became a Buddha.
\end{quote}

In warm, humid Japan, a corpse normally rots. To become a sokushinbutsu, it isn't enough to simply die. It is said that severe ascetic practices were undertaken---wood-eating austerities to strip away muscle and fat, and even drinking lacquer tea to try to preserve oneself.

He even took into his body defensive plant chemicals that even flying squirrels deliberately avoid eating, trying to break down his own proteins from within.

With that level of resolve, Sanki mummified himself. Perhaps the area around that site was designated a restricted zone.

``I see.''

The words slipped out.

If you simply died in the middle of the forest, then no matter how much effort you put in, your body would rot. To become a sokushinbutsu in that forest, you'd need to choose a place sheltered from rain and wind, and with low humidity. It was hard to imagine Sanki---who hated cutting sacred trees---building a hut in the middle of the sacred ground. The only thing that fit those conditions was something like a rocky cave. And if we limited it to the direction the torii indicated, couldn't we pinpoint that location precisely?---

Then maybe what we needed to investigate wasn't literature about shugend\=o or Sanki, but geological and topographical survey reports.

As soon as the thought hit me, I started moving. Leaving only the necessary references at hand, I returned the rest to the shelves, searching not the humanities section but the science shelves. And in \textit{Geology and Vegetation of the Tsunanagai City Area}, I found a passage that snagged my attention.

{\bfseries
\begin{quote}
The calcareous material that outcrops east--west along this fault has soft mudstone immediately beneath it, and it often forms caves of various sizes. It appears to have been used since the J\=omon period, and numerous pottery fragments have been discovered. Of particular note is the fact that due to the basicity of the limestone, even J\=omon-period human and animal bones have been preserved. The next figure shows the distribution of confirmed sites. One can see that they are distributed in an unbroken line along the fault. The partial break is a restricted zone, but there is also a report that caves large enough for a person to enter were found there as well (Hasegawa 1986).
\end{quote}
}

The components of limestone are calcium carbonate, derived from things like coral reefs. Calcium carbonate hardly dissolves in water, but it's a salt of carbonic acid, a weak acid, and calcium hydroxide, a strong base, and it has the effect of weakening acidity. So it would prevent bones from being dissolved by acid---that was probably the idea.

Anyway, what mattered was the mention of the restricted zone. Looking at the figure, it seemed to match the forest north of Nagatsuna Shrine. In other words, if I could find this ``Hasegawa 1986,'' it might say something about caves inside the restricted area.

When I looked at the end of the volume, the references were listed in a long list. Something published in 1986 by someone named Hasegawa---it was there.

\textbf{Doki-Doki J\=omon Exploration Team: Surely You'll Find Prehistoric Treasures}

\textit{It kind of took the wind out of me. Was it for kids? The way it paired ``doki-doki'' with the J\=omon period---you could see the marks of some serious researcher's effort to come up with a pun that would grab the public's attention.}

Now it was the J\=omon period. It felt like I was doing lateral jumps between letters and science.

No one could imagine that \textit{Doki-Doki J\=omon Exploration Team} might contain a clue that would expose fraud in the megasolar construction.

Even after a quick scan of the shelves, I couldn't find \textit{Doki-Doki}. I tried entering the title into a search terminal, but that came up empty too. It didn't look like nearby libraries had it, either.

When I searched online, I could tell it really did exist. But I couldn't find any used bookshops that had it in stock. It was a book from almost forty years ago, and it probably hadn't had a large print run. Where was I supposed to go to get a copy?

``Hey---can I ask you something?''

On a long shot, I spoke to Hijiri. Hijiri, who'd been frowning over a spread of books, immediately turned back with a composed expression.

``Is there something I can help with?''

``Yeah\dots\ I want you to look at this.''

I showed Hijiri the reference list from \textit{Geology and Vegetation of the Tsunanagai City Area} and explained the situation in a low voice.

``I see. I've seen that book before. It's in the school library.''

``Tsunanagai High School's? In the local history corner?''

``Nope. On the history and archaeology shelf---third row up from the bottom, the seventh book from the right, maybe.''


What kind of joke was that? \textit{But I didn't even know how to respond, so I let it slide.}

``If it's a small reference with local content, I figured it would be classified as local history material.''

``It doesn't sound local from the title, so maybe it got misclassified. But I see\dots\ then it's probably going to be tough today. We're already well past the mandatory departure time.''

When I looked at Hijiri's wristwatch, it was already close to 20:00. Tsunanagai High School's mandatory departure time was 18:30, and normally every student, without exception, was driven outside the gates by then. Only during the summer could you extend it to 19:30 with a special application, but either way, it was obviously impossible now.

``Then I'll look for it in the library tomorrow morning.''

``That sounds best. I'll come with you. First thing in the morning okay?''

``Seven-thirty?''

``Yeah. I want to go in the moment the gate opens. How about 7:20?''

``Understood.''

Hijiri nodded, then closed the book in front of him with a soft clap. One after another, he closed the other books too.

``Tomorrow's early too. Let's call it for today. I got a little something on my end as well.''

Hijiri showed me the book that had been left open to the very end. It was a page packed with what looked like place-name strings, line after line without any paragraph breaks. Beside the book were an old map and a topographic map.

``It's a record of a large-scale debris flow that happened in Oku-Tsunanagai in the Kamakura period. It doesn't say where the point of origin was, but if you narrow it down by elimination based on the locations of the affected settlements and the contour lines on the current map, it really doesn't seem like it could be anywhere other than Jyagasawa.''

There were no markings on the map, so I couldn't really grasp the situation, but if Hijiri said so, it probably was.

``The hard part is you can't say the terrain from the Kamakura period is the same as it is now.''

``That's exactly it. It probably can't be decisive proof.''

After thinking for a moment, Hijiri held the topographic map out to me.

``I want your opinion. What do you think? If a large-scale debris flow happened in Jyagasawa, how far would the affected area reach?''

There was no place-name label, but the spot Hijiri was pointing at was probably Jyagasawa. When I traced the contour lines with my eyes from there, the valley naturally led toward the central area where Nagatsuna Shrine and Senzawa-so were.

``\dots I'm not an expert, so I can't really say, but depending on the volume, it would probably merge into the river and flow into the center of the settlement.''

``Right? In the Kamakura period, apparently even the first torii gate of Nagatsuna Shrine was swept away by the debris flow.''

``But then\dots''

\textit{I couldn't put it into words.} If a debris flow on the same scale as back then---or even larger---were to occur, didn't that mean the town of Oku-Tsunanagai could be swallowed by earth and sand? And if that danger was being left alone---no, being actively concealed---while a megasolar site was built there.

Hijiri looked straight at me and nodded slowly.

``This might be a far more serious case than we thought.''

``\dots Should we make copies?''

``Yeah. It'd be bad if someone punched holes in it.''

He said it like a joke, but Hijiri's eyes weren't smiling.

We used the library copier to duplicate only the parts that looked important, then headed home.

On the way back, I found myself turning over how I was going to make excuses to my little sister.

\textit{Without realizing it would be the last day I could remain ignorant of the darkness.}

As soon as the vice-principal opened the school gate, Hijiri and I stepped onto campus. It still wasn't even 7:30. The shoe lockers were open, but the library room wasn't yet. When we waited in front of the library, a security guard hurried over to unlock it for us.

``First up is \textit{Doki-Doki J\=omon Exploration Team}.''

Inside the library, tall bookshelves formed maze-like rows. Hijiri didn't hesitate, guiding me straight to the history and archaeology shelves.

``Third row up from the bottom, right?''

``Yeah. If it hasn't moved since the start of the school year, it's the seventh book from the right.''

\textit{He's still making that joke,} I thought, as I searched the shelf---and unbelievably, there it was: \textit{Doki-Doki J\=omon Exploration Team} on the third row up from the bottom. Before I even took it out, I counted. Seventh from the right.

``No way\dots\ You remembered that?''

All I could do was stare in shock. I'd only found this book last night, from the reference list. There was no way anyone could've memorized its exact location in advance. Which meant this guy had memorized a huge number of the library's books down to the row and the number. \textit{That didn't feel like something a human could do.}

``My memory's a little better than most. Especially when it comes to written information.''

``That's not `a little.'''

``Maybe. All right---let's find what we came for.''

Hijiri said that calmly, pointing to the reading tables. I nodded, forcing my surprise down.

In an empty library room---one where the lights weren't even on---we sat side by side at a table and opened the book. It was unmistakably aimed at kids. Skimming the table of contents, it looked like the final chapter, ``Onward to Adventure,'' was a record of the author's personal fieldwork.
