``So you're telling us to overlook wrongdoing? That can't possibly be acceptable!''

He kept his voice down, but the force in his tone was stronger than I'd ever heard. With Hijiri bristling in open anger, Mikage-senpai slowly shook his head.

``I get how you feel. But please---swallow it. You realized it with the coin locker, didn't you? They've got their hands in city politics, and they don't mind breaking the law. If you dig any deeper, `going to America' might not be the worst that happens. In the past, there have been times when people from this school just vanished. I'm not joking.''

There was a chill in his calm voice---something almost menacing.

And I remembered: the small knife inside the locker. The two dead rats lying in the blood puddle outside my house.

``You understand how dangerous it is to be in a position where you know about wrongdoing that `doesn't exist,' right? To keep things from escalating, we have to comply with their demands in the bare-minimum way---and then cut ties completely. If we can just get through this, Tsurio won't get involved anymore. Please---understand.''

When I hesitated, I looked at Hijiri. Our eyes met. His brows twitched slightly. He couldn't quite hide how unacceptable it felt to him, but in the end, his expression settled into resignation.

``\dots If we don't tell anyone else, does it end there?''

When Hijiri asked in a low voice, Mikage-senpai's face tightened, a bitter crease forming between his brows.

``Halfway, yeah. But it's not enough. We have to put together a convincing explanation for why the funding stopped so abruptly. Tsurio is apparently demanding we pin it on you two---but we can't do that, either. So we decided to build an incident that isn't technically a lie.''

He let out a slow breath, then looked at each of us in turn.

``We're calling it\dots the Tengu Festival Incident.''

Only Mikage Aya followed us out as we left Sansetsuto.

After that talk, I wanted to sort out my emotions. We headed toward the sea again.

By the time we reached the shore along the dim road, the sky was completely dark. There were no streetlights on the beach---only the light from the public restroom, and the headlights of cars traveling along the bypass.

The sea lay black as ink.

``I didn't think it would end like this. I'm truly\dots sorry.''

Hijiri said it in a voice that sounded like the low waves could swallow it.

``It's fine. It can't be helped.''

In the end, we accepted Mikage-senpai's plan.

The outline of the Tengu Festival Incident was as follows:

On the night of April 29, 2024---the night of the Tengu Festival---two first-year science-track students accidentally stepped into an area they weren't supposed to enter. In the process, they damaged property belonging to the CHOSEI Group. The group expressed its displeasure and halted all funding. The identities of the parties involved would not be disclosed; Mikage-senpai and Hongo-senpai would apologize as representatives.

It was a razor-thin compromise: a story that avoided mentioning the megasolar issue, minimally satisfied Tsurio's demands, and---without outright lying---could still be made to hang together.

We'd end up carrying an unwanted secret.

``Still\dots Mikage-san's brother spoke like he'd watched everything happen. For some reason, our actions during the camp and after the camp\dots everything, down to the details, had been completely exposed.''

At Hijiri's words, Mikage Aya bowed deeply.

``I'm sure it felt awful. I'm sorry.''

The clue, apparently, had been limonene. After seeing Hijiri paying attention to the erased place-name in the gym, Mikage-senpai had worked from a starting point---Why did Izuta Sho need limonene during the camp?---and from there, he'd arrived at the map we'd found and cleaned. It was like something out of Holmes.


The ``delinquent(s) damaged CHOSEI Group property'' referred to me---erasing the graffiti on the map. According to Hijiri, there were no precedents at all for applying Penal Code Article 261 to a case like that, but Mikage-senpai---wanting, as student council president, to avoid spreading something outright false---was forcing himself to lean on that silver anyway.

And ``accidentally stepped into an area they weren't supposed to enter'' was a metaphor. We hadn't trespassed into a forbidden zone---but we'd touched a taboo. A ``forbidden area'' in the conceptual sense. It was wordplay to an absurd degree, but better than letting the truth spread and generate even more tension.

The one who told Mikage-senpai about the limonene was, of course, his little sister.

``I told my brother everything I saw. And I probably will from here on, too. So if there's something you don't want the student council president to know, you shouldn't tell me.''

``What a wonderful, doting little sister.''

For some reason, Hijiri smiled faintly.

``I still can't accept Mikage-senpai's judgment,'' Hijiri said. ``There shouldn't be secrets. And using rhetoric that makes people misunderstand isn't something I think is right. But as for pursuing the truth---I intend to fully support him. Everyone has the right to uncover what really happened.''

That was Hijiri to the core. His policy---always aiming for what's right, grounded in truth---was unwavering.

``My brother used to be the same as you, Hijiri-kun. He believed everything should be brought into the light---no lies, no secrets, everything done openly and properly. But he changed over the past two years.''

Mikage Aya looked out at the dark sea.

``As the number of things he had to protect grew\dots he learned that sometimes you have to move in the shadows, where no one can see.''

Listening to the two of them, I thought: we'd already been put through that baptism ourselves.

The megasolar wrongdoing was something that, in a normal world, should've been made public and condemned relentlessly. But if the truth spread, the ``city coordination error'' excuse---the peace offering---would stop working. And until all those contradictions were patched over, how many people would be placed in danger?

The other side was an enormous evil that didn't hesitate to break the law. Meanwhile, we were just high schoolers. To avoid further victims, we had no choice---for now---except to hide the truth and let a false story circulate.

This was the only solution.

To protect the bright stage, we'd become the ones who hid the darkness behind the curtain.

``Kind of ironic,'' I muttered. ``We were supposed to be heading toward the sunrise\dots\ and we ended up sticking our feet into a dark forest instead.''

Hijiri silently lowered his head. Only the black sea answered me with a \textit{zabun}.

``\dots We'd be up against the guys who drove Shimomura-senpai to America. I don't want my one-and-only high school life threatened by people like that. I don't like it, but for the time being, I think we've got no choice but to accept how things are.''

What came to mind was what happened the day before yesterday. The warmth of the three of them who'd celebrated my birthday for me. If they found out about the megasolar thing, even those three would be exposed to danger.

I didn't want that. Not ever.

Just like Hijiri had kept Kannabi at a distance, I didn't want to drag my friends into this, either.

If that was what it took, then it was unavoidable for me to carry a curse---one that would keep me hidden with the secret in the shade of my chest.

``Yeah.''

After saying that, Hijiri walked toward me. That sharp gaze locked on me.

``Izuta, will you promise me? The two of us who share this secret might, from here on, end up stepping into a mire with neither light nor darkness. But someday, without fail---absolutely---we'll wipe out ugly evil, roots and all. That massive evil we couldn't bring down this time.''

Even after yielding to the dark, his grandiose phrasing was still business as usual. I gave a wry smile and nodded.

``Of course. I'm with you.''

When Hijiri solemnly offered his hand for a handshake, I took it without hesitation.

After seeing Hijiri off at Tsunanagai Station, Mikage and I walked together for a while.

Maybe she was thirsty---Mikage bought a plastic bottle of spring water at the station convenience store and drank about two-thirds of it. She held out the rest with a ``Want some?'' but I politely declined.

``Hey, Delta-kun,'' Mikage began slowly.

``If there was a cup of precious wine two thousand years ago, and you assume its water molecules diffused evenly across the Earth, about how many of them do you think are in this plastic bottle?''

``So it's the Cleopatra's wine kind of story.''

``You know it.''

``It's a story people often use to explain how small molecules can be.''

In the bottle Mikage was holding, there was about a third of the water left.

``To keep it simple, can we assume that water is 180 grams, and that the water in the cup of wine Cleopatra drank two thousand years ago was the same amount?''

``Yeah. Of course. As long as the order of magnitude matches.''

I thought for a moment.

``180 grams of water is about 10 moles. In other words, it's made up of roughly 6×10\textsuperscript{24} molecules.''

``The water on Earth is about 1.4 billion cubic kilometers---so let's assume that's 1.4×10\textsuperscript{24} grams.''

``Then if we do a rough calculation, we divide 1.4 by 18 and multiply by 6---''

``Rounding, 0.5 is fine.''

Mikage immediately helped with the math. I appreciated that.

``Then the number of water molecules on Earth is roughly 5×10\textsuperscript{46}.''

Once you got that far, the rest was easy.

``If we assume that over two thousand years the water molecules on Earth got thoroughly mixed, then the probability that a given water molecule was in Cleopatra's wine is 1.2×10\textsuperscript{-22}. The expected value is just that times the number of molecules in the bottle, so\dots''

I did the arithmetic in my head.

``To one significant digit, 7×10\textsuperscript{2}---so 700. Seven hundred water molecules that were in Cleopatra's wine are in that bottle.''

``Correct.''

Mikage said, pleased.

It felt like an unexpected result, but if you accepted the absurd assumptions, the calculation was right. It showed just how many molecules made up the substances you could see.

At the rough scale of things, the ratio between a single water molecule and a cup of water matched the ratio between a cup of water and all the water on Earth. But---

``Why bring that up all of a sudden?''

When I asked, Mikage stared at the plastic bottle as if it were something precious.

``No matter what you eat, no matter how much your body gets replaced with something different, there will always be something left of what you were made of originally---something like that.''

After Mikage-senpai gave her explanation and apology at the schoolwide assembly, the Tengu Festival Incident quickly became the talk of the school.

It was a sensational incident that led to a shocking outcome: starting next year, the alumni association donations that supported our rich school life would be drastically reduced. Naturally, the hunt for the culprit began.

Mikage-senpai must have known---several students from the science department had seen Hijiri and me heading deeper into the shrine grounds on the night in question. Of course, a small suspicion turned our way. But since Mikage-senpai and the vice-principal explicitly forbade any culprit-hunting, it remained at the level of vague rumors being whispered around. Before long, the busyness of the new school year swallowed it, and those rumors faded away too.

Maybe this was the ideal outcome.

The looming demand to paint us as villains, and the student council's intention not to pin responsibility on us, ended up balancing in a delicately political equilibrium.

Kannabi seemed to grasp the situation in a vague way, but maybe because she understood Hijiri's feelings, she never brought it out into the open. As for Mizasaki, he didn't even seem willing to consider that we'd do something as absurd as break the rules in the first place.

Iwama asked me to walk home with her on our first club day back after the all-school assembly.

``I wonder what that whole `Tengu toppling' thing really was, in the end.''

While we talked about the fun parts of the camp, the conversation ended up there. From the subtle change in Iwama's voice, I could tell this was what she'd really wanted to talk about.

``Shimomura-senpai was trying to protect the flying-squirrel forest, right? The no-entry forest at Nagatsuna Shrine. But now there's solar power there. They cut down the trees and installed solar panels.''

I got what she meant. In Shimomura-senpai's fictional investigation from fifty years ago, the survey team that entered the forbidden land witnessed the tengu toppling---the phenomenon of a giant tree suddenly falling.

What had Shimomura-senpai been trying to leave inside that mystery we could no longer even infer, that unsolvable exam question?

``Were the developers the tengu that knocked down the trees?''

``I think Shimomura-senpai wanted his underclassmen to think about something like that.''

I hesitated over what to say back, and in the end, I just shut my mouth.

``The title of that report was `On the True Identity of the Tengu,' right? Almost everything in those accounts could be explained by flying squirrels. But tengu toppling was different. I\dots\ looked into the company that installed the solar power.''

\dots Ah.


She couldn't. She couldn't come any further than this---into the dark. \textit{Not Iwama.}

And yet, I understood too. That was the kind of person Iwama was. Just like me---someone who couldn't stand not knowing the truth. No matter how terrifying the truth was, she wasn't someone who could be happy with lies and things left hidden.

\textit{I didn't want to keep things from Iwama, either. There was no way I did.}

But the truth could hurt people. Sometimes it became a weapon in itself; other times it made you a target for those who didn't like it. \textit{I wanted Iwama to be able to laugh. I didn't want her to lose the happiness of that night's investigation, when ``This is fun'' had slipped out of her without thinking.}

``\dots Just kidding.''

Iwama said it like she was clowning around.

``Maybe I'm overthinking it. Just because two points are close together doesn't mean you should connect them right away---that's the same as making up a new constellation. It's not scientific at all, is it?''

As Iwama forced a smile, like she was trying to convince herself, all I could do was look at her, feeling like I was being torn apart. After we'd walked a little while, all I managed to blurt out was,

``Someday, I'd like to solve the mystery of tengu toppling too.''

``That's right. Properly---scientifically!''

Her innocent smile, without even a hint of suspicion toward me, was too dazzling.

At some point the sun had disappeared, but the moon still wasn't in sight.


% ==============================
%        Epilogue
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Epilogue\\
    Evaporation
\end{center}
\label{sec:epilogueV2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Epilogue}
\vspace{1cm}

Seeing Hijiri step up onto the gym stage, the entire student body---including me---dropped their jaws.

He'd taken off his glasses and covered both eyes with a pure white cloth. It brought to mind Themis, the goddess of justice.

Amid the murmur of voices, Hijiri walked slowly and carefully to the podium. With practiced hands, he adjusted the microphone to the height of his mouth. Just moments ago, a rival candidate had been giving a speech there.

``Everyone---did you bring Yamakawa's Japanese History and World History?''

That was his very first line. Hijiri had announced in advance that all students should bring their Japanese History and World History textbooks. Then Hijiri had the third-year election committee member acting as moderator roll a die, and randomly invited three people up onto the stage. The three of them would each take a position to the right, left, and behind, serving as monitors watching Hijiri.

``I'm well aware of the rumors, of course. And now, I intend to refute them.''

The murmur died down while the monitors were being chosen, and the gym was wrapped in silence.

``Japanese History or World History---I don't care which, and I don't care what page. Give me a quiz on what's written there. I'll answer everything---accurately.''

The blindfold was probably meant to emphasize he couldn't see anything. A performance to flatly deny the accusation that he'd cheated with an anonymous letter. But surely Hijiri had also factored in the visual impact.

He took questions from the entire student body by a show of hands.

``After the February Revolution, the last king of the Kingdom of Sardinia who achieved Italian unification---''

``Vittorio Emanuele II. His name appears on page 229, seven lines up from the bottom.''

``In the Heian period, Japan's \=Oj\=o Goku---''

``Yoshishige no Yasutane. Page 67, six lines down from the top.''

``The height of the colossal stone heads discovered at Olmec religious sites---''

``2.85 meters. It should say so in the image caption, upper left of page 36.''

Hijiri replied calmly, instantly, to quizzes fired at him from all over the place.

For some of us---including me---it wasn't all that surprising, but seeing a second-year answer fluently, precisely, without the slightest hesitation made the air in the gym seem to shimmer again and again.

``Then---who's the printer of the Japanese History textbook?''

``That voice is Aizawa-kun from Class 2-C, isn't it. Then it should be the same as mine. Kyowa Offset Printing Co., Ltd. If you replaced yours it might be different, but of course it's written in the colophon.''

Even with a question pulled from a corner of the textbook no one read, Hijiri didn't get it wrong.

``If you're still not convinced and you want to test me one-on-one, I'll take you on anytime.''

After answering about twenty people's questions at a brisk pace, he sent the monitors back to their original places, took off the blindfold, and put his glasses back on.

``Do you understand now? I have never cheated. I don't make mistakes on memorization-style tests because I've memorized everything.''

No one could deny it anymore.

Tsunanagai High School had monsters living in it.

Who started calling the out-of-prefecture group and the exception group together the ``inhuman group''? In every year, there were at least two monsters with uncanny talent. Existences close to superhuman abilities---barely within what modern science can't deny.

Making that public in this way had no precedent.

``Next, there's one more rumor I'd like to clearly deny.''

Hijiri moved to the next topic with the casualness of someone announcing an exam schedule. I'd assumed he would explain it himself, but instead he sent some kind of signal toward the wings. A low mechanical sound rang out, and when a screen descended behind Hijiri, I broke out in cold sweat.

\textit{No way---was he going to show that?}

``The truth is, I wanted the person in question to explain themselves, but I'm told they're absent today.''

An image appeared on the screen. The culprit who'd spread the anonymous letter sat meekly on a sofa, shoulders hunched, looking toward the camera. It was high-definition enough that you could even make out the sheen of greasy sweat.

It was unmistakably the video I'd filmed.

The morning after the anonymous letters were scattered, I identified the culprit and confronted him directly---alone.

It hadn't been that difficult a task. By the time students arrived at school in the morning, the anonymous letters were already plastered everywhere, so there was no choice but to assume they'd been put up during the night. But students were made to go home without exception by the mandatory full-clearance time. The first suspects had to be teachers or security guards.

I called in a teacher who knew a great deal about the Tengu Festival Incident.

``Excuse me.''

``Ah---go ahead.''

In the reception room, Vice-principal Nihitsu offered me a seat on the sofa. His face was tired as usual.

``What do you need?''

When I sat down and set my tripod and camera bag beside me, the vice-principal also dropped heavily into the seat across from me.

``I wanted to talk a little about the past.''

``Hm?''

``About what happened a little over a year ago---during Golden Week. Thanks to you agreeing to be our supervisor, we were able to go on that camp, weren't we?''

``Ah, well, yes. That's how it was.''

``Then why did you lie back then and say you just happened to already have the inn booked from before?''

``A lie? What are you talking about, all of a sudden?''

Rubbing his bald head, the vice-principal looked at me, troubled.

``When I thought about it calmly, I realized something didn't add up. At Senzakusou, you were staying in the same annex as us, weren't you?''

He didn't answer. \textit{I hadn't directly seen him coming and going, but the fact that he didn't deny it meant I could assume it was true. If there was even a chance we'd seen him, the vice-principal couldn't afford a careless lie here.}

What tipped me off was something the vice-principal had said: that on the same night as us, he'd heard an animal's footsteps echoing from the ceiling. The main building of the inn was concrete---there was no space for an animal to get into the ceiling. On the other hand, the annex we'd stayed in was wooden. Animals like flying squirrels could get into the ceiling and make loud footsteps, and we'd actually heard it.

The vice-principal had heard the same thing. In the room next to ours.

``That annex only has two rooms. Which means we used one, and you used the other. And then it gets weird.''

``Huh.''

The vice-principal still looked like he didn't understand what I meant.

``When we booked Senzakusou, it was full, but then a group canceled and we secured two rooms. But when we got there, they told us there was only one room available due to a mistake.''

``Like the inn told you, it was an unfortunate mistake.''

``If two rooms opened up due to a group cancellation, and one of them was an annex room, wouldn't the other also be an annex room? Didn't you force your way in after we'd already made our reservation?''

``What's gotten into you? That's not like you---making baseless accusations against a teacher.''

``Besides, isn't it a strained excuse to say you just happened to have the same dates booked as our camp? Even if we concede it was a coincidence, why would you decide to travel on the same schedule to an inn where it's obvious the Physics Club and Chemistry Club will be holding camps?''

The vice-principal's face flushed all the way up his bald head. His leather shoes rattled \textit{katakata} as he bounced his leg.

``Izuta-san, that's incredibly rude. If you're saying I lied, then what do you think I was trying to do?''

``That's what I want to ask.''

Our words kept getting sharper.

``When the Biology Club camp you were trying to crush nearly ended up being two nights because we resisted, why did you pretend it was a coincidence and volunteer to be our supervisor?''

``Trying to crush it? For what reason?''

``Because of what we were going to do. We were trying to investigate the northern forest of Nagatsuna Shrine.''

``And how exactly would I have any way of knowing that?''

``The copier. That thing keeps a copy history---right down to the content. If you're a vice-principal on the management side, checking a student's logs would be easy. You could easily learn what our Biology Club investigation was about. And you needed to keep an eye on us so we wouldn't step into a taboo.''

``I have absolutely no idea what you're talking about.''

``You were in contact with the CHOSEI Group. That's why you tried to crush it when the Biology Club camp was revived. But you couldn't. So you pressured Senzakusou to secure a room, became our supervisor, and decided to keep us under surveillance.''

The vice-principal crossed his legs beneath his round belly and glared at me.

``That's pure delusion. In the first place, with my authority, stopping a club camp would be easy. And if I really could pressure the inn, then I should have had them cancel your reservation. Try examining your claims a little more objectively.''

``At that point, you didn't need to go that far. Like you always said yourself, one of the vice-principal's big jobs is to soft-land trouble. You worked hard to make sure nothing got too sharp for either the students or outsiders.''

Trying to stop us by saying Shimomura-senpai's leftover materials were fabricated was probably part of that.

I could more than imagine the effort. I couldn't say I felt no sympathy.

``But in this case, you let the edges get too sharp.''

I pulled out one of the anonymous letters from the shade of my chest and set it on the table.

``Let's start simply with physical evidence. I had the Chemistry Club analyze the paper and ink used for this anonymous letter. They even used a scanning electron microscope---SEM---and a thermogravimetric differential thermal analyzer, TG-DTA. As a result, it was a perfect match for the recycled paper and copier toner used at Tsunanagai High School.''

``So what. It's natural to say the culprit was someone in the school.''

``No. I just explained it. This school's copier keeps a history. If you print on campus, it's easy to be traced. Of course, unless you're in a position where you can avoid having the history investigated---or erase it.''

I sprang up and pulled a small spray bottle from my pants pocket. Inside was an aqueous sodium hydroxide solution adjusted to pH 13.

``Izuta-san! What do you think you're doing?!''

``Did you think it had some kind of truth serum in it?''

I moved around the table and sprayed it at the vice-principal's feet as he panicked and took a stance to evade my bizarre behavior. It was a strong base---if it got in your eyes, there was a risk of blindness. It worked out that he jerked aside. I sprayed the floor from almost zero distance, then immediately pulled back.

The change appeared at once.

On the white linoleum, tiny reddish-purple blotches began to bloom in scattered dots. They were concentrated where the vice-principal had been standing.

``What did you do?!''

``I sprayed a sodium hydroxide solution. Do you see the reddish-purple color on the floor?''

The vice-principal still didn't seem to understand what was happening.

``It's phenolphthalein. It's colorless when neutral, but it reacts to bases and turns a vivid reddish purple like this. If it gets on something at high concentration, it doesn't come off easily. It's hydrophobic, so even washing with water won't remove it very well. Unlucky for you.''

The vice-principal was wearing indoor leather shoes. Phenolphthalein was on his soles.

``You posted that document in front of the chemistry lab, didn't you? Actually, the evening before those anonymous letters were found, there was an accident near the second-floor chemistry lab where a large amount of phenolphthalein was spilled.''

I didn't know what kind of stupid experiment the Chemistry Club had been trying to do, but that was the decisive blow.

``I heard it was right up against the dismissal time, so they couldn't finish cleaning it that same day. It's transparent, and it must've been hard to tell where any residue was left. You walked through without knowing, and you neatly left an easy-to-read marker on the soles of your shoes.''

I showed him several photos on my phone. They were from when we'd carefully sprayed sodium hydroxide solution on the floors where the anonymous letters had been posted, including in front of the chemistry lab. At multiple points, we'd found clear reddish-purple shoeprints, their toes pointed toward the anonymous letters.

They were left by the culprit who'd passed in front of the chemistry lab in the school building after the students had gone home.

``If you truly don't know anything, then show me the soles of your shoes. We'll check the shoeprints.''

``I just happened to step in it later.''

``Then that's fine. They won't match the shoeprints we found at seven of the fifteen locations where the anonymous letters were posted.''

After the video played, the gym filled with unsettled murmurs.

Ironically, the 4K footage---shot on the same $\alpha$7 that had filmed the flying squirrels---captured the vice-principal's confession in crisp detail. To avoid anyone claiming it was a deepfake, we'd taken every precaution and used high-quality video that would be hard to dismiss as fabricated.

In it, the vice-principal admitted that he'd scattered those anonymous letters, and stated his motive: out of personal resentment, he'd wanted Hijiri to lose the election. And he emphasized that all three contents were concocted from student rumors he'd heard, and were utterly baseless.

But the problem was that making this public had never been part of the plan.
Once the vice-principal became the top suspect, we changed our plan to make the culprit apologize.

We wanted to get some control over the vice-principal---the one who'd been acting as a go-between while doing spy and near double-agent work between Tsunanagai High School and the CHOSEI Group. So we filmed this video as a half-threat and got leverage on him. Today, the vice-principal was supposed to accept our demands and give a false report here.

That we'd identified the student culprit who scattered the anonymous letters, that it had already been settled privately, and that the name wouldn't be revealed---

But today, the vice-principal didn't show up. An unexcused absence, apparently.

Hijiri had said this morning, ``I'll handle it,'' when he learned that---but I never imagined he'd make the secret video we'd shot for leverage public.

It was practically a social execution.

Hijiri wrapped it up as if he had nothing more to say about the video.

``Now then---just to be safe, one last thing. Kannabi-san is a wonderful person and a very important friend, but of course I've never had a girlfriend. In all my life, studying has been my only sweetheart.''

The situation was so extreme it didn't even earn a bitter laugh.

And with that, the all-school assembly held right before the election came to a close.

After the assembly, I headed for the wings to talk to Hijiri, but someone was already there.

Kannabi. Seeing the two of them face-to-face, I reflexively hid behind the curtain. There was no need to hide, and maybe it would've been better not to---but seeing them alone together, I thought I shouldn't interrupt. Eavesdropping was wrong. I covered my ears.

Not long after Kannabi left, I joined Hijiri as he put away the PC backstage.

``Good work.''

I'd decided on those first words back when I was behind the curtain. No matter what complaints I had, I'd start by acknowledging him.

``Yeah. Thanks.''

With a soft snap, Hijiri closed the PC and picked up the two textbooks beside it---one red, one blue. Maybe he misread the way I looked at them, because he said,

``It took a lot of work. I'm human too, you know---so even if I don't remember things wrong, I can still get distracted, and there's a one- or two-percent gap. To get rid of that, I reread them five times. Because of that, I'm sleep-deprived today.''

All I could do was give a wry smile. Either way, it wasn't something a human should be capable of.

At Hijiri's suggestion, we stepped out into the passageway beside the gym. Outdoors, with a clear view, and less worry about being overheard.

``\dots Was this really the right call?''

As if he'd sensed what I was feeling, Hijiri answered gently.

``There was no other way. With the vice-principal missing, anything we said about the Tengu Festival Incident would just look like me desperately making excuses. I needed to leave the impression that I'm completely innocent.''

I silently looked down at my own hands. I was the one who'd pressed REC. My hands were the ones that passed judgment on the vice-principal.

``Izuta---it's not you.''

Hijiri spoke in a serious voice, like he could see right through me, and tapped my shoulder.

``You feel guilty, don't you. It's true---you were the one who pressed the shutter, Izuta. But I'm the one who pulled the trigger. I'll take responsibility for everything. Izuta, you did great.''

``Yeah\dots''

The vice-principal probably wouldn't be able to come back to school, and he'd likely lose his job as well.

To begin with, the vice-principal was the one at fault for posting documents that slandered students. But that foolish act hadn't been driven by personal resentment. He hadn't said it outright, but it was obvious he'd been pressured by Tsuruo of the CHOSEI Group and had no choice but to carry out an anti-Hijiri campaign.

The student council president was the highest authority in school self-governance, with real power. For Tsuruo, who was trying to rebuild relations with Tsunanagai High School, Hijiri taking that seat was the worst possible outcome.

The election interference happened because it was inevitable.

The vice-principal had been appalled by us over the Tengu Festival Incident, but he wasn't in a position to nurse resentment.

Apparently, he'd decided to dig up that taboo precisely because he thought Hijiri wouldn't be able to refute it so easily. And in turn, that was proof of just how spotless Hijiri had been.

The vice-principal had wanted Hijiri to swallow his bitterness and finish the student council election without ever touching the documents. And by having Hijiri lose, he'd been trying to arrange a soft landing for the situation.

Even if we couldn't give up the election, couldn't we have aimed for a softer landing too?

``At the very end, the vice-principal protected us. When we threatened to reveal the full truth of that incident if he didn't confess, he finally gave in after saying that was absolutely not allowed. He must've wanted to avoid putting us in danger, no matter what.''

``I think that resolve deserves praise. But I still think he should face consequences.''

``Even if it's punishment that ruins his life?''

Hijiri gave a small nod.

``If all he did was write bad things about me\dots\ maybe I could've ignored it, and maybe I could've forgiven him.''

\textit{Then what couldn't he forgive?} I looked at Hijiri's side profile---and regretted it.

In his eyes, a terrifyingly bright light was burning, bright enough to incinerate a person.

``But I just couldn't forgive it. Writing it up like dating Kannabi-san was one of my crimes.''

During the break after we'd seen the ballot boxes off, I was so restless, I headed to the bathroom even though I didn't need to go.

``That was a pretty nice shot, wasn't it?''

As I was about to enter the boys' restroom, Kannabi called out to stop me. \textit{Of course she'd realized I was the one who filmed the vice-principal.} When I nodded and turned, Kannabi followed.

``You sure you're good on the bathroom?''

``The second floor is cleaner. Wanna come along?''

``A tandem pee, huh.''

We stopped in the lounge area in front of the gym. Just the day before yesterday, we'd used this very spot for a secret talk.

``If I had to guess, this time's MVP is Delta.''

When Kannabi said that, I couldn't help letting out a breath tangled up with complicated feelings.

``The match isn't decided yet. And the one who scored was him. Praise him for it.''

``\dots You look like you're not satisfied.''

``Of course. I still can't honestly say, hand on heart, that what I did was definitely the right answer.''

It was probably something I didn't need to say. But in front of Kannabi, it slipped out.
And Kannabi carefully caught those words.

``Whether it was truly the correct answer or not---don't you only find out once everything's over, once there's no taking it back?''

``That's\dots\ I suppose, by definition, that's how it is\dots''

``There's no if in a life where you keep stacking one-time choices. Worrying about whether it was right or wrong is pointless. In the end, it's a question of whether you affirm it or deny it.''

She poured it out in one heated burst, then looked at me with her usual blank expression.

``Thank you. Delta did something I couldn't do. Hold your head a little higher.''

She poked me in the chest. When I did as she said and stuck my chest out, Kannabi's mouth twisted like she was about to smile.

The bell started ringing.

``There's one thing I want to ask.''

Kannabi said it almost like an afterthought as we headed back to the classroom.

``If you have a close friend of the opposite sex, and because of that person's words, your life's big choices are being swayed without you noticing\dots\ do you think that's what people generally call romantic feelings?''

I gave a wry smile. \textit{I'd expected it, but still---she was at that stage.}

``\dots Aren't you asking the wrong person?''

``Probably. I'm the least suitable person. But this is something I can only ask Delta.''

``Speaking as a specialist\dots\ I can't definitively say that's romantic love.''

After saying it pompously, I turned to Kannabi beside me.

``But it's definitely a feeling you should cherish.''

\textit{I never would've believed a day would come when I'd seriously have a conversation like this with Kannabi.}

It was hard to believe---but in that moment, I felt like this was the only right answer I could give.

% ==============================
%        Afterword
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Afterword
\end{center}
\label{sec:afterwordV2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Afterword}
\vspace{1cm}

It's been a while---I'm Takuma Sakasai. Even though I've billed this as the ``Science Department'' series, a full eight months have passed since Volume 1 came out, meaning there's been a long stretch where it's been hard to even use the nice little abbreviation. I'm sorry for keeping you waiting. And to everyone who waited---thank you.

I can't go into details without spoilers, but a fair portion of the fieldwork sections in this volume are based on my own experiences. (Of course, that only applies to the parts that deal with nature; the parts where a boy and a girl are getting along sweetly are purely fictional.)

When you actually go out into nature yourself, there are plenty of things you only notice for the first time on site, and it's not uncommon to make discoveries that aren't written in textbooks or field guides. Even if the underlying principles are the same, the way conditions differ from place to place is part of what makes biology so interesting---and fieldwork also lets you experience, firsthand, that mystery-like thrill of arriving at a truth that only you know.

It's not too late. If this has piqued your interest, I hope you'll go visit some nature close to home.

That's enough behind-the-scenes talk. I'd like to add a few words that are more like an afterword.

Looking back on my younger days, I realize I was thoroughly unsuited to being the ``star.'' I had a ton of things I wanted to do, and more than enough energy to do them---but for some reason, the only thing I ever started of my own accord was sneaking around and writing novels. Even at school events and activities, I never took the lead and pulled everyone along. Instead, I found it enjoyable to throw my full support behind someone else who would pull me along.

Come to think of it, the protagonist of my debut work was a pig who was about as ``supporting cast'' as it gets, and my editor pointed out over and over, ``Can't you give him something closer to a lead role?'' But there was nothing to be done. In that world, the true star was a hero of dazzling flames, and the pig hiding behind his owner's skirt was only chosen to be the protagonist by chance. (If you haven't read Butareba, sorry for making so little sense. As long as you can take away the difference between being the ``star'' and being the protagonist, you'll be fine.)

Delta-kun, the protagonist of this volume, also isn't really suited to being the star. The only time he'd ever have a sash that says ``Today's Star'' draped over him would probably be his birthday. In this one book, I wanted to do my best to write the story of someone who lives in the shadows.

Now then---with people outside the Biology Club starting to get involved, I think it's finally begun to feel less like the ``Biology Club'' series and more like the ``Science Department'' series. As always, the subject matter still leans toward biology, so I hope you'll forgive that---but from here on out, I'd like to work in plenty of chemistry, physics, and math as well.

Thanks to all of you readers, and to everyone involved, it seems I'll be able to keep this series going a while longer.

In closing, please accept my heartfelt thanks.

Until we meet again in Volume 3!

\begin{flushright}
    June 2025 --- Takuma Sakasai
\end{flushright}

% ==============================
%        Animate Bonus
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Animate SS\\
    The Logic of Holding Hands
\end{center}
\label{sec:a}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Animate SS}
\vspace{1cm}

``When it comes to the appeal of mystery novels, it's all about rigorous logic.''

``Logic is so important! Whether it's mystery novels, science, or everyday life!''

Mikage suddenly brought up the topic of mystery novels, and Iwama answered with keen interest. During lunch break, I was eating with Iwama when Mikage joined us, carrying a hand-rolled sushi from the convenience store.

``Leaving science aside for the moment, I never thought everyday life required rigorous logic.''

``Even in ordinary school life, if you think about the logic behind things, you can discover some pretty interesting truths! For example\dots''

Iwama glanced around the classroom, apparently searching for an example, and Mikage spoke up.

``How about this question---right now, in this classroom, is there a spot where, if you stood there, you couldn't hold hands with anyone?''

Mikage turned her head ninety degrees to survey the 1-C classroom, then added,

``Let's assume the classroom is 9m $\times$ 9m, and there are 37 people in it.''

``How interesting! It's just like a geometry puzzle.''

``Don't we need to define the distance required to hold hands first?''

The moment I said it, I realized we were about to put this into practice.

I had no idea how Mikage had managed to produce a measuring tape, but as a result, Iwama and I ended up being the ones who had to hold hands. We deliberately set aside our lunch, stood apart, and stretched out our arms.

Once we had grasped each other's hands at the very limit of our reach, Iwama flashed that ``Did you see that smile?'' smile of hers.

``So this is exactly the distance where you can just barely hold hands!''

What on earth were we doing in the middle of the classroom during lunch break? Embarrassed, I let go of her hand.

``Arm span is roughly equal to height.''

Mikage shook her head; she was measuring the distance between our feet.

``Your center of gravity shifts, so the distance ends up a bit longer. Right now it's just under 2.2m. Let's use that as our standard---any two people within 2.2m of each other can hold hands.''

It felt like we had gathered all the necessary data, but neither Iwama nor I was particularly good at math. We thought about it for a while without grasping the key point.

``Then I'll give you a hint. Try dividing the classroom into a 6 $\times$ 6 grid.''

Mikage began saying something rather puzzling. Iwama drew a diagram on the back of a handout.

``In that case, each square would be a 1.5m $\times$ 1.5m square, making 36 squares in total.''

``36? But there are 37 people in the class. That's one short.''

Mikage ate her hand-rolled sushi and watched us think.

``If you make good use of the grid points, you can distribute the distances evenly\dots\ You understand, don't you, Del-chan?''

I'm not particularly fond of math, but solving problems like this with Iwama is genuinely enjoyable. No, no, of course it's fun with Mizasaki too---it would be strange if it weren't.

``But where did the 1.5m come from?''

The question suddenly struck me. It should be something you could arrive at without complicated calculations.

``It's simply the number that divides 9m into 6 equal parts, giving exactly 36 regions for 37 people. With only 36 regions to place 37 people, at least one region must contain 2 people.''

``I see! That's the pigeonhole principle!''

The pigeonhole principle states that if there are more pigeons than pigeonholes, then at least one hole must contain two or more pigeons.

``Since at least one of the 36 squares must contain two or more people, then---''

Iwama pulled her hand back and had me stand up. At some point, Mikage had already drawn out a 1.5m $\times$ 1.5m grid with the measuring tape. Iwama and I stood at opposite vertices of one square.

``The length of the diagonal is 1.5$\sqrt{2}$m, which is about 2.1m. That's a little smaller than our limit of 2.2m---''

Facing Iwama, who leaned forward with her hand stretched out, I somewhat reluctantly extended my own.

``In this classroom, there is no spot where you couldn't hold hands with any of the 37 people here.''

At a distance of 1.5$\sqrt{2}$m, my hand and Iwama's joined together.

Mikage watched us with gentle eyes and declared, ``Proof complete.''

% ==============================
%        Gamers Bonus
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Gamers SS\\
    Making Effective Use of Poison
\end{center}
\label{sec:g}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Gamers SS}
\vspace{1cm}

``You can't talk about mystery novels without touching on poison.''
``I know, right! I love poisons too!''

Mizasaki and Iwama were deep in such a dangerous conversation, leaving Kannabi and me a little taken aback.
It wasn't a regular club day. The four of us had stayed after school, drifting to a corner of the classroom to while away the time with idle chatter. Mizasaki insisted on treating us to some homemade \textit{dokudami} tea\footnote{Translator's Note: \textit{Houttuynia cordata}, commonly known as fish mint or chameleon plant. In Japanese, the name \textit{dokudami} (ドクダミ) is written with characters that can be interpreted as ``poison-stopping,'' hence the folk belief in its detoxifying properties.} he'd brewed himself, pouring it from a flask into paper cups for us. It wasn't exactly unpleasant, just a bit bitter.

``There's no poison in this \textit{dokudami} tea, right?''
Kannabi eyed the dubious concoction warily, fully aware that it had been prepared by a former Chemistry Club member. I understood how she felt and chimed in.

``It's fine. There's actually a theory that the name comes from the idea that it `stops poison.'\footnote{Translator's Note: The Japanese name \textit{dokudami} is often explained as deriving from \textit{doku} (毒, poison) + \textit{tami} (溜み, accumulation), but folk etymology also links it to \textit{doku o tameru} (毒を矯める), meaning ``to correct poison'' or ``to counteract poison.'' The plant has long been used in traditional medicine for its purported detoxifying and anti-inflammatory effects.} ''

``That name's seriously misleading---so it actually means something closer to `antidote.' Come on, Nabi-chan, give it a try.''
Urged on by Mizasaki, Kannabi grimaced but drank it anyway. Her expression remained unchanged afterwards.

``The world of poisons is profoundly deep. There are countless varieties of natural toxins out there, and then you have things like calcium chloride---swallowing it does nothing, but inject it and it can stop your heart.''
As we sipped our tea, Iwama chatted about poisons with the same casual enthusiasm most people reserved for desserts.

``You're absolutely right, Gamma-san. Unfortunately, we rarely get the chance to try them out for ourselves.''

``Poisoning someone would pretty much be a crime no matter how you did it, wouldn't it...?''
Mizasaki nodded vigorously. The two of them gave off the distinct impression that, were it not for the law, they'd happily start experimenting with toxins on a whim.

``There must be poisons that aren't illegal, though. By definition, a poison is simply any substance that exerts a harmful effect on living organisms. The capsaicin in chili peppers, for instance, qualifies as a neurotoxin in that sense.''

``So serving you guys insanely spicy food would technically count as poisoning you.''

``But since this is such a rare opportunity, I'd really love to use one in a more \textit{mystery-novel} kind of way. Like a true chemistry expert would.''
Just as I was wondering what exactly constituted a ``mystery-novel kind of way,'' Mikage quietly appeared beside us.

``You're having quite the interesting conversation. If you don't mind, why don't we all try this? Though I did receive it from someone else myself.''
Mikage produced a box of Pocky. She pulled out three sticks and ate them without hesitation. Still chewing, she shot Mizasaki a deeply meaningful look and then offered the box to the rest of us.

Having just been talking about poison, the sudden arrival of snacks made me uneasy. But Mizasaki took one first, so the three of us each took a stick as well.

The moment I put it in my mouth, however, a powerful sense of wrongness hit me. Wait---this was---

Iwama and Kannabi looked equally unsettled. It was obvious none of us were experiencing what you'd call normal Pocky.

The Pocky had almost no flavor at all. Yet Mizasaki, and only Mizasaki, was eating his with visible enjoyment.

``Hey, Mizasaki. You've poisoned us.''
Mizasaki grinned smugly and kept munching his Pocky. To stop his little trick from alarming Iwama and Kannabi, I immediately pointed at his flask and said,

``You put \textit{gymnema} in it. I did think it tasted a little bitter.''
Normally, \textit{dokudami} tea has a faint sweetness that makes it quite easy to drink. That's exactly why I'd found it odd.

``As expected of Delta. You saw right through me.''
Mizasaki spoke cheerfully. The paper cup of tea at his elbow was still completely full.

``\textit{Gymnema}? He put poison in the tea?''

``I've heard of that! It's a tea that makes you unable to taste sweetness.''
Kannabi and Iwama didn't seem particularly upset. Just to be safe, I continued my explanation.

``It's \textit{Gymnema sylvestre}\footnote{Translator's Note: \textit{Gymnema sylvestre} is a tropical plant in the Apocynaceae family, native to India and Southeast Asia. Its leaves contain gymnemic acids, which temporarily bind to sweet taste receptors on the tongue, blocking the perception of sweetness. The effect typically wears off within ten to fifteen minutes.}, a tropical plant from the dogbane family. The gymnemic acids in it bind to sweet-taste receptors and block their function.''

``The effect wears off in about ten minutes, so don't worry. But until then, I'll be happily finishing all of these off, because Mikage's Pocky belongs to me now.''

``...Sorry, Mizasaki-kun. I also got these from someone else, so I think one stick per person is fair.''
Mikage spoke with evident amusement and, pulling out the remaining sticks from the bag, popped them all into her mouth at once.

``Aaagh! The Pocky I should've seized before the poisoning...!''

I noticed a sticky note affixed to the back of Mikage's Pocky box. It read: ``For Mikage-san: Since there were leftovers, please share them with everyone. —A classmate.''

The Pocky appearing right after we finished the tea was suspicious enough on its own.

That handwriting unmistakably belonged to Mizasaki.

% ==============================
%        Common Bonus
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Bonus Chapter\\
    A Small Explosion
\end{center}
\label{sec:b}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Bonus Chapter}
\vspace{1cm}

``When it comes to mystery novels, you've got to have an explosion.''

``Why are you suddenly talking about the movie version?''

Kannabi, who was eating what was either ramune candy or something similar, had just said something rather alarming, and I casually brushed it off. Iwama and Mizasaki hadn't arrived at the biology room yet, and the two of us found ourselves sitting across a table from each other, killing time.

``It doesn't have to be on the scale of a feature film. If you're using it to kill someone, sure, but for destroying evidence or creating a diversion, even a small-scale explosion has plenty of uses.''

``Now that you mention it, I suppose that's true. And bombs aren't the only things that explode, anyway.''

I added that remark. Kannabi tilted her head slightly.

``Depending on your definition, an explosion doesn't necessarily mean detonating explosives. A gas leak can cause an explosion. A balloon popping is an explosion. Even an egg in a microwave can explode.''

``An egg can explode in a microwave?''

``The water inside it boils. The steam causes the explosion.''

``Huh. I had no idea.''

I felt as though I'd inadvertently saved Kannabi's microwave at home.

``Come to think of it, Delta, you used to be in the Chemistry Club, didn't you? Does that mean you could make explosives, too?''

I wasn't sure how to respond to Kannabi's sudden question.

``Well, how should I put it\dots\ if you have the necessary knowledge and materials, making explosives isn't exactly difficult.''

``Have you ever made them?''

``\dots If I were to answer that I'd actually done it, wouldn't that basically be confessing to something that dances along the gray edge of the law? For safety's sake, I'm afraid I can only say NO.''

``Heh heh. You've made them.''

``Like I said, I'm not saying YES.''

I had no idea what she was thinking, but she brought the slim, cylindrical container in her hand over to my side, popped the lid off with a \textit{pop}, and tipped the last two remaining ramune candies into my palm.

``What's this supposed to mean?''

``It's Kabaya's JuiC. A little harder than regular ramune candy. I like it.''

I hadn't exactly been asking for the product name, but I gratefully picked up one of the tablet-shaped candies and put it in my mouth. Grape flavor. Just as Kannabi had said, it was quite hard.

``So. Have you made them?''

So that's what this was about---she thought giving me two candies would make me talk. I pulled out a tissue, laid it on the table, and set the remaining candy on top of it to keep it from getting damp. I rolled the other piece---the negotiation material---around my mouth for a moment and thought it over.

``\dots If you asked Mizasaki, he might just tell you.''

I'd practically spelled it out already, but Kannabi still wanted me to admit it myself. She stood up, her face full of bewilderment. I had no idea what she was doing, but she spent a considerable amount of time washing the candy container, then, having carefully finished the task, dropped the container into the trash and came back.

``Why bother washing it if you're just going to throw it away?''

``It's nothing. Believe it or not, I'm quite particular about cleanliness.''

``I'm not surprised you're particular about cleanliness, but is there any point in washing something you're throwing away?''

``You'll probably understand in a moment.''

I was still turning over the meaning of her words when, suddenly, there was a loud \textit{Bang}. It didn't come from anywhere near the table where we were sitting. It seemed to be some distance away.

Seeing me scramble to find the source of the noise, Kannabi gave a soft, knowing smile.

``I made a time bomb. So? As a former Chemistry Club member, you should be able to figure out what method I used.''

If she was challenging me, I had no choice but to think. The most recent suspicious behavior was Kannabi spending all that time washing the container, then throwing it away. Looking at how she'd washed it---the biology room had a wide array of detergents, since it dealt with all sorts of stains. At last, an idea came to me.

``Baking soda and citric acid. You mixed the two powders inside the container, added a little water, screwed the cap on, and pretended to be washing it. The reaction inside produced carbon dioxide, and in the trash can, the cap blew off.''

``Correct. Explosions really are good for creating a diversion.''

Kannabi spoke with satisfaction and popped a candy into her mouth.

It was the very last one---the one that should have been in my hand.

\end{document}
